THE RED PAGE.
Comparing current impressions with those of
some five years ago, we seem to be in the hollow
of the local literary ripple—“trough” and
“wave” are too ironical. Five years ago half-a-
dozen of our little bards were sweet and vocal
together, doing what is destined apparently to
remain their best work : there was a remarkable
stirring from 1895 to 1900. To-day Jew things
that are worth reprinting seem to get themselves
written or printed : the age poetic is at pause. As
to prose: the' youngsters rarely show brains or
assiduity for great work ; and maybe this is merely
the autumn of our discontent, with winter to
come. The Universities? Well, the yarn
goes that Sydney woke up and rubbed
its eyes not long ago, and demanded
that its children should recite in praise a long
119th psalm of the University’s literary achieve-
ments. The response came in a loud voice and
very truly, “ Please, Mister Senate, there aren’t
no littery ’chievements ! ” So one turns an en-
quiring eye towards The Sydney Mail competition,
in the hope that all that ink will leave a sizeable
mark somewhere.
But the local author is not precisely snoring.
“ Danton ” —“ D. Fenton”—has recently come to
light in Dawnward? (No. VI. of “ The Bulletin
Booklets ” —a limited edition, exhausted by sub-
scribers) and admits the individuality of Ber-
nard Patrick O’Dowd, assistant librarian of the
Supreme Court, Melbourne. The familiar com-
mentator says that O’Dowd was born at Beau-
fort, Victoria, in 1866—50 that he is a
measurably young (or aged) verse-writer. His
parents are Irish. His early education was gained
in Victorian State schools chiefly, and when he
was about nine years of age the Yic. Education
Act permitted him to receive a certificate as
“sufficiently educated ”(!) He matriculated in
Melbourne University at fourteen ; passed his
first year exam, for B.A. before seventeen; and
then, his father dying, was forced to quit classes
for awhile. In 1888 he resumed ; took his degree
in Arts in 1889, honours in Philosophy in ’9l, and
was admitted to the Victorian Bar. So that here
is one useful writer that the Universities may
claim.
J*
“Danton’s” verse is well-known to readers of
The Bulletin. At best it is uncommonly stimu-
lating. His muse is a half-sister of Emerson’s,
devoted heart and mind to the service of the
democratic spirit. His work is sometimes “tight,”
in the painter’s phrase, but full of leashed force,
and often moving nobly. The plea of ‘'Prole-
taria,” for example, could be printed in any high
company—
Our polyandrous dam has borne
To Satan ami to God
The hordes of Night, the clans of Morn,
That through our valleys p10d...
Tho’ blind and dull, 't is we supply
The Painter’s dazzling dreams ;
The rolling flood of Poetry
From Our dumb Chaos streams
Nay, when your world is over-tired
And genius comatose,
Our race, by Nemesis inspired,
Old Order overthrows:
With earthquake-life we thrill your land.
Refill the cruse of Art,
Revitalise spent Wisdom, and —
Resume our weary part ..,
We are the gnomes of Titan works
Whose throbbings never cease;
Our unregarded signet lurks
On every masterpiece....
And our reward ? In this wan land,
In clientage of Greed,
Despised, polluted, maimed and banned.
To xuander and—to breed.
J*
And if leader-writers read verse, this on
“Compromise,” by Bernard Patrick, might be
quoted every week to Bernard Ringrose—
I pencil glaring wings of Right
With Wrong’s sedater black ;
And rushing Freedom’s crotchets with
Remrgent minims slack.
1 paralyse the hand of God
When He would loose at last
The gales of vengeance on the ripe
Enormities of caste . ..
The tiini id clarity of Truth
I phosphoresce, with lies,
And put sophistic hectics on
The pallor of the Wise.
Altogether, one is justified in considering
Dawnward? as a memorable and enduring
contribution to the literature of Australia and of
social effort. O’Dowd’s bronze does not always
run freely ; but when it does he moulds a statue.
Ernest Sando Emerson (“ Milky White”), who
publishes A Shanty Entertainment (printed
by Ford & Taylor, Melbourne ; Is.), is another
Victorian, dating froni#Ballarat, 1870. His little
book, adopting a favourite device, shows u% half-
a-dozen typical Bush characters gathered in a
public-house to wait for a delayed coach. On a
slender skein of narrative, pleasantly diversified,
the author links various verses contributed to
The Bulletin and other papers. Emerson has bad
wide experience as drover, miner, and journalist,
and bis characters are no literary puppets, but
real denizens of the backbloeks. Often his ballad
verses, as “The Lonely Track,”just miss being
good : he has the right emotion, and enough of
it, but wants skill of form. The sincerity and
pictorial truth of A Shanty Entertainment should
win Bush readers.
For some books past one has felt that Have-
lock Ellis was losing mental grip of his
ideas : that his thought-vibrations were grow-
ing feebler. This does not •\mean that his
knowledge is less : it is greater ; but that his
force for dealing with his knowledge is less. And
how does one feel that ? Chiefly in his style. It
is within the knowledge of every writer that style
reflects the mind, and even the mind at the
moment. You can survey your material, arrange
your ideas ; but if you sit down in a cold mood
little that counts is done. Force yourself to
write, as Zola or Trollope did; and you get
Zola sor Trollope’s result—in the main negli-
geable. To be sure, if the bar is merely
indolence, by dint of striving you can heat
your brain, and end by doing worthy work. Butif
the bar is nervous weakness, want of cerebral
vehemence, you can do nothing at that hour but
something that will serve-to be rewritten later, in
a hotter mood. Then, in that hotter mood,'how
cumbrous the colder phrases seem ! How your
pen runs through them—shortening long sen-
tences, eliminating useless words, lopping off
sprawling limbs of verbiage ! There is a clear
connection between rhythm of brain and rhythm
of style : one depends absolutely on the-other.
‘
Of course, words themselves have a rhythm, singly or
in collocation, and pace fast or slowly according to the
contained eye-impression, ear-impression, arid mind-
impression. And, despite the age's heirloom of rhetori-
cal devices, like Pope’s familiar
The line too labours, and the words move slow
(a typical slow line ; or)
Not so, when swift Camilla scours the plain—
(with the context, a fairly fast line), I do not know
a thoroughly satisfactory essay on the speed of
words. T) he hissing sis usually thought to retard a line ;
but evidently long vowels have a greater share in lessen-
ing speed. Some time ago, discussing a line of Ogilvie’s
Slow bullocks snailing by—
it was pointed out that the use of iambic metre made the
bullocks trot as if the drover was hurrying them up—and
this in spite of the snailing. Put the line into trochees—
While the bullocks slowly snailing—
and the mob pauses at once.
A.W. J. wrote on the point
I take it to be a truism that the long vowels and diph-
thongs—o, a (ah), » (at), 00, &c.—are a drag on any line
they occur in, and that most combinations of two con-
sonants are the same : cf. what you call the “ typical slow
line,”
The line too labours, and the words move slow,
where you have i, nt, 00, ndth, dsm, 00, vsl, 6— all long
utterances, and labours is the quickest word of the lot.
What you call a “fast line” teems to me neither
typical nor fast: nor is its successor, till the last few
words
skims along the main.
But take two of Tennyson’s:
(a) Snapt in the rushing of the river rain
(b) Tall, as the crest of some slow-arching wave
and the drag of the latter is seen to depend on the two
long vowels and the huddle of consonants of the last five
syllables,—perhaps also on the drawling v of the last
word.
So far, things seem evident, and hardly worth laying
stress on : and beyond this I can see only one thing
clearly—that the liquids and semivowels give one a
chance of slowing a line if one feels so inclined. L and s
more especially will do just what you want them to, in
this respect: if you wish to drag out a line, you have
And ice that seals the white sea's Ups:
if you are in a hurry, you can quote
His soul is at one with the reason of things that is sap to
the roots.
The only time when liquids (including s, of course)
seem to have a preference for the slower pronunciation
is when they precede mutes: lisp is a distinctly longer
word than lips, and I fancy elder is longer than meddler ,
though Id is a very easy combination. Bullocks, again,
is a much shorter word than mollusc. You quote
Ogilvie’s line about .“slow bullocks,” hut it goes in a
canter compared to
The staid mollusc snailing by.
On the other hand,
Aslant the billows sailing by
has as much hiss about it as Ogilvie’s line, and yet is
very much quicker. But then Ogilvie has used another
device to make his drag—-the long 6 in a syllable that
should not be accented (according to the metre): in my
line slant is as long a word as slow in his, but slant conies
where you expect it in iambic metre, and slow comes
where you do not.
. J*
Both in prose and verse there is a subjective
rhythm independent of the intrinsic speed of the
words. Illustrate with some first sentences of
Havelock Ellis’s prefaces, to show his mental
attack—
This little book is an attempt to present to the English
reader a critical summary of the results of the science
now commonly called criminal anthropology.— The
Criminal, 1890.
There are at least two ways of looking at books and at
the personalities books express.— Ajflrmations, 1898.
For many years material has been growing under my
hands bearing on the psychological and anthropological
characters of genius, and from time to time I have
examined these data and reached certain, more or less
secure, conclusions.—J Study of British Genius, 1904.
Notice the drag in the third example as com-
pared with the directness of the other two : a
drag that is more obvious in context. When you
find a whole book dragging in that fashion, it is
evidence (other things equal) of deficient physical
energy, of feeble brain-pulse. There is doubt-
fully an exception to the rule. It was because
Macaulay had such intense cerebral energy that
his sentences flew like bullets. When he walked
the streets he attracted’ general attention : his
face worked, his lips muttered, his eye 3 flashed,
he flourished an umbrella with which he thumped
the pavement. “ People gazed curiously at him,
-and stopped to stare when he had passed.” And
when he wrote, his style was a series of ebulli-
tions.
Whafc is spirit ? What are our own minds, the portion
of spirit with which we are best acquainted? We ob-
serve certain phenomena. We cannot explain them into
material causes. We therefore infer that there exists
something- which is not material. But of this something
we have no idea. We can define it only by negatives.
We can'reason about it only by symbols.- Essay on
Milton.
And so on, in direct statement after statement.
Compare the style of neurasthenic’ Darwin, in
his prime congratulating himself on being able to
work two hours a day—
Natural selection will never produce in a being any-
thing-injurious to itself, for natural selection acts solely
by and for the good of each. No organ will be formed,
as Paley has remarked, for the purpose of causing pain or
for doing an injury to its possessor.— The Origin of
Species, c. vi.
A couple of short sentences have been chosen
expressly, though Darwin’s work abounds in long
and difficult sentences; but, even at Darwin’s
best, feel the difference in speed of bis style as
compared with Macaulay’s, and argue the differ-
ence in speed of the mind behind the style.—And
everywhere a similar deduction maw be drawn
from stvle, and tested by fact. It does not
follow, of course, that a quick, lucid style is the
best literary style : that s another story.
J*
Ellis's just-publisheu A Study of British
Genius (Hurst and Blackett ; 7s. 6tf. net) is inter-
esting, as all that Ellis writes is interesting.. But
it is not a study of British genius—in our special
sense of ' genius, or even in Ellis's. Genius is...
what Blake had, for example. Not the spirit that
watched over one’s shoulder in ancient days.
Not the individual’s inborn faculty (though this
is a good current usage). But a type of mind asso-
ciated witli nervous instability, characterised by
exceptional exaltation, capanle of intellectual or
emotional creation peculiarly high-pitched and
(compared with talent’s creation) dazzling.
Moreau’s “neurosis” (quoted by Ellis) meets the
idea. Moreau regards the genius-neurosis as the
synonym of exaltation (not trouble or perturba-
tion) of the intellectual faculties. “ The word
‘neurosis’ would indicate a particular dis-
position of the faculties, a disposition still
in part physiological, but overflowing those
physiological limits.” And Moreau presents a
genealogical-tree with genius, insanity, crime,
etc., among its branches ; the common root being
“the hereditary idiosyncratic nervous state.”
And Grasset, “while not regarding genius as a
neurosis, considers that it is united to the neuroses
by a common trunk, this trunk being a tempera-
ment and not a disease.” With this definition
Ellis is apparently, in agreement ; and one
thinks it would be better if the word genius
were restricted to mean this specific ex-
alted genius. In A Study of British Genius,
Ellis uses it “merely to signify high intellectual
ability.” So his types include hundreds of emi-
nent persons of talent, who lived happy or died
healthy, and had not a trace of genius about them—
Jane Austen or John Opie, for example. And his
types include scores of eminent persons so far
away down the stream of history that their genius
is unidentifiable—St. Wulfstan or Admiral Byng,
for example. Ellis should really have called his
book A Study of Eminent British Persons ; since
that is what it amounts to.
Ellis found his eminent persons in the recently-
completed Dictionary of National Biography. He
omitted royalty and the nobility to begin with,
since these classes are for the greater part eminent
only by accident of birth. Then he took the
eminent to whom three pages are devoted in the
Dictionary , and added others who have less than
three pages if the notice-writers! seemed to con-
sider them exceptionally able. Finally, he sorted
out 975 eminent men and 55 eminent women—and
a nondescript lot they are : one would cheerfully
deny genius in the special sense to nine-tenths
of them. Given the basis, procedure was easy ;
and Ellis proceeded. The weakness of his book
lies first of all in the accidental classification (even
though this be the only satisfactory way of
eliminating the author’s-personal equation), and
secondly in the fact that he builds high on a
meagre statistical basis—builds higher than such
a basis will permit. Yet, if his deductions
be considered as marking tendencies only, they
may be welcomed to that extent as regards eminent
persons, but emphatically not as regards genius.
Ellis, then, says, or quotes, or argues, or shows
that of his 1030 British, persons of “pre-eminent
intellectual ability ” 11 appeared from the" fourth
to the tenth century, 5 in the eleventh century, 11
in the 12th, 9 in the 13th, 16 in the 14th, 32 in the
I.sth, 161 in the lOth, 191 in the 17th, 372 in the
18th, 223 in the 19th— only four of these belonging
to the second half of the 19th (incomplete). That,
throughout eight centuries, a marked rise in in-
tellectual ability has very frequently been preceded
by a marked fall; and in every century, from 11th
to 18th, with exception of 17th, most of the great
men have been born in the latter half (like you
and me). . That of the splendid 1030, 659 be-
longed to England, 28 to Wales, 137 to Scotland,
63 to Ireland ; 97 were mixed British, and 46
mixed B. and foreign. That, judged by the
eminent standard of England, Wales has pro-
duced fewer than her share, Ireland still fewer,
and Scotland , decidedly more (which of itself
would show we are not dealing with genius,
despite th eperfervidvm ingenimn). That there are
three great foci of intellectual ability in England—
East Anglia, the south-west, and the Welsh
border. That it seems impossible to find any
eminent person who belongs to London through
all his grand-parents (accident of birthplace
not taken into account), seeing that Cantlie, who
investigated degeneration amongst Londoners,
could not find a single Londoner in this sense
during four years of hunting. That “there is
neither in Alban nor Ireland,” says the Cuehullain
saga, “an army that can put down the men of
Ulster when once their weakness is gone and their
anger is kindled ” —and people think to put down
Dill-Macky!
s
That (with a breath) the Anglo-Dare appears to
possess (like Newton) a special aptitude for
mathematics ; and the mathematical abilities of
Cambridge are due to the fact that Cambridge
drains the ability of nearly the whole Anglo-
Danish district. That the Irish make eminent
actors, and the Scotch don’t. _That the clergy
breed more geniuses, and more idiots, than any
ocher class. That butchers tend to be fair and shoe-
makers to be dark That a man is just as likely
to inherit ability through his father as through
his mother. That the size of the average genius-
producing family is above the normal—as in the
case of lunatios and criminals. That men of
ability are the offspring of predominantly boy-
producing parents, and women of ability the
offspring of predominantly girl-producing parents.
That it is likely that the maternal exhaustion
which tends to produce males tends also to pro-
duce genius. That genius (like crime and
insanity) cleaves more often to the eldest
and youngest children in a family than to
the middle'-ones. (That Ellis’s argument could
have been greatly improved if he had read
N.S.W. Statistician’s unique birth-rate essay.)
That the universities used to foster a majority
of the eminent, but do so no longer. That
eminent men and women marry later, and are
less fertile and more sterile than the non-
eminent; but they live a long time for the reason
that they must live long or they won’t become
eminent. That gout and consumption are asso-
ciated with genius. That, as Good hart remarks,
“illegibility is a disease.” That lawyers,
soldiers and scientists tend to be fair; actors,
divines, and statesmen to be dark ; and poets and
painters run well in both colours. That men of
genius have very bright eyes—this possibly being
connected with an unusual activity of the cerebral
circulation ; and are inclined to be bashful. That
the real affinity of genius is with congenital im-
becility rather than with insanity (disputed).
That that will do; and for further details and
interesting appendices of the classified eminent
you are referred to the book.

Criticising Brinkley’s Japan, The Athenmm
says that |>V
What part Japan may ultimately play in the Far East
it is hard to say, but, as these volumes amply show, the
Japanese are out of all sympathy and touch with the
West in relation to philosophy, art, music, poetry, and
literature. It is, therefore, open to doubt whether,
apart from questions of commerce and navigation, the
predominance of Japan would be more advantageous to
civilization as understood in the West than the pre-
dominance of Russia, who, after all, is not unsympathetic
"■ith the higher aspects of Latin and Teutonic civiliza-
tion.
Perhaps the best proof of Japan’s progress yet
tendered is given to The Times by Fi H. Balfour,
apropos Herbert Spencer’s warning to the Japs
wot to admit the intrusion of Europe. Says
Balfour—
During my residence in Tokio, more than ten years ago,
I was in the frequent, habit "of dropping into Maruya’s,
the well-known bookseller of Mihon-bashi, who gets out
English books on science and philosophy for the use of
students at the Imperial University. One morning, I
made a remark to the shopman upoij the very large
assortment of Herbert. Spencer's work*with which the
shelves were encumbered —regarding it, not unnaturally,
as eyideuoe of Spencer's popularity. “ Ah, yes,” was the
unexpected reply, “ Spencer is a drug in the market. We
sell scarcely any of his books now, as you see. The
young' fellows consider that he is ‘ passb,’ and prefer
something more up to date ” !) ,
As to books about nun ill id the North.
Capt. Brinkley’s just-published work on Japan
and China is the inost comprehensive survey
of the whole Japanese field df afiairs and
arts. It js in twelve vols. (ten volumes pub-
lished) at 14s. net; and can be seen at Sydney
and Melbourne Public .Libraries. A handier book
on war topics and tbe Russian advance is
The' Path o f Empire, by George Lynch;
10s. net. Bor Korea, read. Kora, by
Angus Hamilton ; 15s. net. For .Japanese
social life, Knkoro, by Lafcadio Hen rue;
ss. net. Other interesting books aliout the
Japanese are The Heart of Japan , by C. L.
Brownell, 3s. fid.—not serious; and Japanese
Physical Training, ss. net—recently noticed.
And Asia and Europe by C. M. Townsend, os.
net, gives you a philosophical view of the rela-
tions of Occident and Orient, brilliantly written.
For maps, consult Stanford’s of Eastern China,
Japan, and Korea, 35.; and Stanford’s map of the
Siberian railway, 10s. 6 d.

may be called upon at any time to resume serving his
original sentence. , • , i

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the Gate.
It was long and long ago
(Did her people ever know?)
When a body, young and pretty, used to wait
With a girlish love and strong
(And she never waited long !)
For another due to meet her at the gate.
How she filled the ears that harked,
And she stilled the dogs that barked,
And her lover knew when father was away.
How they used to coo and kiss
Through a fleeting hour of bliss,
And the lovers’ names ?—l’ll tell some other day.
So we courted on the sly
In the starlight, she and I
(And I wonder did her people ever know).
O, the nights we used to flirt,
Vagabond and Miss Alert,
In the shadows by the gateway, long ago.
I am far away, and, p’r’aps,
“ Someone ” flirts with other chaps—
And as gaily as she ever did with me;
But I know it is the way
Of the old world every day,
And I hope they are as happy as can be.
Other girls have come across
Cupid’s highway; and the loss
Of a dear one’s in the game of love and hate.
But I’m longing oft (Ah me !)
Just for one short hour to be
With that “someone ” in the shadows by the gate.
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
A Rhyme of Disillusion.
The lift of the wave and the reeling mast,
The stinging spray, and the whistling breeze ;
The blood runs red, and the pulse beats fast
As we face the gale on the open seas.
Life is a rapture, and sorrow flees
The heights and hollows where seabirds sing.
At least, we are told so, but, if you please,
I don’t think I’d care for that sort of thing.
The gallop of squadrons that thunder past,
’"’’e blazon of bmners above the trees,
FI is ing of sword blades, bared at last
To umpire the nations’ rivalries.
Death by the steel, or by fell disease,
For the land we love —for our home and King.
Thanks ! I prefer to take my ease ;
I’m sure I don’t care for that sort of thing.
The glamor of twilight and eyes downcast,
The twitter of birds and the hum of bees—
“ To this complexion we come at last,”
Snared by the old-new witcheries ;
A dainty touch on the ivory keys ;
A whispered word while the dancers swing—
Yes, poets may tell us of joys like these,
But I’m rather too old for that sort of thing.
Brethren, you care not for words like these,
There is not the least doubt you will have
your fling,
But the years wear on, and by slow degrees,
You’ll find you won’t care for that sort of thing.
M.L.
TAIWA.
Ocean’s Own.
The song that the surf is brawling
Is meant for their ears alone,
Who followed the deep-sea calling
And slaved at it, blood and bone.
Oh ! softly the north wind sings them
A measure that bids them rest
Where Ocean, their mother, swings them
To sleep on her throbbing breast.
The moon lifts gold in the gloaming,
The sun in the west sinks red,
And birds of the sea pass roaming,
But the Ocean’s Own lie dead.
Perchance as they lie they’re dreaming
Of home and a childhood’s tune
That rang through the storm-seas’ screaming,
And sobbed in the warm monsoon ;
Or maybe again they’re thrashing
With spray on the high bridge-rail,
And laboring engines clashing
A dirge to the men who fail.
The world passes on, forgetting,
But, off in the ports, I know
There’s many a brave heart fretting
For the good, brave hearts laid low.
Their ships swept out on the noon-tides,
And lonely their mast-head lights
Were quivering far, when the moontides
Swam glittering through the nights ;
And strong where the storm-stars flicker
They drove through the wash and roll,
And ever their screws spun quicker
When baulked of their distant goal.
For the Ocean’s Own were roamers—
By power of sail and steam
They swung in the long Cape combers,
Or droned up the Hoogli’s stream.
The song that the surf is shouting
Is meant for their ears alone
Who went to their work undoubting,
And slaved at it blood and bone.
Oh ! softly the Ocean swings them
To sleep on her heaving breast,
And the wind from the sweet north sings them
The songs that their hearts loved best.
Soft eyes are sad in their waking
Eyes bright with the tears unshed—
And there’s many a brave heart breaking ;
But the Ocean’s Own are dead.
M. L.
WILL LAWSON.

Nervous Prostration
A Dreadful Period of Suffering 1 .
“Clements Tonic Saved Me."
The Case of W[rs. E. WEEDEN.
(BY a special reporter.)
Ap great interest was manifested in all
circles respecting the following subject, a
representative waited on Mrs. Elizabeth
YVeeden, of No. 67 Cleveland-street, Rtd-
fern, who is well known in the locality in
which she lives.
“ I am pleased that you have called,”
said Mrs. YVeeden, “as it gives me an op-
portunity to let the public know what was
my real position at the time you refer to,
and also to remove any misapprehensions
which might exist in the minds of some of
my neighbours. It appears that the
general assumpti n was that I had profited
in health by following a certain course of
treatment recommended by my doctor. No-
thing of the sort was the case, for after
cairying out his instructions for a long time
my prospects were no brighter, so I gave
him up and tried to get relief through the
aid of several medicines which I had seen
advert’sed in the papers. They were no
less effective, and as they all failed to reach
the base of my afflictions I thought I was
never going to have my health Lack again.”
“ What was the nature of your com-
plaint ? ” enquired the reporter.
‘ 'he doctor said it was nervous prostra-
tion U'uui which I was suffering, and I think
he was right, fori used to tremble as if an
electric battery was at work all through my
body, while the fearsome state of my mind
was most distressing, as I was always
fancying that some terrible trouble was
hanging over me. # If the children were not
home from school to time I worried myself
with the belief that something had happened
to them, and such anxiety seemed to make
me very irritable and short-tempered. I
was ahva s tired and extremely drowsy,
f eling at 9 o’clock in the morning as if I
could go to he 1 and slet, o all day, such a
til ng as a desire to get my work done sel-
dom entering my head. Careless and in-
d decent to everything around me, my life
was one continual misery. There was a
depressing pain in my head as if a band had
been dra u tielitly over my eyes, before
which 1 ttle dark specks frequently floated,
and somet mes I could scarcely see for a
haziness which appeared in front of me. I
used to imagine I had a touch of ear-ache,
owing to a peculiar sensation like the sound
of a bell ling ng in my ears, while the
shock ng headaches I had to endure I shall
never forget. You have no idea how
wretched I felt when I had eaten a few
mouthfuls of food. My stomach became
filled with wind, from which I suffered great
pain until sour belchings came on, when I
felt a 1 ttle easier ; but, in the meantime,
my chest was proving very troublesome,
with a feeling as if a load of some kind
was going to smother me. Then I com-
menced to feel sick, and before long my
food was retched up again. I had the most
awful pains in my side and shoulders; but
the manner in which my heart palpitated
caused me many a fright. If I walked a few
yards my heart fluttered dreadfully, so you
may depend I exerted myself as little as
possible, more especially as I felt faint and
giddy when I moved about. After the least
effort to do a little work I used to collapse,
my feebleness being so marked that if I
tried to sweep a room my strength failed so
completely that I felt powerless, eyen to
the tips of my fingers.”
“ You spoke about feeling so drowsy all
day. How did you pass your nights ? ”
“ YVell, I was in such a sluggish condition
that I could always sleep, but the strange
part about it was that I never felt rested or
refreshed. This was due, no doubt, to the
fact that I had horrible dreams and night-
mares, which made me scream out whilst
asleep.* The children have often come to
me in the middle of the night to wake me
up so that my screaming would cease, and
how terrified I was when awakened ! My
mouth was in a nasty state, too, as if I had
been tasting copper, my tongue also being
covered with fur. I thought my skin would
never get white again, it was that yellow,
and I was just about as thin as I could pos-
sibly be. Nobody but those who have had
a similar experience to mine can form the
slightest conception of what mental torture
is involved when pain is endured and all hope
is lost. There was no pleasure in being
alive, but still I clung to what little life was
left, and now you see a different woman al-
together. lam quite well again.”
“You are very fortunate. But what
made you so? ”
“ It was a medicine called Clements Tonic
which helped me out of my difficulties, and
but for that remedy I do not know what I
would have done. As the doctor’s and
other medicines could do me no good it is
only right to say that Clements Tonic saved
me, and I shall always deem it my duty to
make this fact clear to other people. v Yes,
it was when I had taken nearly a bottle of
Clements Tonic that I felt warmed and
strengthened, with a good healthy glow
running right through my body, and my
spirits brighter than they had bi en ever
since my illness began. Getting some more
Clements Tonic, that miserable drowsiness
commenced to leave me, and as I was al-
ready taking a little food I began to pick
up strength. I thought this was splendid
progress, and I was highly elated with
the great success which attended my use
of Clements Tonic—a fact that was borne
out by the abatement of my tortures. My
headaches became so insignificant that I
hardly noticed them ; then they died out
altogether, and I can-say the same about
the pains in my side and shoulders. I
was so thankful to be thus relieved, and
still more encouragement to pei severe with
Clements Tonic came when flatulence was
remedied and the heaviness was lifted
from my chest. The anxiety&which my
heart had caused by palpitating at every
turn was readily set aside by Clements
Tonic, which seemed to find its way to
every nook and corner of my internal
organs and root out every particle of
disease which had been left untouched by
previous medicines. The sleep which then
came was calm and refreshing, and when
I got up it was with a clean mouth and
a good appetite for my morning meal,
and also with a feeling of energy which
made it a pleasure to have some work
to do. All this time my nervousness was
becoming less apparent in every way. I
could soon tolerate any noise without
trembling, and my mind was free from all
fears. I recognised how different my life
was, and my grateful thoughts were all
centred on Clements Tonic, which I con-
tinued taking for another week or so, when
I found rmself as well as ever, with my
complexion clear and my weight regained.”
“ With your permission I’ll reproduce
this narrative.”
“ You can publish it in any way you like,
as I’ve only spoken the truth."

THE PROPHET ELIJAH GIVES FATHER ABRAHAM THE TIP.

Supposing I go away and describe what has happened. It will injure Australia’s credit with the bondholders.” —Daily paper.

THURSDAY, MARCH 10, 1904.
A Nice Bill of Fare—With No Money to
Pay For It.
The programme for the first session of the second
Australian Parliament as outlined by Premier
Deakin promises a liberal supply of reform—
Federal old age pensions; further assistance in
developing the agricultural resources of the Com-
monwealth ; encouragement to the iron industry;
an effort to stimulate immigration ; completion of
the Federal machinery by the appointment of a
High Commissionerand an Inter-state Commission;
and the taking over from the States of the control
of quarantine and coast lights. Also there ara to
lie further attempts to solve the State debts
problem. There is nothing at all to be
said in criticism of such a bill of fare for
what it includes; a session which accom-
plished all that would be a wonderful one. With
a fuller explanation of some measures, such as the
Arbitration Bill, the proposals of the Govern-
ment may prove to be out of sympathy with
the temper of Parliament. But a Governor’s
; >eech necessarily has to pass over details and
■ mfirie itself to broad principles. Taking it so,
vhere is only one criticism that can be directed
against the Deakin programme, but that is a
serious one. The menu is attractive enough, but
there is no suggestion as to how the viands are to
be paid for. All sorts of fine, expensive reforms,
such as old age pensions and Federal quarantine,
are promised, but there is not a hint as to the
means by which they are to be carried out. The
Commonwealth already comes close to the quarter
share of the customs and excise revenue to
which the Braddon clause of the Constitution
confines it, and soon it will not be able
to rely upon any more, from that source.
Under the circumstances it is useless for the Aus-
tralian Government to propose, for instance,
Federal old age pensions without directly indi-
cating the source from which it intends to get the
money : and so far it has failed to indicate any-
thing of the kind, which is a rather large matter
to leave out of its official programme.
* • • •
A Federal scheme of old age pensions calculated
on the N.S.W. basis would cost £1,568,000 a year.
An agricultural bureau, with bonuses for new
crops and assistance to open up new markets ; an
immigration bureau to advertise the resources of
the continent; a High Commissioner and an
Inter-state Commission ; Federal industrial arbi-
tration ; a bonus for establishing an iron in-
dustry ; the first steps towards founding the
Federal capital and building the transcon-
tinental railway; extension of Australian trade
and interests in Papua and Oceania generally;
Federal control of quarantine, lighthouses, beacons
and buoys—would, in the aggregate, cost
another large sum, perhaps another million. Of
the total £2,500,000, a proportion would be merely
transferred from the States to the Common-
wealth, a proportion would be new expenditure—
old age pensions for Tasmania, Westralia, Queens-
land, and South Australia, the iron and agricul-
tural bonuses, and the salaries of new officials
created. But whether new expenditure or trans-
ferred expenditure, the Australian Government
would be called upon to foot the bill, and since it
has at present no means to do so, it is a quite
useless and foolish business for Premier
Deakin to parade a great list of pro-
gressive measures without any accompany-
ing proposals for taxation to meet their cost.
Allowing that the States with old age pension
schemes now in existence could be in some way
induced to enter into an arrangement to give up
so much of their revenues as they now devote to
that object (a highly improbable contingency), the
Commonwealth would still require, roughly,
£750,000 to meet an old age pensions account, and
on the other accounts—again allowing for State
contributions to meet the charges of transferred
departments, such as quarantine and lighthouses
—£750,000. In effect, the new cost to the Com-
monwealth of bringing old age pensions in Victoria
to a decent standard and of providing old age pen-
sions for Tasmania, Westralia, South Australia,
and Queensland ; of adopting a progressive
policy in regard to immigration, iron and
agricultural bonuses, and the Pacific Islands
and other matters, would total about £1,500,000.
Perhaps Mr. Deakin expects that Elijah Dowie
will summon ravens to bring sovereigns from the
sky. Perhaps not.
* • * •
The speech put into the mouth of the Australian
Governor is indeed singularly reticent in regard
to the financial question. This single paragraph
refers to ways and means:—
The revenue derived from customs and excise ha* been
equal to anticipations. As the incidence of duties under
the tariff contemplates the substitution of Australian for
imported goods, no considerable expansion of such
receipts under normal conditions is to be expected. The
estimates of expenditure will be framed with economy,
having due regard to the magnitude and importance of
the interests under your control.
Which is tine enough, as far as it goes. The
administrati re economy of the Australian Govern-
ment has beei; admirable, and there is no reason
to expect any vciy great growth of the customs
revenue, since the tariff, poor as it is, is in some
directions discouraging imports—and ought to dis-
courage them much more than it does. The
excise returns should, however, soon show the
results of an increasing prosperity, and a revision
of the tariff to cover certain articles now on the
free list might bring in another £500,000 a year.
But, on the whole, taking a most favorable view,
the Common weal tli could not expect, by any
means, to have available for its own use anything
more than another £200,000 a year of customs and
excise revenue, and that is presuming a revision
of the tariff to tax tea and other articles now on
the free list. Not even that much is pro-
posed by Mr. Deakin, inadequate as it would
be to meet the bill for £1,500,000 of new
expenditure which his programme would entail.
But there is available a much more fruitful and
beneficent tax —a progressive unimproved land
values tax, passing over the holder of the small
farm and the home allotment, taxing equitably
the more prosperous landholders, and coming with
severity on the land monopolist—a tax designed
to bring in revenue and to force the land into use,
ranging from Id. in the £ to 3d. in the <£ on large
estates and with a 50 per cent, surtax in the case
of absentee holders. Such a tax, ranging over the
£350,000,000 or so of private land values in the
Commonwealth, would easily reap at least
£1,500,000 of revenue, and would at the same
time penalise the monopoly by a few holders of
the fertile tracts of the continent, giving to the
absentee landlord the option of selling his acres
to those who wish to use them here, or submit-
ting to a practically confiscatory exaction.
• ♦ * *
So many are the political benefits which would
follow an adequate taxation of the land values of
the Commonwealth that, if there were no revenue
needs making it desirable, such needs ought to
be created. The Australian Government pro-
poses bonuses to aid the agriculturist. There
could be no better bonus than access to the
fertile lands of the coast, now locked against the
plough by land monopoly. Land taxation—the
right kind of land taxation—would give that
access. The Federal Government wants to
encourage immigration. There is possible in the
State of N.S.W. alone a population of at least
30,000,000 if the land is made accessible, and if,
by Protection and the growth of manufactures,
a local market is assured. The destruction of
land monopoly offers, with scientific Protection,
the means to at once attract immigration, to make
the farmer prosperous, and to endow the Com-
monwealth with the revenues needed for a policy
of progressive Federalism.
* * * *
It may be objected that the States already levy
land taxation and that a Commonwealth tax
would thus be a double impost. The objection is
partly untrue, and so far as it is true is invalid.
Some, but not all, of the States have imposed
land taxes, but none of them are adequate, and
such as exist might be repealed, if so desired, on the
imposition of a Federal tax. The State finances
would not be thus prejudiced, for the money raised
by this means by the Federal Treasurer would be
devoted to transferred expenditure, or would
prevent new expenditure trenching further on the
customs and excise repayments to the States.
It is just as desirable that land taxation should
be uniform throughout the continent as that
customs taxation should be uniform. Land mo-
nopoly invites attack in every State, and every
State should be given the same advantage of
attracting settlement by the breaking up of the
big estates on the railway lines in its well-watered
districts. South Australia at present has the
best system of land taxation. That State charges
fd. in the £ on all land without exception, and
doubles the tax when the value of an estate is over
£5OOO. In the case of absentees, a surtax of 20
per cent, on the original tax is imposed. An
absentee with a landed estate worth £5OOO or over
thus pays 1 4-sd. N.S.W. levies an unchanging
rate of Id. in the £ on all land, allowing an
exemption of <£24o, and making no difference
in the case of absentees or large land monopo’i ,ts
or people who don’t put their land to pro 7 Lihie
use. Victoria can claim the champion ab.irdity
in the way of a land tax. Estates under £2500 in
value and under 640 acres in extent are absolutely
exempt. Thus the poor farmer of Collins-street,
Melbourne, struggling along with an income of
£20,000 a year from his tiny patch of real estate,
is saved from the extortion of the tax-gatherer.
If there is any person in the State owning a square
mile of city property, and thus liable to taxation
(which is very doubtful), even then the tax would
touch him lightly. He could assess his land as “not
maintaining a sheep to the acre,” which would be
true enough, and return its value at £1 per acre
(though it might maintain a hundred shops and
offices to the aero)! It is not at all wonderful
that this tax, expressly refusing to touch all
city properties, should yield to the Stateless than
£lOO,OOO a year. Tasmania has a tax on real
estate ranging from a half penny in the £ wheu
the assessed value is under £5OOO up to Id. in the
£ when the value is £BO,OOO and over. It is not
an unimproved land values tax, and a ridiculous
system of assessment destroys much of the value
it might have. The impost yields to the State
£35,337 a year. Neither Queensland nor Westralia
has any form of land tax for State
None of these existing State taxes fulfills,
or gets near to fulfilling, the needs of the
case. In no case would any very great harm
be done if they were levied in addition to a
Commonwealth land tax, and in all cases their
repeal on the imposition of such a tax could be
undertaken without unduly disturbing the State
finances.
* • • •
All that stands in the way of a Federal land
tax is some ridiculous presumption, to which Mr.
Barton and Mr. Reid agreed during the first
Federal elections, and which Mr. Deakin seems
inclined to blindly follow, that the power of direct
taxation, the only taxing power that the Com-
monwealth holds absolutely unfettered, should not
be exercised. Such a compact had its uses for
gentlemen who wished to keep the issue in Austra-
lian politics a mere matter of “ins”and “outs,’’and
to exclude from Federal platforms any really vital
question. It enabled Mr. Barton to plead on one
side that adequate Protection was impossible, and
Mr. Reid to allege that Freetrade was impossible,
and for both to argue that the financial exigencies
required a compromise revenue-producing tariff.
And it enabled them both to wearily dodge the
old age pensions question, and many other ques-
tions, on the excuse that money was not available.
But a more progressive spirit should rule the Austra-
lian Parliamentnow. The completion of the Federal
machinery, and the necessity for such reforms as
Federal old-age pensions, call for revenues which
the customs and excise cannot yield. At the
same time, the locked-up lands of the Common-
wealth, the stream of agriculturists departing
from Australia, the forcing of our settlers out to
the arid interior, demand a tax which will be of
economic, as well as fiscal, benefit. With a pro-
posal for such a tax, Premier Deakin’s programme
would be admirable. Without it, no serious
attention need be given to many of its proposals.
But the Federal political situation is just now so
uncertain that Mr. Deakin’s programme cannot
be taken as saying the final word as to the work
of the session. A more radical leader may shortly
come into power, and may be able to see the vital
importance of a Federal land tax, progressive as
regards large estates, suppressive as regards
absentees.
Back to the Coast.
The N.S.W. Farmers and Settlen’ Association
has drafted an election programme:—
(1) Land settlement to be paramount over revenue, to
include (a) abolition of auction sales of country lands,
(b) conversion of holdings, subject to continuous resi-
dence attaching to the land, (c) elective land boards;
(2) State land bank ; (3) closer settlement by compulsory
resumption of private estates of over £20,000 unimproved
capital value ; (4) water conservation; (5) a progressive
railway policy, to include (a) extension and construction
of paying lines, (b) railway revenue to be credited with
increase in land revenue due to new railway constrqp-
tion ; (6) local government.
This is mostly pottering about with the tailings
and letting the metal slide. Take the first plank.
It is not true that settlers have been lately squeezed
in order to provide revenue. Every land measure
for the past 10 years has had for its object the
reduction of rents, interest, and purchase-money
instalments, the extension of lease, or the creation
of new and easier terms and forms of holding.
Administration has proceeded mainly on the same
lines. The chief business of many land boards has
been to find excuses for the non-fulfilment of con-
ditions and the waiving of the forfeiture which
has been incurred. Practically for years past no
man who has proved himself a bona-fide settler
has been disturbed, no matter what breaches of
conditions he has been guilty of; and frequently,
in addition to paying no rent, he has been actually
subsidised and relieved by the Advances to
Settlers Department and from the Seed Wheat
Fund. It has been the game of the mostly well-to-
do coastal landholders, and of the Foreign-trade
crowd who have run in double harness with them, to
get the poor devil, struggling “inside” with debt
and drought and distance, to believe that he would
be allrightif the State did not bleed him so severely.
So often have they told him this that, in many
cases, he believes it; and instead of making a
stiff progressive land tax, with a speoial tax on
absentees like the Peel River Co. and the A. A.
Co., the first plank of his platform, he still keeps
his eye on tne desert, and potters about with
demands that closer settlement shall be made
“paramount over revenue,” and for elective land
boards. Certainly he asks for “closer settle-
ment by compulsory resumption of private estates
of over £20,000 unimproved capital value,” but he
isn’t game to couple a heavy graduated land tax
with it—apparently he is afraid that some day he
might have to pay 2|d. himself.
• * • •
If the graduated land tax were put on first,
along with the special absentee tax, the capital
value of the lands would be reduced; and so
he would get for £750 what, without the tax, he
would probably have to pay .£lOOO for. Also,
the big estates would cut themselves up without
any State resumption policy. But he doesn’t
think of that; he only thinks of the 2£d. The
only way he can see of getting “cheap” land is
by going out into the districts that are either a
desert every ordinary year or are liable to be a
desert any year ; and out there water conservation
is necessary to begin with. That is another thing
the coastal landlord and the amiable theorist have
“kidded” the selector with—“ With water con-
servation you’ll be all right.” Mostly by water
conservation irrigation is meant; yet Mr. Wade,
Principal Engineer for Water Supply in N.S.W.,
lately came back from America to say that there,
in a country much more suited for irrigation than
this, the system is not now applied anywhere to
agriculture, because it is found that it does not
pay. What hope, then, has Australia ? Some
day, perhaps, in the very remote future, settle-
ment will have to slop out over the dry inlands ;
but that time will only come when, the coast
being fairly thickly populated, there will be a
market here for all that the inlands can pro-
duce. Then, not having to pay freight on
their stuff to the other side of the world,
even the inlands may be profitably tilled.
For the present, however, there is room
for all Australia’s population on the coastal
lands, where costly and uncertain irriga-
tion isn’t an essential, and where settlement
doesn’t involve heavy debt for hundreds of miles
of railway construction. To borrow and spend
millions in an almost hopeless attempt to settle
the inlands, when there are millions of acres of
coastal lands which can be settled with far more
certainty of success, without borrowing anything,
but actually by raising money in taxes to pay off
existing debts, is an amazing piece of folly which
only a shockingly foolish and misguided
country would think of. On the one
hand ” we can borrow money and build
railways, and borrow more money to make up the
deficit on the working of those railways, and tax
ourselves to pay interest, and borrow more money
to carry out irrigation works, and then find our-
selves ruined by the first drought; on the other
hand, by a stiff graduated tax on land values, and
a special tax on absentee owners, we may not only
get revenue, but force coastal land into cultiva-
tion, and so escape for many years the building of
long railways and expensive irrigation works, and
the debt and the interest which those things
mean. By one process we pile up debt; by the
other we may wipe off the existing debt. And
still, instead of declaring for the only policy
which will give them a reasonable chance of
living in reasonable comfort, the Farmers’ and
Settlers’ Association can’t think of anything
better than the same old “progressive railway
policy,” the same old “water conservation,” and
elective land boards.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Stinking Fish Party.
Hugh Nelson, a relic of Queensland politics,
who now acts as President of the Legislative
Council and deputy-State-Governor, recently
returned in tears from a visit to Maoriland. The
old man was promptly interviewed as to the
cause of his grief, and after he had blotted
himself he explained the situation. This is the
daily paper report:
Australia as Sub is Seen.
Hr. Nelson stated that in Maoriland he met a number
of persons from the other parts of the Empire on holiday
like himself, and was extremely pained to discover their
ideas of Australia. “ I had to confess for the first time
in my life,” said Mr. Nelson, “ that I was ashamed of my
country. The ridicule with which they contemplated
our White Australia cry, particularly with reference to
the postal contract, although I naturally expected some,
is stronger than I could have believed. That this feeling
is doing Australia immense harm I think there can be no
doubt, as evidence the English press, and articles such
as have appeared in papers all over the globe.
The Bulletin has no doubt that these statements
are true —in fact, a member of The Bulletin
staff who recently overlanded through Maoriland
from Auckland to the Bluff, met the same kind of
globe-trotter and noticed the same feeling. And
he came back feeling ashamed, not of Australia,
but of a pitiable and crawling class of alleged
Australians—the class which is represented by
the Abgus, the Sydney Herald and Telegraph,
the Brisbane Telegraph and Courier, by Hugh
Nelson, and Bruce Smith, and other people and
rags and institutions of their class. He came
back disgusted at the Stinking Fish party in Aus-
tralia—a party which, because of the violent
manner in which it bangs its stale flounder against
the globe generally, is taken by people abroad to
be much larger and more influential that it is,
also much more truthful. English people have
learned, through the Australian papers circulated
in England, and through extracts from Australian
papers reprinted in the English journals
(1) That Australia has been ruined by Federation, and
that a desperate struggle must be made to abolish the
Commonwealth and cast off the yoke. (Only a few
papers and fools talk in this strain, but they are very loud
and clangy, and the people outside don’t realise the
utter insignificance of such folk as John Haynes and
Jack Want).
(2) That Australia has been ruined by Protection.
(3) That Australia has been ruined by the Labor party.
(4) That Australia “ excluded ” the Six Hatters, which
is a lie, but the outside person can’t be expected to
know it.
(6) That the Australian Government refused to allow a
crew of perishing mariners to be rescued because they
were colored men, which is also a shameful lie.
(I) That immigration has been stopped by the legisla-
tion of the Australian Parliament; which is a lie. Immigra-
tion, under the legislation of the Australian Parliament
is larger than it had been for years.
(7) That industry has been blighted by Labor legislation,
which is a lie; since Labor legislation seriously com-
menced (about 1897) the number of employees in Austra-
lian factories has increased nearly three times as fast as
it did before, when then was no Labor legislation. Also
almost every bank in Australia shows vastly' increased
profits since Labor legislation commenced.
(8) That we seek to dictate to British steamship owners
what labor they will employ, which is a lie. We only
propose to dictate to ourselves what steamers we will
subsidise.
(9) That Australia can’t live without an “influx of
foreign capital ” —the country', presumably, being quite
helpless “ on its own."
(10) That we are too ignorant to manufacture for our-
selves, and must depend on imports.
(11) That we are quite unable to work, and must lean
up against a fence and give orders while niggers work
for us.
The Stinking Fish party, to which almost all
Hugh Nelson’s political friends belong, has told
these slanderous lies so often that the outside
world has really commenced to believe them.
Then some prominent Fish goes abroad, and meets
people who believe the whole series of Stinking
Fish theories—they believe them because they
naturally don’t know what a contemptible gang
of traitors it is that circulates them —and Hugh
Nelson is quite surprised at meeting his friends’
lie, and professes that he doesn’t know where it
comes from. Also, he is just as shocked at the
low opinion held of Australia outside as if the
party to which he belongs hadn’t been toiling for
years, by a most amazing process of calumny, to
create that low opinion, and to hold Australia up
to the world’s scorn as a place that drowned
colored sailors, and threw hatters overboard, and
which was ruined by Arbitration Acts and such
like. The attitude suggests that of the spieler
who, with the “ mug ” in tow, is quite astonished
at meeting his confederate, and never saw him
before in his life. The outside world must
have a poor opinion of Australia, until it realises
that the bulk of Australian daily papers and the
bulk of Australian Tory politicians are just a vast
conspiracy of falsehood and slander—a Stinking
Fish conspiracy, in short.

Birth-rate Hysterics.
Sydney Telegraph, which is pretty hysterical
about the birth-rate at all times, broke out in this
fashion when the report of the N.S. W. Commission
came to light :
For the whole white race this question of the declining
birth-rate is now threatening to become one of the first
magnitude. England, America, and various parts of the
Continent of Europe—notably France—are beginning to
lose ground through it in the struggle _ for national
existence. And the worst feature of all is that where
civilisation has its highest modern development, the signs
of the trouble are usually most apparent. Amongst white
nations those less advanced in the social and intellectual
scale are, generally speaking, the more prolific. Ana
the colored races show to still greater advantage
in that respect. This is the really sinister feature in
the outlook for the Australian Commonwealth. . . •
Within a few days’ sail of us are the sv\ arming millions oi
colored people in South-eastern Asia, amongst whom the
rate of natural increase knows no diminution except that
caused by the natural limitations to the carrying capa-
city of the land on which they live. Our population is
stagnant, while that of countries like China and Japan,
by its unchecked growth, is overgrowing the boundaries
at present assigned to it, and being driven to seek living
room elsewhere. . . . If our own population is to remain
stationary while that of the already over peopled countries
of Asia goes on increasing at the tremendous pace whicfi
a normal birth-rate amongst so many hundreds of millions
implies, the maintenance of a W 1 te Australia must
sooner or later become impossible. 1 -ie sterility which is
blighting these States does not go unobserved amongst
the cramped-up hut still prolific myriads of Japanese ana
Chinese, who look to our enormous tracts of umnhabitea
country- for the room which :.o longer exists for them


home. It has already been commented upon in a manner
to which recent events lend an ominous significance.
The Asiatics, starting with such an immense majority
and increasing faster than the Caucasians, are giving
signs of being about to repeat those chapters of history in
which they are recorded as having stood upon the battle-
field to dispute man for man the superiority of the white
races.
The Telegraph really should not turn on excited
young men who know nothing of their subject to
shout in this fashion. There is no need for quite
so much frenzy. No one knows that the colored
races are increasing faster than the whites; in fact,
the scanty available evidence is all the other way.
There are no reliable statistics for Africa nor for
most of Asia, but we do know that in India the
percentage of increase between the census of 1891
and that of 1901 was only one-seventh that of
Australia, and that in the United States the
colored man formed a smaller proportion of the
population in 1900 than he did m 1870. Amid a
bog of conjecture these are two solid facts to go on
with. Japan and China, so far as can be estimated,
are only half as thiokly peopled as England,
and the Indian empire not one-third as thickly
peopled. Asia itself is estimated to be only half
as densely populated as Europe, and Africa is
almost empty by comparison with Europe.
During the last ten years thepopulation of every
white man’s country, bar France and Ireland,
increased at a greater rate than India —some
countries showed 18 times as great a percentage of
increase. Possibly they increased faster than
China also, but that mangy land supplies so little
information. The white man is fast colonising
Asia ; Siberia is accumulating fresh hundreds of
European immigrants every week, and Japhet is
occupying Tartary and may yet ocoupy Thibet.
Australia isn’t really stagnant; its population is
growing at the rate of nearly 60,000 yearly, and it
will grow faster when there is more local employ-
ment and less importing. The population of
Europe, so far as information goes, is now increa-
sing faster than it ever did before; it is over
400,000,000, and that is why Europe is slop-
ping oyer into thinly-populated Asia. Also,
the white man is making a very respectable
struggle for existence in Canada, the United
States, Mexico, and South America. We know
that our white brother is increasing fast, whereas
the theory of the tremendous increase of our black
brother really rests on very little evidence ; it is
the platitude of the cheap boy reporter and that
is about all. There are very few facts to go
upon, but these few may be summarised : (1) The
white race holds Europe and is spreading over
America and Australia, half of Asia, and a little
bit of Africa. (2) As regards the regions prin-
cipally occupied by the colored races, we know
that Shem is increasing very slowly in India; that
China is, and always has been, a mystery—possibly
a greatly over-rated mystery; that Japan is
making no sensational advance ; and that western
Asia, which once bossed most of the known
world, seems to have only a shred of its one-time
great population. Also, we know that the shriek
about Asia’s stupendous birth-rate is mere con-
jecture. Nobody knows much about Asia’s birth-
rate.

Premier Bent’s Land Wriggle.
Th?fi further development of that loud public roar,'
which Premier Bent, of Victoria, calls his policy,
leaves things' indefinite on every point; barring
the fact that the income-tax is to be reduced there
is nothing but a fog and a yell. Bent certainly
did talk for a while about the compulsory repur-
chase of large estates, but the Government caucus,
which was attended by all the most stupid and
reactionary of the ’Pootra brood, sniffed at the
idea ; and it was eventually decided that no estate
should be so dealt with save on a resolution of
both Houses. A resolution of both Houses in that
direction is just about as improbable as a double
revolution of the sun, or a square moon; and, by
agreeing to such an impossible arrangement, the
Government shed its one Liberal garment, and
now stands as the fine, old, crusted Tory that it
always really was.’ The Opposition, which
held its caucus the same day, didn’t do much
better. Its land plank provides for the immediate
acquisition of 250,000 acres of good land, to
be secured —if not otherwise obtainable—by
compulsory purchase. Victoria consists of
56,245,000 acres, so the Peacock party proposes
to promote settlement over less than une-two-
hundredth part of the area, and it is apologetic
about even that. There are in Victoria 348 big
estates (representing 4,583,061 acres) which are
valued for laud tax at only £1 per acre, and 856
b ‘u- ? states m all (representing 6,777,387 acres)
which come under the land-tax regulations,
taking them all round, they pay tax on a sum
equal to about 245. an acre. The frantio and
revolutionary Peacock threatens faintly to cut up
one-twenty-seventh of this area. And yet Pea-
*uCKdlS ni^es ahead of Bent. The situation of
the Bent Government in this matter is all the
more remarkable seeing that one of its members,
M Leod, goes round orating like this:
If farmers were settled in civilised districts, it would
create traffic on the railways and reduce the deficit,
there were large estates in Victoria with two railways
running through them which did not employ 20 trucks
auring the year. These estates had been instrumental
in creating the Government deficit. One of the first
features of closer settlement was that there should be
good land available near a market, with a good rainfall,
where there were some of the oomforts of civilised life.
That is the situation which the Bent Government
realises should be faced, but Bent’s waistcoat
totters before the storm, and he proposes to do
about . n °khing. Still, there is always hope
that the Australian Parliament will move. It
can t be run for ever on the Intensely Polite prin-
ciples of Premier Deakin. A Federal graduated
land-tax which would make it practically impos-
sible lor any man to hold more than 1000 acres of
first-class land would knock the small Bents and
-teacocks into such a heap that no one could tell
which of them the gorgeous tail and the apology
belonged to, and which of them owned the bad
temper and the yell.

The Federal Arbitration Bill.
Once sweep away the rather clever newspaper lie
that a Federal Arbitration Act, which did nob
exclude public servants would “hand over the
control of the State’s servants to the Federal
Government,” and the position is so clear that it
is astonishing that there should be any honest
difference of opinion on the subject. Such an Act
would decree that all industrial disputes affecting
more than one State should be settled by the
honest arbitration of a Court of Justice
[not of the Federal Government—as well
*7 N.S. Wales Arbitration Act places
all businesses in the State under the control of
B. K. Wise and John See). It would make no
exception between State servants and private
servants, holding that if it is right for the disputes
, individuals and corporations with their em-
ployees to be settled by justice and not by force, it
is equally right that the disputes of Govern-
ments and public bodies with their employees
should be settled in that way. Where is the
ogical ground on which such a position can be
assailed . And if logio has to be assisted with
arguments as to expediency, it is reasonable to
urge that Arbitration, necessary as it is to
settle disputes between private employers
and their men, is to a far greater degree
necessary when one of the parties to a
threatened conflict is the State or the State’s
representatives at the head of such a great service
as the railways or the water supply, a stoppage of
which would be disastrous to the public; and to
point out that the N.S.W. Government in its
Arbitration Act did not.hesitate to include State
employees. The necessity of meeting such facts
is overcome by the Tory State-frights partisans
who bellow “it is handing over State servants to
the control of the Federal Government,” and end
the argument there. Unhappily, they have bewil-
dered Premier Deakin into taking their view—
the view which affirms that an industrial dispute
between a worker and private employer is to be
decided by justice, but between a worker and a
public body by violence—and his 1904 Arbitration
Bill again expressly excludes all employees of the
State or of any public body constituted by the
State (i.e., the Railway Commissions, the Harbor
Trusts, and the like, and, presumably, the muni-
cipal councils). The fact seems to seal the doom
of his Government. It may be a small point to
dismiss a Cabinet about, but a Cabinet which has
allowed its judgment to be so warped by the roar
of Bent and the icy whisper of Irvine, and the
shrieks of the Tory press, as to affirm such a
logical absurdity as that justice is to decide
industrial disputes except when a Government is
concerned, is hardly worth worrying about, how-
ever attractive may be its programme in other
matters.
The Chance for an Honest Freetrader.
The return of Norman Cameron, alleged Free-
trader, by an almost invisible majority, to fill the
seat vacated by the late Edward Braddon looks
like a repetition of a very old and sorry joke,
though it may prove otherwise. Edward Braddon
was Freetrade Premier of Tasmania for nearly six
years, and ran the country with a Protective tariff
of about 1200 items. He was a very scornful
Freetrader, too, and his scorn, as a man who
practised Protection because he believed it to
be injurious, for men who practised
Protection because they believed it to
be beneficial, was an emotion that almost
amounted to a shriek. His successor, Cameron,
is claimed as a Freetrader, and appears
to be built on the same lines. Should
that prove to be the case Australia must
still wait for the long-postponed advent of that
Freetrader who believes in his creed, who isn’t a
political worm and hypocrite, and who doesn’t
find his doctrine too dirty to be put into daily use.
All the eminent alleged Freetraders in, or lately
in, the Australian Parliament who have held
Ministerial office—the two Smiths of N.S.W.,
Joe Cook, Gould, Dry Dog Reid, M'Millan
and others—belong to one monotonous brand of
political shufflers, hypocrites and frauds. They
all existed when they were in public life by prac-
tising Protection, by denying that they practised
it, and by scoffing with a great, frothy scoff at
the policy they stole. There isn’t, apparently, one
Freetrader in the two Houses who dares to advo-
cate Freetrade—to say that he believes in balancing
every necessary customs duty with an equal
excise on the local product so that the local
industry may enjoy the blessings of free competi-
tion, and that he opposes the granting of any
subsidies, aids, grants or bonuses, in the shape of
differential railway rates or otherwise, to any
local industry, because such hot-house coddling is
injurious. Taking their individual records there
doesn t seem to be one honest or sincere individual
among all the gang of dodgy political shams who
make up the alleged Freetrade party in the two
Federal Houses. Still, if Norman Cameron is
really the Freetrader he is alleged to be, there is
time for him to be the one honest exception,
ouch an exception would be an oasis in a desert of
drive l , and a refreshing streak of truth in a blatant
land of lies. But if Norman Cameron is an honest
exception the shock may be too much for some of
his associates, and it will probably make G-. H.
Reid, and Bruce Smith, and Bruce’s political
brother Pumpkin, and Local Preacher Cook all fall
down dead together.
The Doctor and His Cash.
N.S.W. Branch of the British Medical Association
has now declared open war against the Balmain
(Sydney) Friendly Societies’Dispensary, and the
doctors’ trades-union goes on strike against the
sweating prices offered by that institution. The
dispute will not go to the Arbitration Court
because the doctors, whilst sensible enough to
form a union, are not sensible enough to register
it under the Act and seek the aid of the law to
enforce a fair thing between medical man and
patient. Instead, they rely upon the boycott.
Not only will trades-union doctors refuse to work
for the sweating Balmain dispensary, but they
will boycott, socially and professionally, the
doctor who does, and treat him or her as a person
of no account and as a “ blackleg.” The method
is crude —carpenters, coal-miners, sailors, wharf-
laborers and the like have, in N.S.W., won to a
more civilised and effective system than that; but,
crude as it is, The Bulletin cordially hopes that
it will be effective. There can be nothing more
dangerous, both to a profession which is worthy of
all possible consideration and to the general
public, than medical sweating. It inevitably
means a lower grade of doctor in a very
short while, and that means a higher death-rate.
There is some difference of opinion as to the
efficacy of good doctors to prevent people dying ;
there is none at all as to the power of bad doctors
to hurry people to their graves. And the cheap,
sweated doctor, the Chinese coolie sort of person
in the medical profession, who is willing to work
long hours for low wages, must necessarily become,
if he is not already, a bad doctor. The medical
union, in regard to the Balmain case, holds out for a
fee of 16s. per member per annum; the Dispensary
wishes to pay something less than half that. By
comparison with other places it can be seen that
the medical demand is quite reasonable. In
the Newcastle district the union coal-miners,
who like to get decent wages, for themselves and
are too sensible and too consistent to wish to pay
anything but a decent rate to their doctors, pay
for medical attendance 265. per annum per family.
That is accepted as a good rate by the doctors.
At Balmain they only ask 16s. and are offered
something like 6s. If the Balmain people succeed
in defeating the striking doctors, and secure men
at their own rate, they will be paying about a
quarter of what is reckoned a fair thing in the
Newcastle district; and inevitably, in the long
run, they will get just as much as they pay for—
that is to say, a quarter or so of the effort which
the Newcastle district lodge doctor puts forth to
save his patient. And they won’t deserve any
more.

That Birth Hate.
N.S.W. Commission on the fall in the birth-rate
has issued a report which contains some good and
some rather foolish recommendations. It did not
take the Commission long to make up its mind
that the decrease in the birth-rate is mainly due
to artificial prevention ; and it is not at all hesi-
tant in absolutely condemning as “ immoral ” any
artificial check on the rate of population. In that
it does not represent the feeling of the community,
probably does not-represent even its own feeling.
To condemn “race suicide” is to stand in an un-
challengeable position. There is no question
about the evil to-the nation of married couples
deciding to have no children, or to limit their
progeny to one or two. Three children from
each married couple are required to keep
the population from actual decrease, and the
family should therefore average something beyond
that—a good deal beyond in a new country
like this. But that is a very different proposition
to a sweeping statement that those in wedlock
should devote themselves to the production of the
greatest number of children possible. Such a
programme will certainly not find public accept-
ance, however well it may plead theological
sanction or argue a moral basis. And, in matters
of State-craft, what isn’t possible need not be
discussed. Similarly the Commission suggests
the impossible when it proposes legislation to
stop the importation and sale of articles used for
checking conception. The very wide variety of
such articles makes any system of prohibition
hopeless—and the prohibition idea cannot be
considered likely of adoption in view of the fact
that nearly 90 per cent, of the population at
present more or less openly favor Neo-Mal-
thusianism. Outside of the impossible, the
Commission is only able to urge, in the way of
remedy for the small birth-rate, that the religious
bodies should unite in an effort to put
down the practice of Neo-Malthusianism. That
also is not a very promising expedient, since
one church —the second in the community in
point of numbers—has carried on a consistent
campaign in that direction for years, using means
not available to other religious bodies, and
has failed, as regards its Australian adherents, to
make any great impression. There is more hope
in an appeal to the national sentiment—in an
awakening of citizens to the danger to Australia
of carrying Malthusian ideas to excess ; that this
is, in some degree, a reasonable hope is shown by
the fact that since the recent public agitation on
the subject there is said to be some signs of an
improvement. On much surer ground is the Com-
mission when it treats of means to save a greater
proportion of the children who are born. There
is far too large a death-rate among women in
child-birth and of children at birth. That could
be remedied by better training of maternity
nurses (only 10 per cent, of midwives now prac-
tising in the State are properly qualified), and by
increasing the accommodation at lying-in hos-
pitals. Certain diseases prejudicially affect the
birth-rate by causing sterility ; increased hospital
accommodation would do something to remedy
that. Factory and shop and office managers often
damage the health of female employees by the
defective sanitary arrangements of the work pre-
mises ; hence troubles which tend to sterility.
The reforming spirit could do something there.
The “primary producer” is not so inclined to
check the birth-rate as the city worker, because
in the agricultural industries the child rapidly
becomes a source of profit. Therefore encourage
the people to go on the land. So far the Com-
mission’s recommendations go, and they are, on
the whole, good. They might have gone much
farther and been better. They might, for in-
stance, have proposed adequate foundling-hospital
accommodation to prevent a huge waste of child
life ; and they might have said something per-
tinent on the text of those advertisements in the
daily papers wherein the “primary producer”
advertises for a married couple to come and work
on the land “ without encumbrance,” and have
condemned the selfishness of the landlords of
tne state who not only lock up gigantic
areas against settlement, but insist on their
few employees being either unmarried or child-
r S 'm 0n whole, the commission was
of a Tory tone. It had a tinge of Liberalism in
such members as Dr. J. B. Nash and Mr. Hol-
man, M.P., but the weight of numbers was with
the Conservatives, and nothing was said offensive
to the upper classes—not even was there men-
tioned the very obvious fact that it is these
upper, classes ” who have set the fashion in
prevention, and are still its most resolute devotees.

Some more sorrowful news about the re'drganisa-
tion of the British army arrives by cable. In
addition to appointing the useless Duke of
Connaught to run the show, the army corps,
established during some previous “reforms,” are
to be done away with ; the country is to be divided
into administrative districts, each under a majah-
general; five generals are to command the different
districts; and the Accountant-General’s office is
to be abolished. It is all the same old kind of re-
organisation—a process of deciding that there
shall be four buttons instead of three, and that
the coat-tail which used to be green shall
now strike consternation into the enemy by
being brown. The worst dry-rot of British
militarism lies in the fact that no man can
j n officer in that great dandified club
called the British army unless he possesses
great wealth ; that the worst noodle with money
may stay in the charmed circle, while the greatest
military genius without money must stay out; and
that the army has to be largely officered by fools
because, among the small handful of eligibles, there
are not nearly enougli men who are not fools.
Everybody knows this, but to touch that aspect of
the case would be sacrilege. It would be throw-
ing dead rats at the.blessed Ark of the Covenant
and putting, the Urim and Thummim under the
pump. So in order that all may be well next war
the “reform ” commission has decided to abolish
the Accountant-General and amalgamate him
with the Paymaster—which will doubtless break
the enemy up. *

A statement with some foundation in fact:—
One ex-member of Parliament, who represented a city
constituency, stated that he could not afford to go into
Parliament again unless he got £lOOO per annum—to
satisfy the begging propensities of his constituents.
But if the State or Commonwealth increases his
salary it must be distinctly understood that it
does not do so to enable him to further satisfy the
begging propensities of his constituents. The
begging constituent has undermined and almost
destroyed the democratic principle of payment of
members ; because of him the nominally paid
member is almost as unpaid as he used to be. In
fact, the electoral cadger has brought matters to
such a pass that a member is now, to all intents
and purposes, a legislator who gets nothing for
himself, but is allowed £3OO a year with which to
bribe supporters, and people who profess to be
supporters. Some day, to prevent the principle
of payment of members being killed dead, it will
be necessary to enact that for a member to give
anything to a constituent shall be bribery, and for
a constituent to ask for anything shall be black-
mail. In the good times coming begging con-
stituents will get three years in gaol.
