A number of inter-state employers have met in
Sydney and resolved that the Deakin Govern-
ment’s poor, harmless little Arbitration Bill is
“ an encroachment upon State rights and a breach
of faith with the founders of Australian Federa-
tion also, that it will “prove unworkable and
ultra vires under our Constitution.” Well, what
are the inter-state employers worrying about ? If
they mean to express no confidence in the Federal
High Court, and don’t believe that that Court is
capable of saying when a thing is ultra vires or
not, why don’t they say so straight out? If, on
the other hand, they have confidence in the Court,
and are so sure that Deakin’s measure is
ultra vires, then it won’t matter whether the
Parliament passes it or not—if it is ultra vires it
will be fired out. If it isn’t ultra vires, then the
inter-state employers are merely barking up the
wrong tree, and should not be taken any notice
of. Who “the founders of Australian Federa-
tion” are with whom the Bill is “a breach of
faith” the. conference omits to say. The Bulle-
tin’s own idea is that the Australian people were
the founders of Federation, and a Parliament
fresh from the same people is a good deal more
likely to know what that people’s wishes and
intentions were and are than a number of mostly
unknown gentlemen who represent nothing but
their own banking accounts, and who meet at a
pub. in Pitt-street.
,
C. De Thierry, in the United Service Maga-
zine :
To hear the Cobdenite talk one might imagine it was
positively wicked for a British colony to develop manu-
factures instead of buying goods from Great Britain.
When he waxes eloquent on the sacrifices entailed by
Protection he forgets that they are small compared with
the sacrifices entailed by Freetrade. Had it become
universal, as Cobden hoped, the world would have been
tributory to England, which would be fatal to both. For-
tunately the nations saw that cheap goods would be dear
at the price, and the colonies are walking in their steps.
Canada, as an agricultural country, was weak, poor and
disunited. Under Protection she has developed produc-
tive power, until she is now strong, prosperous and
united. Freetrade would make the colonies British
farms, which would be profitable to England at the ex-
pense of colonial strength. They could cherish no ideals
as a nation ; socially, politically and intellectually they
would always be backward ; they could never be wealthy.
To develop productive powers a people must be manu-
facturers as well as agriculturists, for on the harmony
which exists between these and commerce, national pros-
perity depends, and to infant industries a tariff is
essential.
The Dry Dog lie is that Protection puts
duties into the pockets of a few bloated manufac-
turers, while they sweat their miserable employees
horribly. What follows is the boot trade journal’s
statement of the average weekly wage paid to
N.S. W. operatives in the boot industryin 1902, with
the figures for 1900 for comparison; 1900 was the
year before the moderately-protective Common-
wealth tariff came into force, and 1902 was the
year after that had happened

Something seems to have gone wrong with the
Foreign-trader’s theory.
The Waking 1 of Asia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Meredith Townsend, in his new book, “Asia and
Europe,” makes these ominous statements :—“ There are
not ten thousand natives in India who, unpaid and un-
coerced, would die in defence of British sovereignty.-. .
Beneath the small film of white men who make up the
1 Indian Empire,’ boils or sleeps away a sea of dark men,
incurably hostile, who await with patience the day when
the ice shall break and the ocean regain its power of rest-
less movement under its own laws. . . The insurrection
will occur within a month of our sustaining any defeat
whatever, severe enough to be recognised as a defeat in the
Indian bazaars. India will fly in pieces.” The burden of the
book is that the dark millions in the Ancient Continent are
determined that Asia shall be for the Asiatics. Britain,
which has sat so long, like a fond and careful hen, on the
Japanese egg, will probably discover, one of these days,
that she has hatched a cockatrice. The victory of the
Japanese—if they are victorious—will be a blow to
the prestige of the White Race in Asia. The unnatural
alliance of England with Oeishaland has blinded the Aus-
tralian daily papers to this fact. Or, if they see it, they
ignore it. Whom the gods wish to destroy, etc.
Starting from his slumber deep,
On divan,
Shall old Shem to battle leap—
All old Shem that was asleep—
When the squadrons westward sweep
From Japan.
Do tl le steeds that graze in green
Khorasan
Scent the far-off fight ? I ween
There are riders fierce and lean
Who would emulate the keen
Parthian.
Comes the day when we may see
The Pathan
With the Ghoorka grim agree,
And the smooth-tongued Bengalee
Join the Sikh and Afridee
Fighting man.
Followers of Siva and
Hanuman,
With Mahommedans may stand,
Side by side, and sweep their land
Bare of British as your hand—
Thank Japan !
Hard of heart, and cool of brain,
Genghiz Khan
Over Shem once more may reign,
And the towers of Tamerlane
Built of heads arise amain—
This is what the West may gain
From Japan.
Better ’twould have been had we
Left Japan
Europe’s Paradise to be,
With her Yoshiwara free,
And her geishas, and her wee
Honorable pots of tea,
And her fan.

*
The gloomy and snow-bound “Resur-
rection ” will eease from happening at Her
Majesty’s on Friday night. Seeing that relations
are already a trifle strained between Russia and
Britain, this resolve to cease casting reflections on
the gaol system of the Bear country is just? as
well. On Saturday night the “Sign of the Cross
will Vie revived for one occasion only, the published
reason being the management’s desire to let
Sydney have one glimpse of Maud Jeffries as the
white-robed girl who fled round the drawing-room
of Marcus Superbus carrying with her some
timber as a moral support, and who after-
wards had her bones publicly picked by
Nero’s lion. On Thursday, the popular Pete
Hughes is to have a matinee, benefit at
Her Majesty’s under promising circumstances.
Bits of “Florodora,” “Romeo and Juliet,”
“Henry V.,” “Monsieur Beaucaire,” “Torea-
dor,” “Chinese Honeymoon.” &c., are promised,
and the proceedings would be complete if there
was added an extract, lasting one minute, from
the “Face at the Window.” All that is wanted
is the window, the face, and the appalling shriek.
With prompt stage management these essentials
of Anderson’s drama could be done inside 15
Seconds, the shriek being done as the curtain
went down.
* sfc * *
The cinematograph continues to trail a
dark shadow of suspicion across a virtuous d rawing-
room at Sydney .Criterion; and by this time
Gregan M’Mahon, who is supposed to own the
drawing-room, probably wishes l.e hadn’t changed
his mind and left the law. Really, though “the
Lady of Ostend” gives her name to Hawtrey’s
piece, there isn’t any visible reason why she should
turn up until the last minute or two; as a matter
of fact, she only turns up once before that, when
she appears in M’Mahon’s previously mentioned
drawing-room, though, as M‘Mali on isn’t there at
the time, it isn’t explained what she came for.
Probably Burnand wanted to teach the Great
Moral Lesson that, though your sin may find you
out once, she’ll be pretty certain to catch
you at home sooner or later. Whether
she was intended for a great moral force
or not, the Lady is doing very good business, and
will probably remain as W. F. Hawtrey’s attrac-
tion until the approaching pig, pug and pumpkin
exhibition brings a new lot of visitors to town.
* *- -*■ *
The Musical Comedy Co. closes a success-
ful season at Sydney Royal on Saturday, and goes
on to Maorilan'd. “San Toy” will fill the bill
until Saturday, when a special mixed programme
will be presented. Subsequently the Royal will
rest in silence until the close of the month. Then,
if report speaks truly. Bland Holt arrives with
much elaborate scenery and sensational melo-
drama—also with the forsred cheque, the unjustly
suspected hero, the baffled villain, the railway
accident, and the aged gentleman who was mur-
dered in the first act, just as he was about to-
restore his son to his old place in his will and
abandon bis foolish idea of leaving everything to-
the person with the scoff and the cigarette.
** # *
“The Best Must Win ” retires on Satur-
day night from Sydney Lyceum to make room for
“The Face at the Window,” which will keep up
the long established reputation of this house for
providing the latest curdle in horrors and the
newest thing in nerve shakes. The “Face” isn’t
new to Sydney ; that horrifying countenance had
a long run here a year or two ago. It was very
popular then, and it used to open itself just before
each crime and give a howl that is remembered
even now.
** * *
The Tivoli, last week, scored 213 not out
with a fair cinematograph film showing Warner
winning the toss from Noble and the start of the
Test Mitch; it was put on while the game was
alleged to be in progress, and temporarily
tilled the void left by Vulcana’s vanished
shoulders. Now that the cricket fever
is over, Clarke and Hamilton are bearing
the heat and burden of. the play, and with their
- remarkably slick “ business,” on top of Olarke s
brilliant piano-juggling, they are bearing it with
much success. The Dumonds are still there;
so is “Johnny” Johnston. Frances Leslie, the
soprano soloist, is back ; and Fanny Powers and
others too numerous to mention are doing the
things that are expected of them—most of them,
just now, being things with music in them.
*
About 3000 disappointed applicants for
a admission were turned away from Melbourne
Princess’s last Saturday. They had been drawn
; to the top of Bourke-st. by the plain and colored
announcements of a Laughing Piece —a Roaring
' Success—a Prolonged Shriek And although “A
v Stranger in a Strange Land ” is merely a broad,
• hollow farce, with no striking peculiarity about it,
nothing brilliant to say for itself, the
Melbourne rushed it on the first night is
a partial guarantee of prosperity for the brief
Willoughby-Geach season. When the multitude
in general feels tempted to go to a particular
place with a vague, uncertain Shriek in view, the
chances are that most of the multitude will get
there, and some of them will Roar joyfully. I lie
present scribe, when he got there, felt a terrible
inclination to Roar —and lash his tail and claw
the air. * *
* * *
“ A Stranger in a Strange Land ” appeals
only to easily-amused persons of both sexes. The
hero is a plump young Englishman who dissipates
his aunt’s money in New York whilst beguiling
his distant relative with tales of his cattle ranche
at Buffalo City, where the wild Indians are ever
hunting for his scalp. Being about to return
to England for some more of aunt’s capital,
this incredible liar is advised, by a benevolent
uncle, to bring an Indian with him for the sake of
appearances, but uncle’s suggestion doesn t reach
him in time. When the wanderer returns,
clothed as a circus cow-boy, he persuades his
sister’s young man to impersonate the harmless
necessary savage in a striped blanket. Also the
uncle, not knowing that a bogus savage is avail-
able, hires a real Indian from a Yankee quack
doctor, who employs the Cherokee chief as an
advt. for a kidney cure. The duplicate figures in
red ochre and blankets account for the usual med-
ley of misunderstandings, after which an idiot
detective from Scotland Yard disguises himself as
another Indian in a third blanket. Then uncle goes
mad and mixes a dose of rough on rats for his own
destruction, but the real Indian caps his career of
petty larceny by swallowing the poison. Here is
a humorous opportunity for the farce to conclude.
The surviving members of the Willoughby co.
might fall prostrate and silent on the Cherokee’s
corpse, instead of which the stomach pump is
applied, and all ends tamely.
* * * *
An extravagant fiction of this sort comes
hardest on the ladies of the co., who are not even
supposed to be trying to deceive anybody. One
of them has to embrace the wrong Indian in
mistake for her husband. The men are largely
•engaged in kicking the wrong Indian, and some-
times six people are required to chase one another
in the interests of public amusement. Willoughby,
Tom Cannam, Lester Hanray, and one “ Mer-
man” M'Keon (on the bill) boar the heat and
burden of the chase. Miss Barton is a handsome,
sceptical heiress from America; there are a few
chances for Miss Watts-Phillips as the financial
aunt, and a couple of confiding girl characters
help to carry out the author’s crime. No less
than two au'hors put their names to “A
Stranger,” and the work of their combined
intellects is staged in a most respectful manner at
the Princess’s.
#* * #
In the uncommonness of its structure,
“The Flood Tide,” by Cecil Raleigh, is even
more interesting than “The Price of Peace,”
which was also a drama of episodes where the
story changed its objects of sympathy and atten-
tion in a fitful manner. Bland Holt’s first pro-
duction of the “ melo-farce ” at Melbourne Royal
showed any number of openings for improve-
ment in the acting and _ stage pictures,
nevertheless it put the merits of the play
beyond question. In the course of thirteen
scenes distributed among four acts the leading
characters are set to work with the smallest pos-
sible expenditure of make-weight dialogue.
Their words and actions are for the most part
vital to the incidents in the story, and if this fact
is not noted at the moment, or subsequently
remarked, the fault is with the performers. In
short, “The Flood Tide” makes exceptional
demands upon the intelligence of _ a company
whose ordinary tasks don’t worry its brains at
all. The performance must continue to improve,
as a matter of course, long after the scenery and
supers have settled down into their grooves.
* * * *
Bland Holt has a difficult light-low-comedy
part to play in direct contrast to the light tragedy
parts of Miss Ross and Miss Ireland. Mrs. Holt,
as the amiable accomplice of Bland in a gorgeous
fraud, gets the most difficult, but the most
pleasing part in the drama, and promises to make
her best up to date when she has dropped the
habit of dropping her aitches, and discarded the
mannerisms that belong to her familiar cockney-
gal characters. This time she is the daughter of a
North of England man, and the sister of a mili-
tary captain. She has seen the world _as
stewardess on an ocean steamer, and in the time
of trouble she engineers a scheme for pulling her
family out of the mud. Walter Baker shines as a
self-made captain, persecuted by a mob of beastly
superior bwother officahs, but his fine manly style
doesn’t excuse his going off for his honeymoon
(with the Colonel’s daughter) in full regimentals.
Styan, the drunken Diamond King of the story,
cuts a quaint jim-jams figure with his boots
slung across his shoulders and his mind dominated
by yearnings for the refreshment bar. And there
are others who fill in forty or fifty minor charac-
ters incidental to the 13 scenes of Brunton. Some
remarks on the story of “ The Flood Tide ” are
reserved for next issue. In the ordinary sense of
the word it is not a story so much as a series of
connected dramatic situations and striking sug-
gestions.
* * *
At Her Majesty’s there will be one more
week of “ A Country Girl.” On Saturday night
the new baritone, Haigh Jackson, put in a first
appearance—a rather stiff and amateurish
stranger, who promises to be a satisfactory
sort of hero when he has gained con-
fidence and been grounded in the rudiments
of dancing. Harold Thorley at the outset
of his Australian engagement, was about as
amateurish as his successor. Haigh Jackson has
a confident warble, anyhow. His full-toned voice
was much more expressive than his face and legs
on Saturday night, and, although he sang dull
music at a rather funereal pace, he was effective.
*% * *
At the Opera House, the latest arrival is
Mrs. Sims Reeves, recently of Sydney. Other-
wise the programme remains much the same as
before, except that Whitburn has gone to the
trouble of learning a new song, or an unfamiliar
one, which seems to issue humorously from weary
William’s throat. On the rare occasions when he
adds a new item to his venerable repertoire, the
question re-arises—Why is Whitburn funny in
Melbourne ? Tradition states that the languid
local identity isn’t especially droll elsewhere.

A Williamsonian theatrical carnival, in-
cluding a ladies’ cricket match, is to happen at the
East Melbourne Ground on Wednesday, 16th,
under the baton of conductor George Lauri.
* 3? $ *
Adelaide—which, in spite of a certain
reputation for other-worldliness, yet reckons to
know a good thing when it sees one—has filled
the Tivoli to the roof during Yulcana’s season.
Atlas is much like other strong men, but the
lady’s muscles are something quite novel, and her
back is a poem. Rickards himself, arriving two
nights late, at once began to sing about the Anglo-
Saxon lingo with much vigor. Irving Sayles
has the “record” humorless comic song. It
tells of a negro who was asked to spell
“chicken”; and he spelt it, quite correctly,
and nothing else happened, and that’s all! From
the Royal the panto, has gone, with its moderate
splendor, and Isabel Webster’s voice, and the
funniments of a string of hard-working principals,
and its other assets. The Majeronis have come
along with quick-change dramas of horse-racing
and jealousy and other national vices. Manager
Pollock announces a long winter of attractions,
including all the new plays, and one or two which
Adelaide is to see before the Eastern cities get a
chance at them.

show what progress the usual State deficit is making. In
most cases it will be found to be a healthy youngster, upon
whom “the break-up of the drought” and “the return
of prosperity ” haven’t jiad any very obvious effect. Cer-
tainly, the first lot of figures look better

The Westralian official figures are not yet available, and
those given above are subject to revision. What, how-
ever, can l>e said with certainty about the Westralian
situation is that, in spite of the starving of railway
maintenance, the surplus of £231,659 with which the year
started has been already reduced to £183,546. The
Victorian figures for 1902-3 are always under suspicion ;
shameful political dodges were worked in that
vear, and how far they went it is impossible to say.
If, instead of comparing the past eight months’ figures
with those for the like period of 1902-3, they are com-
pared with 1901-2, a shrinkage of £53,222, instead of an
increase of £175,039, is disclosed. Queensland also,
instead of being £48,674 better, would on the same
basis, show up £154,446 worse, South Australia £2665
worse, and Westralia £42,465 worse. N.S. Wales, on the
other hand, has had in the eight months £282,576 more
revenue than it had in the same part of 1901-2, and still is
in a hole, and busy scraping up odd pounds from anyone
who will lend. The N.S.W. Government talks about the
shockingly bad luck it has bad, and the frightful period
of depression through which it has battled. The revenue
returns for the first eight months of the past four years
throw scorn at that contention

Prime Minister Deakin is just now investiga-
ting the bona fides of a recently-registered London co.
called the Papuan Native Industries Ltd., which is under-
stood to have been more or less generated and formed by
the London Missionary Society for the purpose of “ edu-
cating the New Guinea natives to become producers, and
to make a profit by exporting their products to Austra-
lia.” The co. that is floated in a moral atmosphere, and
which poses as being more philanthropic than profit-
seeking, is always worth careful inspection.

There still remain £166,530 of deposits, against which

amount stated. As, however, the land tax paid in 1903
amounted to only £lllos., it doesn’t look as though the
real value exceeded £3OOO. Still, whatever it is, there
certainly seems no reason for prolonging the agony, as
expenses are mopping up the few assets that are left.
The whole capital' (£57,058) is, of course, too much lost to
be worth mentioning.

“Via”: The new regulations of the Federal
Government as to compulsory assurance of all Federal
civil servants raises some trouble. It is ukased that
each servant shall insure (up to the limits of lus classi-
fication) for a sum equal to his annual salary, payable at
the age of 60. (His retiring age is 65.) What happens to
the lively corpse that no respectable co. will touch Or
the one aged 50 that is loaded 10 years? will the
Australian Government throw out the live dead-men .
Writer can name a score who have been refused by every
reputable co. in Queensland, and yet they do their work
and earn their salaries. In M.L. the State takes the job
on, and, using a sliding scale, the plan seems to work
well, and the fund is quite solvent. Perhaps Deakin and
co. are feeling the way for a State Life Insurance
Department. , » *

The Wilfley Concentrator has now reached a
very exalted position in the concentration industry. It
had long been recognised that older types of concentra-
tors were unsatisfactory in many respects, being small in
capacity, intricate in construction, costly in maintenance,
difficult of adjustment and required constant super-
vision ; but the “ Wilfley,” while retaining all advantages
that may have been possessed by its predecessors, was
superior to them in the following important respects.
It will handle several times more material than any belt
concentrator made, without exception. It will save a
higher percentage of the values than any other table, and
will catch anj' float gold, amalgam, or quick-silver which
may escape from the amalgamating plates. It will make
cleaner and more valuable concentrates, thus saving use-
less expense for transportation of valueless material, and
also reducing the smelter charges. It requires little or
no attention when once adjusted, and no expert is re-
quired, either to adjust or run the machine. It handles
the pulp with less power and less floor space per ton than
any other concentrator. It has no expensive belts to
crack and wear out and no complicated machinery to
keep in repair. The Sole Agents and Manufacturers for
Australia are The Austral Otis Engineering Company,
Limited, South Melbourne.*

* « * *
British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Loekingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to36inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

At the end of the half-year deposits amounted to
£15,525,725 (£264,431 more than 12 months previously),
notes £471,023 (£17,208 more), bills etc., £1,841,505
(£179,785 more), liquid assets £5,456,362 (£1,281,732 less),
drafts in transit, etc., £1,775,787 (£661,216 more), advances,
etc. £12,497,141 (£1,117,495 more). These figures, show-
ing a heavy reduction of liquid assets (especially in the
matter of specie on hand and cash balances) and a large
increase in advances, account for the improved profit.
Liquid assets, nevertheless, still equal 36 per cent, of
liabilities to the public.

* • • *
Among the other prospects that lie ahead of
New South Wales is one regarding a very large and very
disastrous slump in an important branch of the revenue
that derived from conditional purchases. The slump,
in fact, showed itself last year, but that was only the
beginning, and much worse things are to come. Con-
ditional purchases still yield N.S.W. a revenue of over
£1,000,000 a year, and the fact that this income is
threatened with something not far short of extinction is
a serious matter. The position for ten years to 31st
December, 1902, was as follows :
Revenue from Amount still
Conditional due on Conditional
Purchases Purchases
during year. at end of year.
1893 .. £1,047,529 £14,609,500
1894 .. 1,052,198 13,608,038
1895 .. 1,082,692 13,187,636
1896 .. 1,075,331 12,381,760
1897 ... 1,102,014 11,624,855
1898 .. 1.152,356 11,169,757
1899 .. 1,125,470 10,738,045
1900 .. 1,214,628 10,305,902
1901 .. 1,219,384 9,922,411
1902 .. 1,066,500 9,298,367
This shows a very serious state of things. The new con-
ditional purchases taken up don’t by any means counter-
balance the old ones paid off, so since 1893 the amount
B tiH to be collected has diminished by considerably over
£5,000,000. Probably, as a multitude of conditional pur-
chases are nearly paid off, it will take a very short time
for the income to drop from the present £1,066,500 to
£500,000 a year. It will probably get down, before very
many years, to £200,000 a year. And there is no increase
in any other branch of land revenue to make up for this.
N.S.W. has collected over £26,000,000 from conditional
purchasers, and its total land revenue of one kind and
another has been over £50,000,000. This has all been spent
as revenue, and there is nothing to show for it. Even
now N.S.W. doesn’t have much success at making ends
meet; how it will get on in the near future when one of
its biggest props gives way under it, is hard to sav.

. ® ee Government’s habit of borrowing
in all directions small and large sums repayable at short
dates is bearing fruit. Within the next four years
N.S.W must needs borrow £9,000,000 for renewal pur-
poses alone, and if it spends £2,000,000 a year of loan
mon ®J'> 11 will have to find £15,000,000—a trifle of
say £4,000,000 a year. And on present appearances the
man who can raise that amount at anything like reason-
able interest could raise the dead.

An experimental lot of 1000 tons of mangrove
bark is being obtained in North Queensland. Unfortun-
ately, the Private Enterprise which is running the
business is of the hungry sort, and, whereas African bark
is saleable in Europe at £5 or £6 a ton, only £2 2s. 6d. is
being offered in N.Q. A sample parcel of 25 tons was
lately tested with good results at Hamburg ; it is under-
that >t was upon these tests that the syndicate
vi hich now has the business in hand floated into existence,
ihe stuff loused for tanning.

co . ncer n shows still another loss. The
» s revenue is only sufficient to pay 3 per cent, on the
What investment it is that has increased in value is not
stated ; and, since the writing- up of assets has been
brought into discredit by societies which don’t occupy
the standing of the City Mutual, it is just as well, when
reputable societies adopt the same plan, to give
the most ample particulars regarding it. Three years ago
this society began to pay higher bonuses than it had
been doing ; and, if this Were a less reputable society, it
might be suspected that this writing-up of assets was a
mere trick designed to cover a considerable shrinkage
in the sum available for adding to the funds, and to thus-
give some color of justification for the maintenance of
an excessive bonus. Certainly it came in a year when
the income showed the smallest increase for ten years,
and when, but for the writing up, the addition to the
funds would have been the smallest addition (bar that of
1900) since 1896. In any case, if there has actually been
an increase in the value of investments, it would be far
better to put the amount to a reserve fund to meet con-
tingencies, instead of making it available to swell
bonuses. Big bonuses are bait for new business ; but
there is such a thing as using too much bait to'
make the fishing profitable, and the tendency
in mutual life offices is nearly always that
way. That there is need for a reserve fund
the society’s own experience proves; for down to
1900 it had a securities reserve fund, and then apparently
found it necessary to write the amount off—anyway, it
disappeared. Of the City Mutual's total funds of
£254,713, all but £22,458 (Government securities) is repre-
sented by mortgages, properties, &c. ; and, as it is pretty
certain that land values have been hitherto inflated by
State borrowings which must one day stop, the prudent
assurance co. which is up to its neck in land investments
will prepare for a possible slump, and certainly won’t,
when it happens to benefit by a small rise in values, hurry
to use it all up for bait.

The expense rate has been brought down a little
lower: —
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1893 .. £27 15 11 percent.
1894 .. 35 9 5 „ „
1895 .. 29 8 3 „ „
1896 .. 25 10 0 „ „
1897.. 22 18 9 „ „
1898 .. 22 10 11 „ „
1899 .. £2l 18 4 per cent.
1900 .. 29 311 „ „
1901 .. 25 16 2 „ „
1902 .. 23 3 2 „ „
1903 .. 22 3 9 „
This is still high—much too high. And then the bonus-
bait must be kept in mind. A society which aims at
paying high bonuses (the directors say that their object is'
“ to make the City Mutual the most prosperous and
largest bonus-paying institution ") ought not to have a.
high expense rate; the high bonuses should serve the
same purpose as the canvasser.
Interest earnings (.£13,436) are still excellent,
amounting to no less than £5 9s. per cent, on the average
funds. This is a full £1 above the Australian average
—and the Australian average is higher than that of
either the U.K. or the U.S. The difference between the
Oity Mutual rate and the average rate pays about 20 per
cent, of the whole expenses. As against that, there is
always the probability of high interest earnings and
doubtful security going together ; which makes it all the
more necessary to start that securities reserve fund
spoken of just now.

Last week The Bulletin said that, as far as it
knew, the City Mutual did not have a single law-suit on
hand just now, Crowley v. Glissan having been settled.
And now it hears that fresh litigation has already broken
out over the same case.

The year’s loss on business has been reduced by the
recovery of £313 previously written off as bad. The
£38,798 nominal capital intact is very nominal ; a loss is
shown in spite of the recovery just mentioned, and not-
withstanding that the £71,686 of deposits are carrying
no interest. The properties in possession and bor-
rowers’ and purchasers’ balances, which are down in the
balance sheet for £114,531, gave a return of only £7lB, or
about 12s. 6d. per cent. On a 4 per cent, basis their
capital value would be £17,950 ; and that would leave the
depositors with poor prospects and the shareholders with
none at all. Nevertheless, some progress is being made
in reducing liabilities ; the total has moved as follows :

Of these liabilities, as before mentioned, £71,686 is
represented by non-interest-bearing deposits ; and the
concern which cannot make progress when it has to pay
no interest to its depositors is in about as bad a way as a
concern can be.
* * * «
The Rockhampton (Q.) Gas and Coke Co. finds
itself in the bog prepared for the co. which goes on paying
dividends in excess of earnings year after year, and then,
when all its reserves are gone, remembers that it has
forgotten to write off any thing substantial for deprecia-
tion. Not only has the eo. been running an electric light
department at a loss, but its preference shares are en-
titled to a 9 per cent, dividend, and it also carries deben-
tures ; and among these three handicaps, and the con-
tinued payment of a dividend on ordinary shares, the
co. drifted into such a state that at last the auditors
tacked on to their report a remark to the effect that the
balance sheet was, in their opinion, a fake, inasmuch as
“ the gas plant, meters, buildings, fittings, electric light
machinery, mains and services, &c., are not of the value
set forth.” As a matter of fact, the co. had been too
busy trying to earn 9 per cent, dividends to even write
off the expenses of the debenture issue, which still stand
among the assets. To get out of this bog in a straight-
forward way some of the directors proposed that the
capital should be written down—the honest thing to do in
view r of the auditors’ report that a good part of it
had been lost. The bulk of the shareholders, how-
ever, have other notions about such matters, and the
motion that capital be written down was fired out
decisively in favor of a suggestion that for the future
dividends shall be limited, and the loss be written
off gradually. Even if the process of depreciation doesn’t
continue it will take, so far as can be seen, about 10
years for the depreciation which has already occurred to
be wiped out; and meantime, presumably, the co. is going
to issue balance-sheets in which electric light mains, etc.,
that are worth nothing except for old copper, are included
as if they were still new electric-light mains. There is, of
course, another way ; everything may be written down
to present value, and the shortage put boldly among the
assets as “deficiency.” The shareholders would not write
nominally intact capital (£9622); hence it is not sur-
prising that even the very modest expenses nearly
always manage to get ahead of the income. Most of
what assets are left consist of interest under a will, of a
book value of £6028 ; and over that there has been long liti-
gation, the end of which theoo. doesn’t yetsee. The other
trifling assets are also mostly entered up at book value
or at cost, which is, of course, unsatisfactory. In fact,
there isn’t much that is satisfactory about the co. except
that, so far as can be seen, its position is frankly stated
in the published accounts.
• • * *
B.H. Block 14’s zinc plant, on the acid principle,
is capable of handling 600 tons of ore a week ; after
many difficulties, it is being got into steady running
order, and already 250 tons of zinc product has been
shipped. The B.H. Prop, is busy also building new zinc
plant; it has been announced that it will go in for
magnetic treatment as well as for Delprat’s acid process.
In connection with the latter, the Potter people who
opposed its patent, have been ordered by the Victorian
court to pay the costs of the long and unsuccessful pro-
ceedings before the Commissioner. Broken Hill North
directors are anxiously watching the lead market, which
has been a bit firmer lately, and, if it keeps so, they will
make a start again after nearly three years’ stop. On
past figures, however, there seems to be no profit for this
co. in lead at anything under £l2, notwithstanding the
recent improvements in other directions.
* * * *
The often-resurrected Pinnacles, outside Broken
Hill, is more alive now than it has been for years,
having been galvanised by the mostly English co. which
owns the smelting works at Aldershot (Q.). The mill has
been re-modelled, and is running steadily, though only
on a small scale, treating something under 500 tons a
week ; but a fair amount of ore is being hand-dressed up
to a high percentage. Hitherto the mine has been
handicapped by its distance from railway (six or seven
miles), and by difficulties of getting out a reasonably
tractable concentrate. The former handicap still exists,
but. it is said that the Queensland co.’s metallurgists have
got over the other trouble. There are good ore boelies in
this and smaller mines, though from a variety of causes
the district has hitherto proved one of the most disas-
trous for investors.
» * * *
Capt. Warren definitely takes charge of Cobar
Chesney, on which he lately reported. Presumably he
goes as a concentrating expert, though his mana in that
respect isn’t what it was by a long way. And, any way,
it isn’t primarily a milling problem which is sticking up
Chesney.

Reefs, 15950 z. ; Princess Royal, 13220 z. ; Golden Ridge,

Most of the large producers publish their returns in
fine gold, which makes a comparison of their relative
outputs much easier. Costs of most of the mines have
been much reduced, but, unfortunately, some give them
per long ton and some per short ton. ' As the short ton
(20001 b.) has been adopted in most of the leading gold-
fields of the world, there is a good deal to be said for
introducing it to W.A., so as to compare on an equal
footing with the Rand. It is a horrible example of the
slipshod nature of British weights and measures that
there should be two separate weights with the one name.
* * * •
Tom Doyle, of Kanowna (W.A.), has had a
parcel of 12 tons treated from his Red Hill lease, which
brings up the total gold won in less than two years-to
39470 z. from 236 tons.
* * * *
Great Fingall Consols, Day Dawn (W.A.), has
had exemption granted on eleven of its 12 leases, on the
ground that no payable gold has been got outside of one
24-acre block. Its big area is merely a blind to satisfy
the Great British Public. The niain block is 1450 ft.
long, and the combined length of the two chutes proved
at No. 5 level is about 1500 ft., including 200 ft. of a blank.
The.reef splits at both boundaries, and has never proved
payable in any outside property either north or south.
At No. 9 level the chute pinched out 580 ft. north of the
shaft, or practically the same distance as in the levels
above. No. 10 level is being driven north and south, and
so far is quite equal to expectations. This level should
be completed in another five months. The co. is at
present fourth on the list of producers, but will shortly
take the lead, though it will not hold it long. The
Horseshoe will again be top dog when its extra 50 stamps
make a start.
* * * *
Associated, Kalgoorlie, has been very energeti-
cally worked since Gieisberg took charge, and is a very
different mine from what it was. The main shaft is on its
way down to 1600 ft., but progress is slow owing to the
hardness of the country. The 20-head mill to treat the
low-grade ores is nearly completed, and should be
running within a month. Two more Merton furnaces
have been ordered for the sulphide plant as a stand-by to
ensure a full supply of roasted ore. As most of the
capital expenditure of the company is now complete
steady divs. are assured. The only additions likely in the
near future are further stamps, as the reserves of low-
grade ore are increasing very fast. This ore runs about
Bdwt., but is expected to leave a good margin. It has
to be handled in any case, and sending to the mill will
add very little to the expense.
» * * *
W.A. mines are already declaring divs. for
March, the first being the Great Boulder, £65,625, closely
followed by the Princess Royal with £4OOO. The latter
mine has resumed payments after a lapse of three months.
Investors should not allow themselves to be led away by
these distributions, as they are practically being paid out
of capital; it is questionable, in fact, whether the
Princess will pay the 20s. per share at which the scrip is
quoted.

To show how far Kalgoorlie has outstripped all
the other W.A. goldfields, it is interesting to note that
for January, Kalgoorlie’s output is given as 118,7210z.’
while the runner up, the Murchison, comes in a very bad
second with 22,3860 z. Kalgoorlie is still forging ahead,
but, almost without exception, the other fields are
dropping behind.

A show called the Great Fingall Associated
Day Dawn (W.A.), is being hawked about on the strength
of being near the famous Great Fingall Consols. Of
course, as with the defunct Kinsella, and the much
puffed Rubicon, the Fingall reef trends through the
property ; but, as the gold chute itself exists in one
block only of the big Fingall property, the
where the lode goes is of very little moment. W.A. has
thousands of reefs and lodes, but probably not 1 per cent
of %hem carry payable gold, and even the 1 per cent"
carry it for a very short distance, very rarely exceeding a,
mile. ' ®

Queensland Menzies (W.A.) new battery made
a start on Feb. 1, and the next clean-up should show an
output of 1000 tons. The mine is looking better, and the
co. should pay several divs. during the vear. Investors
must, however, keep in mind the shortness of the chutes
and the smallness of reserves, as, once the new level is
worked out, there will again be a period of uncertainty.
The bullion of this mine has always been very poor, and is
becoming poorer at depth, and it is not safe to value it
much above £2 an ounce, including cyanide gold. Possi-
bly the return may be published in fine gold, as orders to
that effect have been issued by Government, but small
mines appear to take no notice of the order
* * « *
A mining catechism. What is a mine reserve ?
It is a hoard of gold on a mine kept by the manager!
either with or without the knowledge of the directors
and is used to average .yields. Is there anything wrong
in a mine reserve ? No, given that the shareholders know
of its existence and the extent to which it is drawn upon
iiut, if they do not know, can it be used for fraudulent
purposes ? Most easily. It can be used to manipulate
make it appear that a good return is being
obtained when the output is really low. Shareholders
and the public are thus deceived and a false market is
created, during which those in the know can slip out of
their shareholdings. But if the reserve is kept secret
from the directors? Then the co. is in the hands of
the manager and the mine-hand who countersigns
£°ld-book. .On the other hand, if the secret
of the reserve is shared between these two, the
directors and the legal manager, it gives to
these the command of the market as well as the
control of the mine. Have many mine managers grown
rich under the system? Very few; because in the
instances where they alone knew of the reserve they were
not tempted. Is the system against the shareholder?
.Decidedly; because he does not know whether the
reported yields are true or not, and so either appraises
his shares at too high or too low a value. He conse-
quently must sell or buy under false conditions. Does
such a system tend to honesty? Not so long as Stock
Exchange temptations exist, and the market is looked to
In'fu . an kh e mine. Then what is the remedy? To
tell the truth and report every ounce won. Not to do so
is to lie
* * * *
In the deepest workings of the New Chum
Railway, Bendigo, the temperatures are almost unbear-
able. Where a free circulation of air can be managed the
heat can be borne, though it affects the miners' working:
capacity ; but in other places, where ventilation is for
the time being impossible, only strong men can work at
all, and even they for not long at a stretch. Lately a
rise has been started from a crosscut 400 ft. from the
shaft at 3865 ft., the object being to connect with a winze
dropped from 3200 ft., and in the top of this rise 98
degrees were registered—9Bdeg. in an atmosphere in
which breathing soon becomes difficult. This record
has, however, been reduced to 92deg. by the use of a
spray which discharges 11,200 gallons of water per shift.
Further in, where the spray cannot be used with effect,
98 is still recorded. Lately several experiments have
been tried with a view to making the working conditions
more tolerable, but nothing has done much good. Great
blocks of ice have been lowered, but the heat of the
surrounding rock thawed them so rapidly that no appre-
ciable change was made ; nevertheless, for want of any-
thing better, this experiment is being continued in the
faint hope that the rock may be cooled off a trifle. This
depth is just short of three-quarters of a mile.

There’s an effusive Bill Adams spirit in
the cable about Japanese sailors offering- in
letters writ with .their own blood to beard the
A PALPABLE HIT.
“ My husband is so jealous!”
“ How ridiculous I” “ Isn’t yours?"
“ Certainly not!" “ How humiliating!”
Bear in his fortressed den—a spirit which does
not augur too well for the cause of Japan. If a
man is so grateful that “he would shed his heart’s
blood for you ” beware of seeking to borrow six-

Australia has produced a fair proportion
of horrors, but very few to equal that for which
the two Macdonalds, husband and wife, have jusb
been sentenced to death at Rockhampton (Q.).
Macdonald had married a second time, and for no
explainable reason—unless it might be that their
miserable victim had inherited a fragment of
property—he and his wife seem to have set them-
selves to kill off the 15-year-old daughter of the
first Mrs. Macdonald. She was sent for to their
home at Longreaeh, and in the course of seven or
eight months she was flogged, starved, and drudged
to death. The post-mortem showed that two ribs
were broken—one in two places—and had been in
that state for weeks ; an ankle and a wrist were
broken and the bones rotting away—the broken
bone of the wrist protruded through the skin; and
the body was covered with malodorous sores, ap-
parently through continued flogging, and horribly
emaciated. Apparently the pitiable wreck had
to hustle about her daily tasks almost to the
last. As an aggravation of the case the step-
mother had been trained as a nurse, and it is
reasonable to suppose, not only that she realised
the state of her victim, but that she could have-
relieved her if she had felt so inclined. Even the
widespread feeling against the execution of a
woman seems hardly likely to save hospital nurse, e
and stepmother Macdonald. Certainly she rs
likely to give a severe set-back to the hospita.
nurse craze in Rockhampton. The ministering
angel who smoothes the invalid’s pillow will
appear for a time in a new light.

Peacock, High Cockalorum of the Craft in
Victoria, hit the right nail on the head when he
stated that ’Lijah Dowie, “for reasons of his
own,” was repeating his old American lie as to
a conspiracy of the Freemasons for the overthrow
of “Zion.” The Bearded Bounder, before he
raised his Freemason cry in Melbourne, must
have been alive to the fact that his meeting at the
Exhibition building was invaded by hundreds of
forged ticket-holders whose “ briefs ” issued from
a notorious railer at the apron Brotherhood.
Then what is ’Lijah's reason for attributing all his
blessed “persecutions” to the wiles of Masonry?
Apparently the worried old schemer is bidding
wildly for the negative sympathy of the R.C.
public. As though they could possibly see any
redeeming quality in John’Lijah D.’s incoherent
new sectarianism 1 The prophet has grown no
wiser during his years of opulence. He returns
to Australia the same narrow-minded, pugnacious,
indiscreet, ridiculous impostor of a Dowie who
left 20 years ago, and if in the meantime he has
acquired an American character for cuteness and
eloquence, the explanation lies in the dreary
dulnessof his old-fashioned followers. A certain
class of slow, piously-inclined Yanks is darned
slow and almost incredibly gullible.
# * * *
Victorian Inspector-General of Prisons, in
his annual report, urges the Government to adopt
a provision, recently introduced into the English
prison system, whereby a person sentenced to
imprisonment in default of payment of a fine may
pay part of the fine and “do time” proportionate
to the remainder. The idea certainly has points
about it. Its adoption would mean increased
revenue—in plenty of cases the convicted person,
although unable to pay the full fine, could pay
part of it, and would cheerfully do so, if allowed.
This would also mean a big saving in prisoners’
maintenance,

The cable-
gram an-
nouncing
the death of
the chim-
panzee Con-
sul brings
food for re-
flection.
When God
was adding
the finishing
touches to
his Creation,
the earth
seemed in-
complete, so
he added
thereto pri-
mitive man
and hishairy
relative, the
ape. Theape
remained in
his original
condition,
but primi-
tive man
passed through the whole gamut of evolutionary
changes, developed and perfected himself, until he
reached his climax in such as Herbert Spencer.
The philosopher devoted his life to the explanation
of that evolution that had produced himself, and
lived contemporaneously with the most perfect
specimen of his one-time hairy relative, the chim-
panzee Consul. The philosopher worked under
the stress of providing his own sustenance ; the
ape had all found and a manager to attend to his
daily wants. Intellect was neglected, and caudal
appendage was courted, invited to banquets and
wrapped in luxury. The philosopher's ideal of
life was to make himself a good animal; the
animal’s ideal of life was to ape the genus to which
the philosopher belonged. Both died. The
philosopher had scraped together £16,000; the
ape was insured for £20,000.

The site of the “old” Ship Inn, which
was not old, at Circular Quay has been granted to
the Sydney Corporation by the Railway Com-
missioners at one shilling per year rental, It will
be made “ ornamental’’ until the Railway
Commissioners.require it. This “Old Ship” was
built in 1867, and named the “Galatea,” in honor
of tbemsit of the Duke of Edinburgh’s ship to
Australia. The name was retained about a year,
when it was re-named “The Ship.” The “Old
Ship ” Inn proper still stands, though not
licensed, as a monument of ancient days, at the
corner of Essex and Gloucester streets, on the
Sydney “Rocks.” In old-hand vernacular,
Essex-st. was Gallows Hill, and from the path-
way at the Ship Inn could be seen the executions
of criminals in the gaol yard below. On such
fete days as the execution of the Jew Boy’s gang
(March 16, 1841) the windows of the “Ship”
were well patronised, and the bar did a roaring
trade, the rocks in the immediate vicinity being
a place of vantage. This Ship was erected
apparently over 90 years ago. It, or something
very like it, is pictured in old plates of Sydney,
issued in 1810. William Davis, transported for
making pikes in 1798, lived and had his smithy
not far away. St. Patrick’s church occupies the
site of Davis’s old premises.
In a case under the White Australian Act
in Melbourne the other day, one of the grounds of
appeal against the conviction of a Chinese was
that English was not a “European” language
within the meaning of the Act, and the point was
argued with all the sodden gravity of an under-
done damper or of a drunk delivering an oration
in a horse-trough. There is probably nothing on
earth that that strange animal called “learned
counsel ” won’t argue for a consideration.

It seems, from the lately-
discovered remains of one of his
note-books, that Captain Sturt
constituted himself a commis-
sion to inquire into the Aus-
tralian birth-rate while he was
camped at Depot Glen, in the
heart of Australia, 60 years ago.
He found that—
The (aboriginal) mother retains
them (her children) at the breast
until they are full three years old—
a custom not confined to this part
of the country, hut one that may,
with other preventive causes, ac-
count for the limited population on
the Australian continent.
The Australian mother of 60
years later doesn’t adopt the
heroic measure mentioned by
Sturt; but she gets there just
the same.

* * * *
The Light monument committee in Ade-
laide has just voted its own extinction after being
twelve years in collecting £475. This is to be
handed to the City Corporation, which has under-
taken to add £5OO, the Government finding £lOOO
more. Colonel Light was the man who surveyed
Adelaide’s site, and, in fact, caused the capital to
stand where it does, instead of at Port Lincoln or
Encounter Bay. He is buried in Light Square, in
one corner of the city, and the small monument over
his grave is crumbling away. Unfortunately the
Square has, in the course of time, become about the
most disreputable quarter of Adelaide, and this
makes trouble. To put the hard-cadged obelisk
over the old man’s grave is like putting a
pearl in the midst of swine, and yet to leave the
grave unmarked while a big tombstone glares at
the public elsewhere seems ridiculous.
• • * *
Peter Nicoll Russell, who endowed the
Sydney University Engineering School with
£50,000, promised another £50,000 provided the
Government supplied £25,000 for buildings and
equipment, which condition has been fulfilled.
Meanwhile the magnificent Mitchell bequest of
books to the State stands legally forfeited because
of the failure of the Government to fulfil the con-
dition of providing a suitable building. There
seems to be a great difference in finding money for
the University—benefits of which are practically
confined to the well-to-do —and for the public.
The Uni. can score every time, whilst the common
public oan’t kick a goal once in ten tries.

A JEREMIAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 have been a Sundaj -school teacher for nearly 60 years.
X find the ignorance of the children of the simplest facts
in Scripture history something really appalling.— Wail of
anguish in Melbourne Argus by “ J.S.”
Oh, what is to be the fate
Of this graceless, godless State,
If the children will not wait
In Sunday-school?
Never know what Samuel saith
Of the death of Ish-bosheth?
Never know or hear of Beth-
Saida’s pool?
Ah, their ignorance is dire ;
Take the story of Machire—
Or of Meshach in the fire—
Have they heard ?
Do they know about Zedad,
And the punishment of Gad,
Or the story of Bildad ?
Not a word 1
Did the woes of Azabart
Ever stir a little heart ?
Did a tear-drop ever start
For Zeboim ?
Have they heard of Meshulin—
Or of Iddo and Hoshin ?
Sir, they answer with a grin—
“ We don’ know ’im !"
If you ask of Melicu,
Or of Ezra and Ziklu,
They will simply stare at you—
Or at their hats ;
If you speak of Amariah,
Shecaniah, Jedadiah,
Nehemiah, or Isaiah—
They say “ Rats! ”
Sir, I shudder as I write 1
They can’t tell a Jebusite
From a wicked Beerothite
(See Samuel two).
They know nothing of the Acts—
They are weak in Scripture facts
So the outlook in these traots
Is pretty blue 1
vie.
O C. CABOT.
IN HEAVEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Phyllis sings at eventide to me
Dark birds of care unfold their heavy wings,
And fly far out of sight—beyond the sea
When Phyllis sings.
The smile, the touch, the glisten of her rings,
The soft, sweet light, the sweeter melody—
And then my lot is happier than a king’s.
In town the lumbering day goes heavily,
But through its length I bless the hour that brings
My heart in Heaven where it fain would be
When Phyllis sings.
ILL.

PROPRIETY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What is Proprietee ?
Who is the beast, and why ?
I’ faith, a mighty boon ’twould be
If old, morose Propriety
Would go away and die !
Where is Proprietee?—
Where does the spectre live
That ever chases heartlessly
Across the Plains of Misery
Some hapless fugitive ?
How is Proprietee ?
Which is its dreaded lair ?
Where hides the lank, hyena She,
Who ever clutches cruelly,
Some poor girl by the hair ?
What is Proprietee ?
HTto is the beast, and why ?
I’ faith, a glorious boon ’twould be
If old, false-toothed Propriety
Would eat her gamp— and die !

Some British officials have brought the
mean little art of insult to a fine point in the
Transvaal. Among other things, all Dutch
inscriptions on public buildings have been ordered
to be erased, and all over the country bumptious
public vandals have been reminding the Boers of
their downfall by hacking away the names of old
Boer soldiers and statesmen from foundation
stones and such-like. Britain selects its officials
mostly from the aristocratic caste, and a large
section of the British aristocracy has a gibbering
capacity for insult, and collects enemies just as
other people collect postage stamps and old
china.

Melbourne Argus was vastly amused lately
at a poor attempt at a strike among a few inmates
of the local Benevolent Asylum. Some old dere-
licts in that ‘‘charitable ” institution work in the
kitchens and thereabouts, and when they bucked
at having to do seven hours a day for seven days a
week, the committee of Lords and Ladies Bounti-
ful pointed out how they were flying in the
face of a beneficent Providence, and ordered them
to get to work forthwith, or be kicked out. A
paper with any sort of a conscience might have
spoken kindly of these ancients who get bread
and scrape for their 49 hours’ weekly work but
1 ne Argus always indulges its choicest humor at
tli© expense of the poor and nged.
At a Kilkivan (Q.) race-meeting a few
days ago four young fellows (three of them
brothers) got into trouble with the police. Ono
was being arrested for language, and the others,
showing an inclination to rescue him, were “held
up” with a revolver, and marched off to the lock-
up. Next day they were arraigned before three
local JVsP. on eleven charges of language, “ tear-
ing me uniform,” resisting and hindering the
police, etc., and the Bench, swollen with conse-
quence over the case, fined them in various sums,
with the usual alternative on some of the
charges, and on others gave them gaol without
the option. The total was £l7 in fines and seven
months’ imprisonment for the lot. Friends
immediately took the matter to the Supreme
Court, and secured an order quashing all the con-
victions except one—language, fine £2. The
four were released from Brisbane gaol in
triumph, but rejoicing was cut off at the stalk by
the arrival of the lawyer’s bill of costs —£100 !
And now defendants and their friends are asking,
“ What price liberty?” and also “Is the Great
Unpaid an invention of the lawyers ?”
* • * «
The woman continues to get into places
where she has never been before. In South Aus-
tralia the Methodist conference has been stormed
by a “ lady delegate.” The president said he
knew of no rule by which she could be shut
out, and so he proposed to let her sit until
the general (Australasian) conference could
pronounce judgment next May. The old
Wesleyan body probably wouldn’t have wel-
comed the lady; Wesley himself hadn’t
too pleasant a matrimonial career. The
minor Methodist sects, however, have always
looked more kindly upon the female, and
at least one of them —the Bible Christians, the
group which in S. Aus. has Chief Justice Way at
its head—has always had women preachers. The
chances are that, all the Methodies being now re-
united, the influence of the minor bodies will win
for the Methodist woman this new claim. And
from that to the Rev. Mrs. Melchizedek isn’t far.

The question of why Solomon poisoned
Rassmussen seems to present the Westralian
police with an insoluble problem, especially as
Solomon is dead now and can’t explain. Solo-
mon called late at night on Rassmussen, whom he
seems to have known very slightly, and brought
some beer and cake. After he had gone, the
neighbors were roused by the hollerings of Rass-
mussen and fled for a doctor. The case was diag-
nosed as strychnine, and Rassmussen was brought
back from the brink of the grave with a stomach-
pump. Then the police went after Solomon, but
Solomon promptly took poison, and all the
stomach pump on the Boulder failed to bring him
round. It couldn’t even keep him alive long
enough to state why he objected to Rassmussen.
Possibly, after all, he simply didn’t like the way
Rassmussen did his hair.

That £25 shout mentioned in a
recent Bulletin was a miserable
affair compared to Major-General
Tulloch’s invitation to fill-’em-
up. At the annual encampment
at Langwarrin of the Victorian
forces (I think it was in ’94), our
gallant Commandant, being
mightily pleased, ordered beer
for the whole assemblage—
about 4000 or 5000 men. The
men filed into the canteen on
the one side, had their beer,
went out the other side and
dispersed. The weather was
hot, and one strategical genii a
conceived the idea of march-
ing round the tent and falling
in again at the rear of his
company. Immediately an
endless chain was formed, and
not until the chain began to
sag and heave violently was
the fraud discovered.
“ Marat ” : The medical profes-
sion is not the high and haughty game it used
to be. A lodge medico, who attended on a
relative, handed me a draft of a letter (not
in his handwriting) which he wished me to
copy and forward to the Grand Panjandrum
of the lodge. “ Unremitting care,” ‘ ‘ un-
tiring energy,” and “ extraordinary skill,”
were a few of the extracts. I plaited in
17 more compliments, and forwarded it.
A lodge doctor wants all the testimonials
he can get to balance the complaints
that are sent in from members who want
to own his soul because they pay a shilling
a week, and who call him up in the
barren waste of the night to show him a corn.
* * * *
“ J.R.’ ” on the situation up North, where
the Bear and Monkey are :
The j’arns that come through the cable are too absurd
for anything, and S.M. Herald, in what it calls “ Com-
ments on the Situation,” or observations on the job, or
remarks about the billet, makes the confusion ten times
worse. The other day the old, solid and unreliable
penn'orth had a rigmarole about 25,000 Russians at
Possiet Bay, some 50 or 60 miles from Vladivostock, and
soon after announced that the Japs had landed there
without the least trouble. Now the Herald is busy
speculating on the .movements of 50,000 Russians in
buckram, who are said to be wandering about the north
of Korea, while the Japs are half-way between them and
their bread-basket at Harbin. What these alleged
Russians in Korea get to eat is a mystery. They need at
least 50 tons of food per day to keep them in fighting
and marching condition, and they must by this time
have eaten up everything and be reduced to grass. Fact
is, both Russians and Japs seem to be pretty good at
keeping their own counsel, and though anyone who
knows the rules of the game can guess pretty well what
is likely to be going on, no one can be at all sure about
details. It is pretty certain, for instance, that there are
very few Russian soldiers, except those in Port Arthur,
south of Moukden. The railway (600 miles long' between
Harbin and Port Arthur would, at the S’Afr.can rate
need 60,000 men to watch it, and they can scarcely be
available. Port Arthur is only held because the men and
stores cannot be got away.

“M.”: A fat woman in my street
died suddenly the other day. The doctor
said “ heartdisease.” The chemist told me
yesterday that she had bought enough
“anti-fat” mixtures at his shop to bring
the whole Victorian Legislative Coun-
cil down to featherweight. It is well for the
fat to know that anti-fat quackeries are mostly
either very dangerous or quite useless.
* ♦ * *
That coarse and violent old person, “ Eli-
jah ” Dowie of Zion, made a final disturbance in
Melbourne by blaring forth from the platform
that Methodists were liars, that the worst liar in
America was a Methodist parson, and that
Methodist parsons were liable to be thrown out of
his meetings on sight. A mild-mannered
Methodist preacher asked Dowie to withdraw his
remarks, whereupon the prophet went off with his
usual bang, leaped ungracefully in the air with
his thick body and brief legs, and shrieked with
wrath. He continued to leap and howl till
some of his “chuckers-out ” had thrown the
inoffensive Methodist into the street. Aus-
tralians are a long-suffering people, and the
arm of the law is very short hereabouts.
If it were otherwise, most of Dowie’s bullies
would now be in gaol for assault, and the Attorney-
General would be seriously considering whether
the old disgrace himself couldn’t be tried for
getting money by false pretences. If the pro-
fession that he is a reincarnated Elijah isn’t a
false pretence at law it ought to be one. Also,
there ought to be a law against an inflamed and
stumpy person, with a whirlwind of white beard,
leaping in the air on a public platform. Such a
proceeding is an outrage on art and a blot on the
landscape.
* • • •
Some of the extracts from “Elijah”
Dowie’s paper, Thieves and Stealing, suggest
that it is easy to build up a reputation as a healer
when the right kind of patient is found. One
believer tells his story in sudden yelps like this :
In June, 1899, my wife and I went to Chicago and
stayed for one week.
We attended the meetings in Central Zion Tabernacle..
One day my stomach troubled me very much, so we
came to your office, asking you to pray for me.
The Lord healed me, and I have never had it since.
I give God all the glory and thank you for your prayers. '
Probably there had been dozens of occasions when
the same poor wretch’s stomach, after a brief dis-
agreement with the meat pie, settled things with-
out prayer, but the sort of man that Dowie preys
upon never remembers such things. The faith-
healing wile mostly lives on temporary struggles
with the meat pie. By th® way, has anybody
heard anything further of the boy with the club
foot who met Dowie on his arrival in Sydney, and
was assured that he would get well if he had faith
enough ? Has he had faith enough ?

Dowie the Prophet got a letter last week
from a Melbourne solicitor demanding payment of
£75 alleged to be the balance due on a promissory
note given by Dowie to John Brown, formerly of
Carlton. The alleged Elijahbility was incurred
in December, ’B6, since when the Prophet has
risen far above the consideration of such small
details.
* * * *
Collier’s Weekly tells of a picturesque
incident in the Dowie campaign in New York.
A presswoman had got into one of his healing
meetings—supposed to be secret—and was only
discovered on leaving :
“Do you mean to write anything about this ? ” a
Dowie minion demanded. “If you do, you’ll regret it.”
“ I’ll write everything I’ve seen and heard.”
“ Well, then, listen. The Great Overseer is on to you.
He has looked up your record, and he knows more about
you than you think—more than you would like to have
him know. If you don’t promise right here to quit
writing about us, the Great Overseer will expose you to-
night in open meeting. The whole city will know what
we know about you.”
“And listen,” the young woman retorted—“ Tell the
Great Overseer from me that if he dare to utter a single
lie about me—if he make the slightest effort to attack
my character—there will be a dead prophet in this city
to-night.”
And Dowie “laid low and said nuffing.”
* * * •
Dowie gets less of the dead cat of scorn
from Adelaide than from any other Australian
city. _ No religious crankiness would be
sufficient to earn for him Adelaide’s hostility.
It feeds on that sort of thing. You can
strike little frilled sects there by the dozen.
HIS BURDEN.
S HE: “ Why didn’t Brown buy his wife that
motor car? ”
HE : “ Well, he came to the conclusion that one
unmanageable thing was enough for him.”
The Swedenborgians include a few of the best-
known city men. Christian Scientists, Theoso-
phists, Metaphysicians—all are more or less
influential. One of the holy city’s leading archi-
tects—the man who was responsible for the
design of the Stock Exchange— went to join
Dowie in Zion some months ago. Several of the
unfortunate village settlements were founded on
“ Christian Socialist ” principles. There is a
New Jerusalem Church. There is also a sect of
Christian Israelites, who won’t cut their hair
because they say Jesus Christ didn’t; yet, though
they are so solid on wearing their hair in the
alleged old Palestine fashion, their clothes are
such as were never seen “ down in Judee.”

“Alba”: Dowie is an indifferent stage
manager. Talmage monkeyed his way into public
favor. Johnny Toole brought a message from the
Prince of Wales. Albani rushed us With greetings
from Queen Victoria. The Australian barracker
and general interrupter demands some kind of a
smooge from visiting celebrities. In private life
the barracker is usually a parrot-chested enthusiast
who yells from behind some strong man’s chair.
Have made his acquaintance on various occasions,
and he usually drives a tip-cart or wears a Panama
hat and slaves in a bank. He has an eagle eye
for counter lunches and free public meetings, and
his parrot-chest is a perfect volcano of coughs.
In the presence of a gaud mayor he is silent,
mirthless. The god of laughter touched him not
when the Yorklet appeared wearing a bearskin
busby. If Dowie had arrived with a bogus mes-
sage from the King, he might have flapped his
wings in peace. If a royal underling were to visit
us accompanied by a barber’s pole or a black rod,
Parrot Chest, the interrupter, would wrap him-
self in last year’s flag and form himself into a hol-
low square of beer. Just now the bottle-oh’s and
bank clerks are telling us that, in future, all
visiting charlatans will have a warm time. As a
matter of fact, they would put up with an under-
sized cabbage or a duke’s big toe if the occasion
warranted.

In a complaint to the Victorian Chief
Commissioner of Police Mrs. “Elijah” Dowie
referred to the Jingo song, “ Sons of the Sea,” as
“very rude and extremely vulgar,” and declared
that she had never heard it before. “ Sons of the
Sea ” was sung by the crowd at the Exhibition
Building to break up Elijah’s meeting. Three
years ago if anybody in Melbourne had called
“Sons of the Sea” a very rude song, that dis-
reputable pro-Boer would have been struck by
lightning. If there was no lightning about the
share-brokers would have rolled tram cars over
the caitiff.
THE OLD MAN AND THE PARROT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O Woman, in our hours of ease,
Uncertain, coy, and hard to please !
When pain and anguish wring the brow
The cause— alas / is chiefly thou I
Scott Up-to-date.
The old man sat and talked aloud,
Alone in his old arm-chair ;
And nobody heard
Not a word
But the bird,
For nobody else was there.
“ These women would be Jurors now 1
It makes a person vexed ;
And lawyers too 1
Can this be true?
flood gracious me ! What next'?”
The old grey bird
In tones absurd
Picked up the word :
“ What next?”
“ What next ? ” said the bird ; “ Ha, ha ! ho, ho !
That’s just what we all would like to know.
As the tadpole said when his tail dropped off :
What next 1 ”
“ It seems to me,” the old man said
A gleam in his cold grey eye ;
“It certainly seems
That she schemes
And she dreams
To rule in the world or die !
These twentieth century maidens make
A poor old man perplexed.
They’d change their rigs
For horse-hair wigs
And lawyers' gowns ! What next ?
The gage is hurled 1
These damsels curled
Would rule the world 1
What next ?”
What next?” said the bird, as a smile be hid,
" Don’t the old man know that they alien.,s did ?
What a silly old man to sit and cry :
‘What next?”’
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
