A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 8, 1904.
My Dear Arini.—
The flat habit is growing on our “ leading society
women” and “prominent club men.” It is spread-
ing like—l was going to write Cape-weed or Scotch
thistle, but the noxious-plant simile hardly fits
such a case. Up-to-date flats are rare in Sydney,
and also horribly expensive. In Macleay-street a
house of flats is growing towards completion,
and will be inhabited by the Forbes-Sempills
(the aristocratic young man-o’-war shares his
hyphen with a golden daughter of the Hudson
Soap millionaire), the Alec Hays (she is a daughter
of Mrs. Burdekin), the widow of the late Justice
Manning and her daughter, and the Gollins.
Then there is a bird-of-Paradise pile of flats (Dai-
ley’s property) going up near the Burdekin house
in Macquarie-street. Said that every flat
in this is already secured, principally by
nil admirari young men who get their chif-
fon velvet pyjamas from London and have no
White Man’s Burden sitting up for them o’ nights
with a cat and a candle and reproaches. No
ohildren will be admitted as residents. The Birth-
rate problem will simply be shooed off the
premises. After this building has got its coping
stone there will, ’tis said, be an exodus to
it from “Cromer,” in Phillip-street, at present a
popular “flatter,” inhabited by a few exclusive
dukes in disguise, and Imperialistic Anglo-Indians
who superintend their own currying, as well as by
such moderns as Dr. Nash, M.L.C., Mr. Jack
Dailey and Mr. Owen Cox.
Once you’ve acquired the flat habit you never
again want to have the bother of a garret-to-
baseraent house ownership. You have shifted a
large layer of your responsibility (i.e., worry) on
to someone who has a genius for management (i.e.,
for overcoming worries).
Concerning worries ! The report of the Royal
Commission on the declining birth-rate, though
scareheaded by some of the dailies with “Start-
ling Revelations! ” provoked a mental retort from
the majority—“ Queen Anne’s dead.” It simmers
down to the fact that most women will no longer
willingly endure the worry of rearing large
families. A few weeks ago that clever woman’s
letter from London in S.M. Herald had a noble
sentiment, to the effect that “it is when she is
self-sacrificing, and lives and works for others,
that woman is nearest her crown.” I overheard
a woman reading the letter to another, who
presently interrupted with “she ought to specify
that the crown would be of thorns.” Whilst
allowing that a slight decrease in births is due
to the rise in the marriage age during the period
1889-1902, the Commissioners have concluded that
the main cause of the declining birth-rate is one
over which individuals themselves have control.
One may imagine the gloomy sigh of the collective
Birth-raters when they came to that con-
clusion, knowing as they do that one of the most
difficult things in the world to arouse is the collec-
tive conscience of a nation. For, despite the legis-
lation which the Birth-raters urge to help the
stork to tap at more windows, the cure of the evil
must come from aroused conscience. And in many,
many houses you might pound on the door till you
drove a mass of wrecked panels into the hall, and
rouse everything except a conscience.
‘ * British Empire League ” ! What a fine, large
sound it has—like Brotherhood of Man or Arch-
angels’ Co-operative Stores for Celestial Outfits.
It lifts one right above the fruit-vending Dago
end of George-street to the radius of the Ever-
lasting Daisy. When I got an invitation to a
“Conversazione of the Women’s Branch of the
British Empire League ” last week I threw over a
prior engagement to wash my Gordon setter dog
in order to conversaz. with the Imperial leaguers.
But they didn’t give anyone off the plat-
form a show. Bruce Smith —a pin curl over his
right eyebrow—talked for what seemed like two
months on the beauty of patriotism and the
encouragement of the growth of the Empire spirit
in Australia. Mr. Knibbs followed with a quan-
tity of oversoul. Then Dame Barton read a fare-
well address to Mrs. Hugh Dixson, the President
of the Branch- Mrs. Dixson, who with Mrs.
Robert Farrell received her guests —they would
have been her guests if his Smithjesty and his
Knibbs hadn’t talked so much—leaves for Europe
in the Mongolia presently. Her two daughters—
Mrs. Eaton and Miss Marion Dixson—accompany
her. Her other daughter, Mrs-. Ronald Keep,
is already in London.
The feminine medical profession in Sydney
always rolls up in force when someone speaks
from a platform on “ the magnificence of British
action in sending 250,000 men and spending
£250,000,000 to vindicate the rights of a few
British subjects in South Africa.” Dr. Grace
Boelke, in a pretty blue frock, and with a nice
contralto speaking voice, was on the platform ;
plump little Dr. Mary Booth and Dr. Harriet
Biffin —commonly called “Biff” —were there in
please-the-Pope bodices and hygienic skirts. Mrs.
B. R. Wise, just back from Melbourne, occupied
a strategic position near an exit, but failed t
make use of it till the last speaker had spoken.
Talking of women doctors, Dr. Mabel Graham,
who married the Rev. Gordon Baillie on the last
day of February, had a brilliant series of winnings
at Sydney Uni. When she emerged from her
Final, three years ago, she had but one man in
front of her fine red head.
Buds—clusters of buds, hundreds of buds —
from the various girls’ colleges crowded Sydney
Town Hall on Saturday night to hear Yvonne
Leverrier give an account of herself, on the piano.
For seven years (five in Vienna, two in Paris)
she has been, we are told, working hard at her art.
And one can well believe it —first, because of the
mastery she has got over her chosen instrument;
second, because of her over-worked appearance
and piano-practice figure. Miss Leverrier has a
very pretty face, with big pathetic grey eyes and
a fuzzy-wuzzy head of dark hair. If she doesn’t
give the symphony-concerto a rest and take to
whole meal porridge and cream and open
air she may develop a grasshopper back. Appear-
ance is so much to a girl who has to plat-perform.
Half musicianl v y Sydney, amateur or professional,
was present and helped the buds to applaud some
of the best pianoing this city has heard. The
pianiste has great power and delicacy and her
music flows without a break—now rushing like a
river in flood time, now rippling, a thin
streak of sound. Miss Neville Vane, a mezzo-
soprano from Goulburn, who looks as writer
Ethel Turner does in her fluffy portraits, made
her re appearance. She has a promising voice and
was either very nervous or awfully self-assured.
She picked out her friends in the audience with
perky little smiles and returned for flowers and
encores as if she were coming to chase geese out of
a garden.
Miss Leverrier’s matinee concert is postponed
from Wednesday to Monday on account of the
mayor’s luncheon in the vestibule of Town Hall.
Thus do Victuals get in the way of Art.
JRHaruko (isn’t that a pretty name ? I expect to
see it on gate-posts soon, or used by some girl-
writer as a pen-name), Empress of Japan, is, I
learn—in the rush of Jap notices —the daughter
of a noble of the first rank, and is 54 years of age.
Her only child —a son—died at birth, but the
Mikado has assured the succession with the
children of the various Princesses who share his
affections. Haruko is small in stature even for a
Jap lady; her husband is taller than most of his
people.
Persons who want to get or keep up a reputa-
tion for being “in the know” have been telling
all they don't know and never heard about the
alleged engagement of Miss Maud Jeffries to a
member of a well-known N.S.W. squatting family.
On a night last week a huge basket of fine blooms
was passed up to Miss Jeffries. Mr. Knight
received it and made as if to pass it to
her, but she refused it with a haughty gesture,
putting her trophy-hand behind her and walked off
the stage. The omniscient ones immediately con-
nected the incident with “ the man she had re-
fused,” the man to whom she was “engaged for
one week, ” the man of whom “ her brother doesn’t
approve.” Meanwhile, Katusha keeps her own
counsel and declines to be drawn.
Mr. Theodor Boesen, Consul-General for
Denmark, who died last Sunday, was one of
the most assiduous concert-goers Sydney pos-
sessed. _He was as familiar a concert-mark as the
grand piano, and he and his wife were always to
the front in any charitable movement.
Some one sends me from Fogtown the pro-
gramme of a charity-concert at which Alice
Hollander sang “Night and Dawn,” Florence
Schmidt something from the “ Sicilian Vespers,”
and Eva Mylott “My Heart is Weary,” and to
which George Lambert contributed a pictorial
front page. Even in its blurred reproduction,
Lambert’s sketch looks very good.
How must it feel for an artist, used all her
days to sunshine, to sing before a matinee audi-
ence of which the front row only is visible to
her? Contralto Mylott, says the S.M. Heeald,
made her first appearance at Chappell’s ballad
concerts in a dense black fog, and it was like
singing to one hazy line of people, one line of
indistinct blots, and a vast, murky blanket. The
names of two of Eva’s songs, “The Hour of
Sleep” and a “Barque at Midnight,” seemed to
fit the nocturnal atmosphere.
A Juliet cap of pearls worn by a bride at her
wedding is a welcome change from the wreath of
orange blossoms. A very rich girl (daughter of a
millionaire knight named Pearson), who recently
married Lord Denman (first Lord Denman
defended Queen Caroline in the famous divorce
suit) had the originality to so cap her golden hair.
The marriage has a faint aroma of interest for
N.S.W. because_t he Denmans are nearly related
to the M'Mickings, a well-known Pure Merino
clan of this State.
We have in Australia quite a number of heirs,
or heirs a few times removed, to very 18-carat
titles that live in England. A certain young
Sydney solicitor who writes stories that are
reminiscent of water gruel, is heir to a marquisate,
and a young Primrose in Queensland has a third
chance of being Lord Rosebery. Lord R. has
two sons.
Mr. Reggie Broomfield, son of the late Captain
Broomfield, of Balmain, was married last week to a
Melbourne girl, Miss Mac Nab.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The very complete and thorough fashion in
which the new Gov.-General is doing things was
shown at the Parliament Opening, when at the
rear of his detachment of aides the private secre-
tary advanced in a black velvet court dress. No
previous Australian Governor’s secretary ever
sported such a garb. It is an additional effort to
give impressiveness to the little G.-G.’s ensemble.
If you haven’t got any importance of character, or
of intellect, or of appearance, you must eke out
the situation with clothes—on yourself and on
your flunkeys. Lord Northcote though, by the
way, has a melodious voice. That, with plenty of
clothes, should pull him and the Empire through.
There were a few notables scattered about the
audience. Lord Monkbretton, an intelligent-
looking person, sat with Lady Northcote and the
retroussA Lady Knowles. Up in the gallery Mr.
George Peel (of Extension Cable note) directed a
series of coal-black glances on the proceedings.
The cable authority has a marble-white face, inky
eyes, and an inky thatch. He is oppressed, to
all seeming, with the seriousness of life. Near to
him sat Miss Doyle—a Sydney blonde, sister-in-
law to barking Rep. Conroy. She ranked as
almost the only charmer in a gathering particu-
larly free from smartness. Wearing a forget-me-
not blue, her friends in the House seemed to wear
an answering forget-thee-not expression.
“ Lady Northcote’s opening -of - Parliament
toilette was less striking than her conversational
achievements. She can talk ! Most of her speech
fell to Senator Drake —always a suave, sympa-
thetic listener to feminine utterances. When he
was P.M.G. he was once interviewed by an Irish
lady, wrongfully accused of maltreating a depart-
mental scrubbing-brush. Smarting with injured
innocence, wounded honor and congested indigna-
tion, the widow entered the chief’s sanctum like a
lion and came out like a lamb. He listened to all
she had to say, she afterwards testified, and
‘treated her as if he had been her own father.’
Even in the first burst of excitement, when (as the
goaded Celt confessed later), she had called down
curses upon the Commonwealth with special
allusion to the Federal Post Office authorities,
* there wasn’t so much as a wry look on his face.’
“Every place of public amusement (and Parlia-
ment on Opening Day is a place of amusement)
has its own type of female devotee. Theatres are
favored by fine, large women with roomy anatomy
for diamonds and decolletage. Cycle girls are
young and pretty. In the Parliamentary galler-
ies, there are, of course, majestic political she-
personages. But in addition to these, there’s a
skittishly - clad, restlessly - mannered section.
Green veils are epidemic among them, red fringe-
nets, (which give the head a coppery tinge), and
cerise under-skirts.
“Lord Dicky Nevill, that long, lean and
careworn-looking person of uncertain age, still
strolls around Melbourne as though he were
an item appertaining to Guv’ment House, which
seemingly he isn’t. In a way of speaking, Lord
Richard is an actor open to engagement for a
walking-gentleman part at the Vice-Regal
Comedy Theatre. If the forthcoming Talbot
doesn’t require his services, perhaps Tom Bent
can appoint him Minister of Etiquette, or Fine
Arts, or something. The Bent Cabinet could do
with a Minister of Etiquette, anyhow.
“Ex-Premier Irvine’s departure was a frigid
concern. There were less than 20 people present
to see him off the premises, and they mostly stood
around and stared. As the steamer got moving,
Chief Railway Commissioner Tait called for three
cheers. The response was feeble, but some work-
men on the outside of the little crowd offered to
cheer if the departing statesman would promise
not to come back. Mrs. Irvine looked as cold as
her lord and berg. She goes away leaving three
young children behind her.
‘‘The G.-G. beats all previous efforts at
amiability in his published intention to face the
bone-mills, the abattoirs, and miscellaneous smells
of Footscray, to inspect the small-arms factory
there.
“Strange to say none of the Madden clan
turned out to see the Madden Cup contest at the
Polo Tournament the other Monday. Languid
indifference this—as if gold cups were thrown to
the mob by the Maddens every day. The North-
cotes were represented by all the aides and Lady
Knowles—to say nothing of several poodles
gently lowered from the drag by the green and
gold Jeames for a scamper over the course. All
Society stooped to stroke the poodles, in a way
which suggested that a blue bite from a vice-
regal dog would be a proud distinction. The
greab splash of the day was achieved by the
Pursey Chirnsides, who arrived on the scene in a
motor that looked as if it had escaped from a
circus—a gorgeous affair, with tarpaulin roof,
glass sides, scarlet decorations and a band-box
flunkey in immaculate white uniform.
“The most complete gymkhana yet accom-
plished in this State gave the polo junkets
a lift into favor last Friday. A special tent was
pegged in an above-the-salt manner for the vice-
regal party which attended. All the fair whips
of Melbourne who are in possession of pony-carts
supplied the critical item in the gymkhana, and
their turn-outs, their driving gear, their handling
of the ribbons, kept a great circle of gapers in-
tensely interested. Some of the whips had a girl
mate for company, Mrs. Fred Payne had a
brother-in-law, and Mrs. Kemp a Pomeranian
poodle wearing a fashionable emerald tie. Surgeon
Embling’s dame carried off the prize fairly, but
sports liked the trim jauntiness of Miss Whiting,
while Toorak nearly wept because ‘ dear Milli-
cent ’■ didn’t lick all the whip competitors.
“Some of the finest dresses at Friday’s polo
break-out were spoilc by the newly-painted cane-
chairs that offered comfortable rest with the tea
and ices under the refreshment tarpaulin. In
honor of the swagger occasion all the chairs had
been given black rims, and these proved to be
transferable.
“Mrs. Webster, who was the beautiful Miss
Raleigh before she wedded the rich Collins-street
medico, is airing some of the smart wardrobe
treasures she recently brought from Fogtown. At
polo, the other day, she wore the latest thing in
autumn gear—a dark bottle-green rough tweeci
made with the brief skirt and the tailed coat. It’s
not the thing to inspire excessive joy, but it has
the stamp of the latest London tailoring and so it
takes. At the same rally the Misses Landale
surpassed all previous early-Victoria efforts by
their light billowing frills, their stringed bonnets,
and fussy sunshades. They might have stepped
out of Vanity Faie.
“ An Englishman who captured an Australienne
on the high seas, and who was, from written
aocounts, the most desirable, and nobly-bred, and
sweetly-beautiful bridegroom ever captured, turns
out, on viva voce examination, to be a black-
whiskered person with a squeaky voice and
enough side to capsize an ocean liner. But
Toorak presses polite overtures upon the new
arrival, and gurgles delightedly over ‘ the lucky
bride.’ ‘ She married an Englishman, you know.’
That is sufficient. Yet the average gently-swear-
ing cookie from Gippsland or the sore-footed
prospector from Marble Bar is a finer fellow in
most respects than most of the weedy samples of
the well-born Britisher that the sweet Australi-
enne is proud to bestow her cash on.
“ Comic-opera baritone, Haigh Jackson, is not
the kind to fire the feminine imagination. He
just sings and has not a look, a sign, a gesture to
answer to the Melbourne girl’s notion of an ador-
able baritone. He’s even slipshod in the amorous
business, and kisses with little of the beautiful
care ordinarily exercised by the stage-lover. The
matinee girl may pardon the baritone who sings
out of tune so long as he kisses with distinction
and ogles with verve and obligato and other fix-
ings ; but the perfunctory lover on the stage may
lift the voice of an Orpheus in vain.
“Furnished houses to let are a sign of the times—
the original occupants dallying abroad in most
cases; in others the country crib calls. Two
moneyed land-owners will put their town proper-
ties under the hammer this autumn, and sail to
the Cold Country. Mr. Ernest Raleigh does the
same in a few days. Fashionable people here are
growing out of conceit with the place. The
example of other Victorians quartered in Fogtown
is pernicious. London seems, to an increasing
number, to be the best place to splash about the
lucre earned by the fertile areas and not too fer-
tile toilers of this Gibeonite land.
“On Australian Gup Day Premier Tom Bent
figured prominently. Thomas the Bulky was in-
vited up the carpeted stairs of the vice-regal box
by a sober-faced aide, and was seized by a ludicrous
ambition to approach the Presence in a dignified
manner. Short of steam, he stopped at the second
top step, whereupon Lady Northcote did the
gracious thing and met Thomas half-way. Tommy
wasn’t flustered ; he stuck his dipper of a hat
down on his ears, and, after a few words of cordial
intent, waddled .qff with his duplicate. Mr. ,E.
Morey, M.L.C-
-“ Probably Lady Northcote’s costume on Aus-
tralian Cup Day was the best of her sartorial
series up to date. It was in black and white and
gold—a silk under-slip of white having a top layer
of inky lace in the Spanish pattern. Over its rear
fell long sash ends (there’s something essentially
feminine in a flying sash end) of pale gold-flowored
silk. More of the gold shade appeared at the
corsage and feathered hat. The whole costume
gave an opulent air, of a Bank of England note
walking out with the gold reserve.
“ Toorak is sorry for Colonel Selwyn Campbell,
who was not given a proper show in guiding the
Waxellency’s craft during his temporary Gover-
norship. Soon after the Colonel established his
ink-pots at Stonington and got going on the Priv.
Sec. work, a bereavement fell on his chief’s
family, and it had to draw in its horns. Now,
just as these are showing again, the new Governor
is called to Stonington.
“Mr. E. M‘Caughan, when all the affairs of
the family estate have been fixed up, takes a
yacht party up as far as Torres Straits. They
dally awhile in the vicinity of the Papuan shores,
then the yacht-owner returns to pack up his kit
for a long sojourn abroad.
“ The much-delayed presence of Governor Le
Hunte’s wife was accorded to a Holy City sports
meeting the other day ; but scarcely had the
impression of her nice, middle-aged manners and
pleasant smile been registered than, with her
small daughter, she hied away to the V.R. cottage
in the hills.
“ A young official had to wait on the temporary
head of this State a few days ago. He presented
himself at the residence, and was told by the
porter to wait a little time as his Excellency was
‘ having his massage.’ Shortly after, he was
summoned to the functionary’s exalted presence,
and found the odor of steamed herbs, orris powder,
and rose water clinging to the atmosphere ; and
the bumps of the Excellency shone dazzlingly like
the brass plates of Collins-street.
“Melbourne Argus proprietors have always
had a weakness for ostentatious display of horse-
flesh. Archibald Michie, who hated the Argus
men, once stated publicly that he delighted to
“ rub it into” Wilson, who used to prance about
Melbourne on a fiery steed. Lachlan M’Kinnon,
a nephew of Wilson’s partner, now drives to the
races a drag-and-four with two tigers in blue at
the back, and himself in front with a white bell-
topper falling over his eyes.
“A bachelor known best among the seasoned
Nimrods is conducting a skilful campaign for the
heart and fortune of one of Melbourne’s wealthiest
young prizes. The smart set are fiercely betting
on the chances of the event coming off.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.


RACKED BY RHEUMATISM.
“ I felt the first twinge of Rheumatism about a year
ago,” says Mr. J. H. Gam.es, 12 Elizabeth-street, Sydney.
“It started in my shoulders and gradually spread all over
my body. It racked nre day and night. I got so bad that
I could hardly turn in bed—when I got up I could hardly
move. I could just crawl about. No one knows the
fierceness of Rheumatism unless they suffered as I did.
The pain told terribly on my nerves, for I was as shaky as a
leaf. I used all kinds of liniments and boasted cures, but
they did me no good. I was beginning to give up hope
when a friend made me take Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. He
said there was nothing like them for rheumatism and
neuralgia. He explained that they actually made new
blood and drove away all the painful acid from the veins.
I have reason to be grateful f r this friendly advice, for
four boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured my rheumatism
completely. They filled my veins with new, pure, strong,
rich red blood, that swept out the rheumatic poison and
conquered all my pain. My health was improved in
fifty ways besides. I slept well, ate well, and was filled
with fresh energy and strength. Now I tell everyone that
there is no blood-building nerve tonic in the world that
can be put in the same class with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People.”
The best treatment for most common diseases is a tonic treatment, as every doctor knows—and, as
Mr. Eames says, there is no tonic like Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. They do only one thing,
but they do it well—they actually make new blood. Bad blood is the one cause of all common ailments,
like anaemia, indigestion, headaches, backaches, lumbago, rheumatism, sciatica, neuralgia, nervousness and
general weakness. Bad blood is the one cause, and good blood is the only cure. There you have the secret
of the never-failing success of the great blood-building nerve tonic—Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.
Write for Free Medical Advice. P>r. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place. Sydney. 2s. 9d. a box. —Refuse all substitutes.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The new Governor-General, Northcote, has
already found liis level. He is to open the dog,
pigeon, and turnip show at Moore Park, Sydney,
at Easter!
After 20 years of toil Joseph Symes has at
last been compelled to suspend publication of the
Liberator. He is circularising his friends for
funds with which to resuscitate the vigorous
atheist publication.
Japan’s representative, Angas Parsons, is
the shortest consul in Adelaide, but he wears the
longest coat and the tallest hat. Russia does not
possess any agent in the city, so local bloodshed
will probably be averted.
Another politician has died comparatively
poor. For probate purposes the estate of Edward
Braddon was sworn at £8720. Also the will of
Archibald Campbell, late M.L.A. for Wollongong
(N.S.W.) has been proved at £2103.
Old Sam uel Davenport, the oldest legis-
lator in Australia (he was a member of S.A.
Nominee Council in 1846), completed his 86th
year on March 5. Adye Douglas (Tas.) will be
89 on May 30, but he did not enter Parliament
until 1856.
Angas Johnson, a wealthy Adelaide (S.A.)
doctor, who has already studied largely in France
and Germany, leaves early in April with the
intention of spending several years in the search
for a cancer cure. He is a near relative of
millionaire Angas, and is an enthusiast in his
profession. To a doctor, nowadays, a cure for
oancer is what the North Pole is to an explorer,
or the blue twopenny Mauritius stamp of
eighteen-forty-something is to a stamp-lunatic.
THE CORPSE'S JOB WAS VACANT.
“I’ll be lavin' ye, Mrs. O'Brien."
“ Leavin' me is it, Mary?”
"It is, ma'am. Ye know I was out all night at the wake."
"Well, didn't I excuse ye for it?"
“ Fes, ma’am; but"
“But what, Mary?"
“ Well, ma’am, ye know I'm gain' to be married."
“Married is it, and to whom, pray?”
“ Oh, only the corpse's husband."
March 3, 1904, was the 50th anniversary of
the first Australian telegraphic message, which
passed from Melbourne to Williamstown. There
is one telegraphic official surviving from that day,
Mr. T. R. James, who was pensioned from the
Victorian P.O. in 1893.
Nerli, of Sydney, who is a relative of
Pius X., has gone to Rome, report says, to become
a sort of official painter to the Pope. Nerli, when
on a visit to Samoa in 1892, painted the portrait
of Robert Louis The painting, sold
for £4O, is now in Edinburgh Art Gallery.
Obit. : Charles Dean, explorer Gregory’s
old shellback, of the 1855 expedition in search of
Leichhardt. Dean had the reputation of being
the humorist of the camp ; and men who travelled
with Gregory badly needed to hear a cheerful
remark now and then. Dean ended his days at
Lismore, N.S. W.
George Dean, who made a big sensation in
Sydney a few years ago, will be liberated at the
end of this year. He is now assistant to
the electrical engineer in Goulburn Gaol. Dean
put in eight years at the tailoring trade, with a
longing always for the engine-room, which long-
ing the department at last gratified.
Elias Solomon, late M.H.R. for Fremantle
(W.A.), who was emptied out by boiler-maker Bill
Carpenter, now spends his time in attending
bazaars and semi-political meetings. Sol has con-
siderable property in Fremantle. He intends to
stand for the local Parliament, and, if the other
man goes to sleep or dies, Sol will win easily.
S. Australia has a “bloodless limb-
straightener ” living near Angaston. Told that
when a local millionaire hobbled to him, saying he
couldn’t bend his knee, the doctor promptly
placed him in a recumbent position on a couch,
and sat on the knee. The millionaire used ungodly
language at the time, but was able to pray on his
knees for forgiveness by night.
“A.G.”: Alas, all is vanity. There is
'nothing real in this world of shams. Even The
Bulletin believed in the truth of Dill Macky’s
beautiful shanks. So did I till I saw the saintly
shanks, one morning recently, on the beach at
Manly—naked and unashamed. My one remain-
ing religious illusion was shattered on the spot.
The “Tabernacle,” where Elijah Dowie
preached (in Johnston-st., Fitzroy, Melbourne)
a quarter of a century ago, is now a second-hand
furniture shop. In those days Dowie used to be
summoned once a month for obstructing the
public thoroughfare, but he and his chief hench-
man went to court joyfully, and compared them-
selves to Paul and Silas.
N.S. W. Senator Gould, the icy man who
calls himself a Freetrader because he belonged to
the old Parkes Ministry, which protected nearly
everything, was woefully misled by the Foreign-
trade papers when he tried to wrest the Presi-
dency of the Senate from Baker. It was almost
a three to one majority that banged Gould’s
frozen head against the wall.
Reginald Talbot, Victoria’s threatened
governor, is tall, but a buried brother was taller.
Captain Talbot, of Cheltenham (Eng.), aggregated
80 inches from his No. 12 boots to his No. 1\ hat.
He was at one time violently athletic, and once
backed himself at the Lamb Hotel, in the garden
town, to drink a bottle of whisky in three breaths.
There are men in Australia who could do it in
two.
Memory of old Paul Kruger is revived
by a cable that he is dying at Mentone (France),
and that his mind is giving way. Kruger was in
some respects narrow-minded, and he has often
been called stupid. But the march of events
adds every day to his reputation for prescience
and statesmanship. He saw from the first the
evils which the gold fever would bring to his
country, fought them as well as he might, and to
the last ditch withstood the efforts of the mining
magnates to run and ruin the Transvaal. He
failed, because the might of Britain helped the
arm of Cohen. But it was a failure with more of
glory than the success which has delivered
S’Africa over to Asiatic helotry.
“An Altar Boy” writes about the late
Bishop Torreggiani, of Armidale (N.S.W.): —
Bishop Torreggiani used to travel
by coach through his diocese attired
in his soutane and the Roman mis-
sionary hat—to wit a black, hard
one, with a wide verandah held
on to the roof by two black silk
bands like wire stays holding on a
ship’s funnel. The sitting area
of Torreggiani’s soutane always
showed signs of wear, through the
massive cleric falling heavily on it
every time the vehicle bumped.
The wear was so severe that the gar-
ment was once laid up in dock for
about a day undergoing necessary
repairs before his Lordship could go
to administer confirmation. At
that memorable time, Dean Doyle,
Bishop Torreggiani and John Sex-
ton, an old and good lay pillar of
the R.C. church at Lismore, under-
took a journey of 60 miles to the
Tweed River over the mountain
ranges. At that time Torreggiani
netted 22st.—weighed at Peter
Dawson’s store, Lismore. A con-
ference, lav and clerical, was held
re the means of transporting the
Bishop to the Tweed. In the end
John Sexton provided a heavy
draught horse, averaging about
knots per hour. The Bishop was
put on board, and the contingent
started. After the first day’s
journey the Bishop got fatigued,
and at the approach of night a
temporary camp consisting of a
large umbrella for a tent, and the
contents of a swag for a bed, was
fixed up on the Nightcap Ranges.
The billy was boiled, and then the
Bishop went to sleep for about 12
hours. The other members of the
contingent retired to a distance
of a kilometre, for the Bishop was
a heavy and regular nasal trom-
bone operator. It is alleged that
on one occasion, when Torreggiani
wanted to hire a vehicle for a
journey, the owner postponed a
definite answer till next day,
and in the interim took his
trap into a lone spot and loaded
it with boulders to test its stay-
ing power. Torreggiani replaced
O’Mahony, of Armidale, mostly
through the anti-Irish views of the
Vaughan party and its influence
with the Papal Brief Department
at Rome. Torreggiani always fed
well, slept well, and did his duty
well, but he was a failure re pay-
ing off church debts. Bishop
O’Connor, a zealous laborer, now
keeps the evil spirit in check in
and about Armidale. He was born
at a fish town called Dungarvan—
but that is another story.
“ Tot ” : Everybody has
heard of the “blessed word
Mesopotamia.” When the late
Rev. Chas. Clark first arrived
in Melbourne, a prominent
mercantile man said to me: “What a beautiful
word he used in his first sermon —‘etiolated.’
I wonder you didn’t notice it. That’s the preacher
for me. ”
Contrary to expectations late “Jock”
M‘lntyre left a good deal of property ; his will
shows a total of £6865. The impression that
MTntyre was stone-broke perhaps arose merely
from the fact that most old Parliamentarians are
that way nowadays.
It is pointed out by correspondents of the
Sydney daily papers that several officers of the
local defence force act as consuls for various
powers. “Powers” is particularly good in this
connection. Imagine Colonel Waddell, Consul
for Peru, advising the Peruvians how easy it
would be to capture Australia, or Major Freehill,
Consul for Spain, telling State-defence secrets to
the Spaniards.
“ Elijah ” Dowie’s brother Andrew, who
owns a row of little cottages in Adelaide, had
them painted in the highest style of art in honor
of the prophet’s visit, and then Elijah did not
take the trouble to go and see him. The Elijah
establishment in the hills near Adelaide, during
the stay of the Zion party, was replete with every
luxury, and the equipages in which Zion took the
air were various and aristocratic.
Little Fred Magona, late of the Age, is
lying seriously ill in a Perth hospital. Fred is
undoubtedly one of the most eloquent men on the
globe. Writer once heard him, at Parliament
House, Melbourne, give his opinion on the disap-
pearance of a lead pencil. He did it in one
sentence, consisting of something like 76 words.
The sentence would have been longer, only he
found the missing pencil behind his ear.
“Anthony Hordern.” Another Anthony !
The first Hordern (Sydney, 1820, or thereabouts)
was an Anthony. He had a son, making the second
Anthony. That Anthony had two sons—Anthony
tertius, and Samuel. The former had a son
Anthony, who died. Now Sam has a son Anthony,
the fifth, and this Anthony recently laid the
first brick of the new premises in George-street.
Apropos the mansion of Tobacco Dixson,
senior. The home of the old man while he was
building up his fortune was “Lulworth,” on the
road now known as Roslyn Gardens, running
from Bayswater Road to Elizabeth Bay, Sydney,
a house subsequently occupied by Justice Win-
deyer and Just-ice Gould. Old Hugh Dixson, in
the fifties, kept a tobacco shop at 301 George-
street, and resided on the premises.
When “Elijah” Dowie was preaching in
Collingwood years before he founded “Zion,”
there was a certain wicked hotelkeeper whose
threepenny beers wrought great havoc in that
suburb. Dowie arranged a great temperance
demonstration, to be held in front of the
offending pub. When Mr. Public-house heard of
this he issued handbills setting forth that liquor
would be dispensed gratis during his friend Mr.
Dowie’s address. There was no demonstration.
Dowie probably feared to trust his flock within
the free bar radius.
Francis Hope, brother and heir presump-
tive to the Dukedom of Newcastle, has married
Olive “daughter of the late George Thompson,
banker of Melbourne.” Can anyone say who
“George Thompson, banker,” was. Francis
Hope is the cheerful gentleman of 37, who was
husband to May Yohe with whom he visited
Australia. That marriage was dissolved. The
Duke of Newcastle is only 40 years of age, so
there isn’t so very much joy in being his heir pre-
sumptive. And it is not a wildly affluent duke-
dom. It is many years since a Newcastle last ate
a £5 note in a sandwich.
“M.” : Some years ago (say a dozen) I
met the late George Francis Train in Singapore.
He came in one hot afternoon and appropriated
my office. He celebrated his own exploits at
murderous length, and applauded himself with
vehemence. He was making a record “ round the
world.” His immediate demand was for a ship to
carry him on to Europe ahead of the mail. Some
of us managed to get him a passage in a steamer
then coaling at Tanjong Pagar, on a homeward
race with tea. His gratitude for this small kind-
ness was tumultuous. He gave the press boys a
dinner that night at the Hotel de l’Europe. It
was a goodish dinner, and afterwards, while we
drank his whisky, he made a two hours speech.
He was an amusing old cuss, but tiresome.
Thus T.P.’s Weekly on the late Herbert
Spencer:
On the day that Spencer died the principal item on the
broadsheets of the papers was a cricket match in Austra-
lia. The irony of the situation is irresistible. One
Italian journal appeared in mourning' borders, another
devoted seven columns to the exposition of Spencer’s
work and aims; and here, in the city;in which he lived
and labored nearly ail his life, people were asking each
other, “ who was he? ” and reading of the famous scores
at the Antipodes. The Italian Parliament interrupted it
sitting to condole with England op the loss of her greatest
son, and Mr. Balfour was probably playing golf some-
where in Scotland. The obsequies of this man of genius
. . . were attended by a few personal friends, without a
single official representative of the nation which he has
done so much to glorify.
Ex-Acfcing-Chief-.Justice Stephen had some
stiff notions about his duty. He was away on
circuit in the West when his father died ; got the
message while on the bench, read it, and went on
with the case, mentioning it to nobody. Later in
the day the news reached the lawyers through the
newspaper offices, the matter was mentioned, and
an adjournment suggested. The Judge said he
had been through a hard morning, that his first
impulse had been to rise, but that he had looked
at the dock (it was a committal matter) and con-
cluded it wouldn’t be fair to the accused to hang
the case up on account of his own personal loss.
LEWIS COHEN, M.P., MAYOR OF
ADELAIDE.
Senate-President Chaffey Baker was led
up to the chair by Gregor M‘Gregor and J. T.
Walker. That was getting between Labor and
Capital, anyway. Walker, if he hasn’t wealth
himself, represents plenty of it—has for years
helped to direct the affairs of one of Australia’s
biggest private estates, and of its biggest insur-
ance co., and its oldest bank. M‘Gregor, on the
other side, never got further than “general
laborer”—was a pick-and-shovel man by day and
a student by night—till he was sent to the South
Australian State Parliament. M‘Gregor has also
the distinction of owning perhaps the largest
collection of stories in the House, while Walke
has never been suspected of a single one.
Some men never know when to stop-
especially the man whose front name is Donald and
whose back name is Fraser. One, answering to
that description, after running for eight years a
sort of branch of Dr. Charles Strong’s Australian
Church at Newcastle, has quitted it to take up
the medicine course at Sydney University.
Already he has graduated as Master of Arts, and
has also passed through courses in Divinity and
Philosophy. Arts, Divinity, Philosophy, and
Medicine—and the man is still young, so there’s
yet a chance for Music and the Sanitary Plumb-
ing course at the Technical College.
Francis Fitzgerald, who has been made
Recorder of Newbury, England, is a grandson of
John O’Shannassy, the Victorian old-time
Premier. Fitzgerald is a son of old Nick, the
beer-founder, of Castlemaine, who married “Big
John’s” daughter. Frank Fitz., after he was
“called,” tried to get work at the Victorian Bar,
as junior to Bryan O’Loghlen, but didn’t succeed,
and then cleared out, went to London, and
married into the nobility. When Big John kept
that little draper’s shop in Elizabeth-street, Mel-
bourne, and sat on the counter and talked politics
with Father Geoghegan, he didn’t dream of a
grandson being one day “ His Honor.”
“ War E.” : Looking at that portrait of
Gregan M‘Mahon as Cavendish Howley, I cannot
realise that it is the gentle-featured youth who
used to worry Greek aorists and dithyrambs with
me in ’9O. It was at a R.C. institution which
always made it a rule to fire a polyglot programme
at Dan O’Connor, Ned O’Farrell, and other great
Romans on “Speech Day.” Gregan and I were
elected to bombard the unfortunate audience
with about a yard and a half of Greek dialogue,
and went into hard training for it during a course
of five weeks. But I took my holiday a day
earlier than the advertised date, and left poor
Gregan to apologise to Danyell Pericles Demos-
thenes for being unable to dig up the ancient
“Grakes” for his benefit, owing to the “sudden
Sndisposition ” of the other performer !

Aboriginalities.
“ Blackbutfc ” : Be par. (8., 25/2/’O4) on
the early extinction of the lyre-bird. Agree that
the beautiful lyre is becoming extinct in many
parts of Grippsland, but your paragraphist could
not have spent much time amongst the fern glades
of Gippsland, or he would know that the cock
lyre bird roosts as high as any of the feathered
flock. I have been paling-splitting far back in
the haunts of the lyre, and have always noticed
(about dusk or a little after) the cock bird light
out for its lofty perch. It starts on the lowest tree
and works its way to the highest, often beginning
its climb a quarter of a mile from its final roost.
It is never known to come down the way it goes
up, always descending in another direction at
about the same angle. It is in the daytime that
the greatest destruction of lyres occurs, more so
with the hen bird than with the cock. The hen
always warns the cock bird when it sees any
danger, and will stay making a great noise long
after the husband has gathered up its tail and fled
into the next parish.
“ Wilmot W.”; Until recent years, per-
sons desirous of securing land on the
foot hills of Kosciusko had to
travel to Albury and other
distant towns, 100 miles
or so. and sometimes,
in the long ago, the trip
cost the selector almost
as much as the land is
worth to-day. Twenty-
seven years ago an old
friend of mine decided
to sling gold digging
and become a cocky.
He rode into Albury
and hitched himself on
to a 40-acre block of land
in the Tumbarumba district,
Eaid £lO for the privilege of
ecoming a land-owner, and
another £lO for beer, food,
and other ex.’s on the trip.
He has since made the block
freehold, and grubbed three-
parts of it; most of it cost
£8 per acre for fencing,
clearing, etc.; and the land,
which cost ss. an acre 27
years ago, is for sale to-day,
and ss. an acre is the best
offer for it. Another block of
land in same locality, which
must have cost the first owner
nearly 30s. per acre, was sold
t’other day. One shilling per
acre was reserve price. It brought
ss. ; old hands say it was dear at
that. Two hours ride on a slow
horse—almost a dead horse—
from Tumbarumba, nearly in a
line with Mt. K., a man can
strike the finest land in Austra-
lia. Have heard men who’ve
travelled Australia from end to
end say one station on Upper
Murray is the pick of the Southern
Hemisphere. And this was sold in the
first place at the same price as the messmate
and stringy bark hills. There was mighty little
discrimination or classification about those old
Governments of N.S.W.
“ War E.” : Since the break-up of the
drought have noticed several varieties of weeds
which are new to this (Junee) district. In my
“cabbage-patch” I have three fresh weeds
flourishing. One has a pretty magenta flower
of the poppy type; another is a creeper with a
yellow blossom, and the third is a succulent-
leaved plant with a root as tenacious as Dowie.
The local theory is that the seed has been carried
from “somewhere” on the dust-storms of the
drought, and germinated when the rain fell.
Another pest which is making rapid headway
here is the “Californian thistle”—a thick-
growing, spiky variety, which, if properly trained
as a hedge, will knock out the barb-wire fences in
one act.
• * • •
“ A.L.M.H.” : The coolness of the summer
seems to have completely deceived the swallows
about Sydney. Though a large number of these
birds habitually remain during the winter, the
majority migrate. The city swallows usually
assemble towards the end of March, collecting in
hundreds about George-street, Haymarket, near
Mick Simmons’, and leave early in April. This
season, however, they gathered together in large
numbers at the end of December—the telegraph
wires being literally crusted with them. They
were much excited, and were apparently holding
indignation meetings every evening. Probably
they were discussing the formation of a union
against the tyranny of the weather. Finally they
must have decided that the evil was irremediable,
for f-bey have nearly all disappeared, presumably
northward.
To say nothing of Commissioner M'Kic in h[?]
pursuit of King Billy!

“ Ed.” on fires in crops and other
matters:
A fire started in the middle of a paddock of standing
wheat. This was the first crop from the paddock, the
rung timber having been burned off in autumn. After
the fire the roots from a burnt out stump were found to
be still burning 20ft. from the stump’s old position. The
roots had
smouldered
while the crop
was growing,
and when the
wheat was ripe
and dry it took
fire. Wheat in
the same dis-
trict (North-
eastern Vic.)
was being con-
tinually stolen
from the
stacks of grain
in the pad-
docks. One
cocky hid by
his wheat
stack. About
midnight a
dray pulled
up, hacked
into the fence,
and two men
came out.
They lifted a
bag of wheat
and were tak-
ing it to the
dray. The
cocky chal-
lenged them.
They told him
if he inter-
fered they
would burn
him out. As
he still had 200
acres of stand-
ing crop he let
them go. In
the township
near by one
man had an
acre and a
half under
wheat, and he sold, ostensibly from that area, a total
of 132 bushels. While living in this township I was
told of the wheat-burner’s tactics. The neck is cut off a
bottle, inside is placed a lighted candle, fine light stiaw
is placed in the cut bottle touching the candle at the
base. This is placed inside the ripe crop. When the
candle burns down the straw is lighted, the blaze spread-
ing to the surrounding crop. This gives the burner time
to be dead-drunk in a far-away pub.

“.D’Harum ” : I credit the iguana with
having sound common-sense. I gained
this impression the first time I came in
contact with one. It was when I was
doing a long coach journey in Nor-
thern S. A. I was fresh from the
city then. The other fellows were
a jovial lot, and they played on
me what is, I believe, a com-
mon joke in the Never-Never.
One part of the journey we dis-
embarked, and walked through
the scrub. Two of the fe lows
set the dogs to work worrying
iguanas. Presently they got a
big one moving. It turned
round, faced the dogs a moment,
and then rushed towards me.
There was no stick handy, so 1
ran, closely followed by the iguana,
and behind him the dogs. Oh,
horror ! Suddenly, I felt it clutch
at my trousers, and then run up
on to my shoulder. The other fellows
stood off and howled with mirth.
I shouted, “Come here, you fools,
and take this thing off. ’ They only
laughed some more, so I fled back to the
coach with the iguana writning, and
spitting at the dogs from my shoulder. At
length the dogs left the lizard and me alone,
and then it allowed me to take it from my
shoulder, and place it on the ground. If an
iguana is being worried by a dog it will in-
variably rush to a human being for protection if
one happens to be in the neighborhood. It
isn’t particular either, but would climb on
an alderman or a monarch us readily as
on a Labor member.

a slush-lamp ?

“Sox”: Am afflicted with a desire to
prove things, and—having read “Prooshan’s”
par. (B. 25/2/’O4) re the inability of bulldog ants
to sting if the breath is held—l sought “bully
in his native lair and began experimenting. Got a
particularly warlike bull on to a stick, and thence
on to my finger. Held my breath for all 1 was
worth, and awaited developments. Didn’t have
to wait long either. The next few minutes I
occupied in slaying “bully,” cursing “ Prooshan,”
and sucking my finger. Afterwards it struck me
that “Prooshan” must have meant the experi-
menter to hold the bulldor/s breath, in which case
he may be right in his assertion, otherwise I point
the stung finger of scorn at him, and aver that he
is talking through his eye. [The Bulletin
opines that this bulldog-breath business may be
something like faith-healing. If you aren t
healed you haven’t enough faith ; if you are stung
you didn’t hold your breath hard enough. N"
man knows that he is really holding his breath for
all he is worth unless he bursts.— Ed. B.]

CARROTTY SAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Carrotty Sal was a servant gal, who worked round the
Horseshoe Bend,
And Betsy Jane (who was fast and vain) in a pub. at the
western end.
Now, Carrotty Sal loved Nuggetty Ilal, and Nuggetty
Hal loved Sal ;
And you wouldn’t have found, in the country round, a
happier boy and gal.
Betsy Jane (who was fast and vain) of lovers had quite a
score,
But her heart was wide, with room inside, to easily stow
some more ;
While Carrotty Sal, the servant gal, had only her Nuggety
Hal,
So I think you’ll say, when you’ve read this lay, that she
was an ill-used gal.
For Betsy Jane (who was fast and vain) and a hussy both
bad and bold,
Would leer and fleer as she jerked the beer and collared
the shining gold.
Nuggety Hal, along with a pal, to the pub. went in one
night,
And drank and drank till he filled his tank, and got, well,
blooming tight.
'then Betsy Jane (who was fast and vain), she wunk with
her bold bad ey'e,
And Nuggetty Hal forgot Carrotty Sal, and his pal who
was standing by.
And he said : “ Sweet Jane, though you’re fast and vain,
you’re the girl that was meant for me.”
And he clasped her tight on that self-same night, and
dandled her on his knee.
On many a night when the stars shone bright, and the
Moon on the earth did smile,
Nuggetty Hal and the barmaid gal would together the
hours beguile;
They billed and coo’d, and kissed and woo’d, and Hal
drank beer and gin,
And hugged and quaffed, and laughed and chaffed, and
revelled in wine and sin,
While Carrotty Sal, the servant gal, around in the Horse-
shoe Bend,
Sat all alone on the cold hearthstone and prayed that
her Hal might wend
His steps once more, as he did of yore, to the hole in the
garden wall,
And clasp her charms in his brawny arms as into them
plump she’d fall.
But Hal, alack ! Such a sliding back there never was
seen before—
Having once gone wrong he sung him a song, and longed
to go wrong some more ;
If one girl was good, as he understood, two must be
better still.
“I’ve been false to Sal,” said Nuggetty Hal, “and the
maid of the whisky mill *
“She reckons she’s spry, but I guess I’m ‘ fly,’ so I ’ll go
back to my Sal;
With two strings to my bow, I’ll be right—ho !ho ! and
never without a gal.”
So Nuggetty Hal went back to his Sal and she fed him
on giblet pie,
And drew from his lips love’s fond, sweet sips, the while
she would long and sigh.
And Betsy Jane (who was fast and vain) drew him many
a foaming beer ;
Hal wasn’t a flat, so he waxed quite fat, as he should on
such varied cheer.
But the Cherub aloft, that we’ve heard of so oft, must
have suddenly gone to sleep ;
And Hal and his schemes, and his blissful dreams, were
knocked in a blistered heap,
For an envious pal told Carrotty Sal of the doings of
Nuggetty Hal ;
And Sal swore a swear that she’d tear the hair off the
head of that barmaid gal.
So late one night, when the gas burned bright, she
waltzed into the whisky show ;
And Hal in his fear, he spilled his beer, as he turned
through the door to go.
But Sal was game as her carrotty name, so she dotted the
bold bad eye
Of the barmaid wench, who fell on a bench, and “ Mur-
der ! ” began to cry.
Sal spoiled her face, ’ere she left the place, then went
home to her domicile,
And Betsy Jane came to again, and yelled for Sal volatile.
Now' Nuggetty Hal, has lost each gal, as well as the beer
and pie ;
A bloke forlorn, from night till morn, he’ll fume and he’ll
fret and sigh.
And the moral is plain, if a Betsy Jane should happen to
cross your track —
Be you Bill or Hal, fly back to your Sal, or your head and
your heart may crack.

FAIRFAX & ROBERTS, Manufacturing: Jewellers, 23 HuiltCf St*
The aggregation of plumbers’ waste and
scrap-iron that serves Victoria for a railway
system keeps on distinguishing itself in its own
peculiar way. It made a special effort to break
its record a while ago, and succeeded as well as
if Bent and Irvine did the firing and driving
respectively, what time Raven Shiels struggled
with a dilapidated brake in the guard’s van—
though heaven knows Victorian trains need no
braking; they stop of their own accord. A
western-district grazier hired an alleged “special”
to shift a couple of thousand ewes, the same being
on the verge of a general lambing ; and, after
much frantic telegraphing, &c., got the disguised
rubbish-heap of a locomotive to the station he
wanted to load at. When the sheep had been
trucked, the concern refused to budge with its
freight for two hours, what time the local black-
smith and a sewing-machine repairer, and several
other artisans, lent a hand to urge the exhausted
apparition along. Finally it got going, to break
down again and stick for seven solid hours on a
plain 40 miles from anywhere in particular. The
sheep, crowded into an insufficient number of
trucks, were lambing and dying by dozens. By
the time another engine arrived to haul the outfit
along fully a third of the ewes were defunct, and

* * * *
Lately there was seen on a droughty,
dusty West Queensland track a family trudging
along—father, mother, and eight kiddies, some of
the youngsters shod and some shoeless. Dad
carried the big swag. Mother humped the baby
and part of the furniture. The rest of the kiddies
each carried a share of household belongings, down
to the three-year-old who battled along with a
billy in his fist. What rendered the sight more
remarkable was the fact that in Queensland a
woman is hardly ever seen on the track, though
she isn’t uncommon as a “swaggie” in parts of
N.S.W.
* * * *
The lovely water-hyacinth is taking un-
broken possession of a great many South Queens-
land lagoons and water-courses. A few weeks
ago one local body paid £9, just to have the afore-
said weed cleared away sufficiently to make a
drinking place for cattle.
“ Flabby Paul ” writes concerning the
huge crocodile (locally “alligator”) pictured in
this issue:
The way we make dum-dum bullets up here in N.Q. is
by filing the point of an ordinary bullet till the lead
shows through the nickel and then filing a cross on the
point. The bullet will spread, on striking, to size of a
half-crown and is mostly used for shooting 'gators. I
enclose photo of one killed at Geraldton (Johnstone
River, Q.) with one of these bullets by a man who had
never used a rifle before and who all but missed, the
bullet travelling just low enough to sever the spine. The
reptile was 18ft. 6in. long and weighed just over a ton.
He met his fate at a spot where the local youngsters
bathe, a circumstance which probably accounted for his
presence there. One Geraldton man has, during the past
11 years, slain over 100 ’gators, ranging from one to 16
feet in length.

“A. Zel wood ” rises to contradict :
“ Ed.” (Bulletin 25/2/’O4) gets in a good many wides in
his over.
1. The Haunted Hill between Moe and Morwell is not
a stringy-bark ridge, but messmate and peppermint—an
important distinction to persons using the respective
barks for roofing.
2. “No animals or birds are ever found in its vicinity."
All local native fauna and not a few domestic cats gone
wild are to be found there, poverty of the soil preventing
the close settlement which elsewhere leads to their
extinction.
3. “ Cattle camped in the vicinity invariably break
away. ” This may have been true of former days, but
the explanation is most likely found, not in the “ rum-
bling ghost,” but in the promptings of hunger. Bayonet
grass, ferns, heath, etc., don’t urge cattle to stay.
Nobody now camps there with stock. Drovers always
camp either at Moe on the one side or at Godridge (Mor-
well River) on the other. Paddock accommodation
available at both places. (This is not an ad.)
4. “ There is always a dull rumbling on calm nights.”
This is most audible when the train to Sale is puffing
along. “Ed.” could have heard a good deal of grum-
bling, as well as rumbling, since Bent’s trains have to
make two or three attempts to get over. Last Xmas Eve
the passenger train had to be taken from Moe and Mor-
well in two sections. Bent’s train is the only thing that
rumbles thereabouts
* * * *
Slackness of trade and extra-keen com-
petition is having a demoralising effect on the
liquor dispensed in Kalgoorlie nowadays, and
strange brands of snake visionaries haunt the
streets. The youthful Clerk of Courts has re-
cently experienced some hair-raising interviews
with self-confessed homicides and weird terrors of
the West. One individual displayed an assort-
ment of keys, pebbles, brass wheels, and other
samples of ostrich food which he asserted were
rubies. He accused a villainous butcher of steal-
ing one of the most valuable. He also expressed
his intention of presenting one to a lady of his
acquaintance, and if she refused to marry him he
promised to slay her, and bring the remains before
the court. “I have murdered nine persons in two
days,” he declared, while the opportune policeman
was dragging him off the premises. Same day
the clerk was interviewed by another wild-eyed
man who wanted a summons against the devil,
because his house was besieged by a horde
of demons who were trying to break in and
murder his wife and family. A steady stream
of characters like this passes through or wanders
round Kalgoorlie. The lonesome far-back fields
are beginning to ripen their crop of lunatics by
now, and when the loony speck-hunter comes into
town the deteriorated liquor finishes him.

IMPORTED BLESSINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was once an imported fox
Who said “ ’Tis as plain as a box
That the country’s resources
Are immense, and the courses
Of those who neglect them are rocks.
There was an old sparrow whose cry
Was “The importer man’s business shan’t die,
’Twould be a shameful decree,
Since he imported me,
Which was excellent business, say I.”
There was a great grasshopper green
Who said it could plainly be seen
That Providence meant
Him to pass over Lent.
Such crops! and an appetite keen.
There was a young rabbit, who said,
“ This land lies all empty and dead,
But for cattle and sheep
And people ; I’ll keep
My end up and fill it instead.”
There once was a Maoriland Moa
Who believed in a free, open door.
“ Let ’em all come,” he said.
Just now he is dead,
And you still find his bones by the score.
M.L.

The long, weary hunt of the bogus Machine
Shearers’ Union by the Australian Workers’
Union was rewarded with a little success last
week. Various M.S.U. officials and friends were
fined for refusing to give evidence before a Royal
Commission to inquire into the M.S.U. and its
doings ; some were fined £2O with the option of
seven days; others, 20s. with the option of two
days. This may be the beginning of the end.
But the M.S.U. is a cute sort of rat and requires a
deal of ferreting out from the legal nooks in which
it takes refuge.
“Boro-Belle”: Re the much-talked-of
luck of the drunk :
A number of dogs used to assemble outside my palace
at midnight and render musical selections in opposition
to the concert of cats in the yard. Such concerts were
repeated nightly, and finding that I could never lay my
hands on any suitable projectiles, I collected some good
sized stones one afternoon in readiness for the evening’s
performance. I waited until 1.30 and was just on the
point of going to bed when I heard one of the musicians
tuning up. I rushed out the front gate and seeing a dark
object in the gutter opposite I threw four stones in
quick succession and noted with grim satisfaction the
hollow thuds when they struck the target. I got an
awful shock when the dark object sat up and hiccuped.
He told me that Bung had just kicked him ten times and
he further said that I was his witness. After promising
to meet him at the Court house at 10 o’clock I went home
to bed and stayed there for a week.
LEILA DOUBLEDAY, the 10-year-old pianist
and violinist who icon a scholarship at the
Marshall Hall Conservatorium (Melb.).
Photo, by Melba, Melbourne.
Little Leila Doubleday, winner of the
pianoforte scholarship at Marshall-Hall’s Mel-
bourne Conservatorium the other day, and also a
runner-up for the violin scholarship, is the
daughter of Mrs. “Columbus” Doubleday, the
plucky teacher of singing, who discovered Amy
Castles and put up the money necessary for intro-
ducing the Bendigo voice to Melbourne notice.
Miss Leila was eight years old when she began to
capture gold medals at the A.N.A. competitions.
Since then her improvement has been remarkable.
She carried off the Conservatorium prize from a
number of older competitors, and promises to be
a “ phenomenon ” before she arrives at her teens.

The Inky Way.
Probably without knowing: that he did it, the police
magistrate at way-out White Cliffs (far west N.S. W.), gave
a decision the other day which, if it could be followed out
to the end, would change the whole complexion and tone
of journalism. Somebody had written to the local paper
that the trustees of the village common had permitted
ditchers to run mobs of sheep on the land without regis-
teiing-, while miners and others were compelled to pay
tor a single horse oi" goat. The truste es said that they
nad, through this libel, suffered in their reputations, and
that their usual peace of mind had been damaged, and
that when they went up for re-election to the progress
committee lately they had all been cast out upon a dry,
dusty world ; also they wanted £lO a-piece. The P.M.
cut it down to £1 and made an order for that and costs.
How much he would order the S.M. Herald to pay each
Federal Minister if it accused the Ministry, as a body, of
gross inhumanity towards a shipwrecked crew, amounting
to something like attempted murder, The Bulletin does
not know, but presumably it would be something con-
siderable. He might also be expected to order something
handsome to workmen who had been comprehensively
and untruthfully referred to as loafers and thieves and
drunkards. And, in the main, the P.M. would be right.
As far out in the wilderness as White Oliffs you can’t
always find the Statutes for dust; but you can get the
cardinal principles of justice under almost any mulga.
* * ♦ *
“ Alba ” : The newspaper owner out back is oftimes a
shark and a quicksand when the occasion offers. Self
and partner got stranded at Broken Hill some years ago,
but managed to save 15,000 envelopes from our miscel-
laneous stock. Sold our waggon-horse and pinched
to a place which it might be libellous to mention. It was
the Last Tank, the western hob of Sheol. If you don’t
do business the first day you cultivate a limp and keep
the sun on your left shoulder till you reach Hungerford,
or Bourke, or the Vale of Jehosaphat. We tackled-the
wily people with a co-operative advertising scheme —
offered to print their business cards on the backs of 15,000
envelopes. Each of the 15 investors was to receive a thou-
sand. Every time an envelope was sent out it advertised
the sender, and the others—and the others—we stressed
this point—“ are continually advertising you." The
idea hit the Last Tank between the nose and eyes.
We crowded in £2O worth of ads. in two days.
Then we rushed round to interview the only printing-
machine and newspaper in the place. The boss was
behind the counter, and we asked his price for printing
15,000 envelopes. Country printers usually asked ss. per
1000. The boss looked at us for two minutes, while his
nose seemed to grow large. “I’m getting tired of you
people stealing my ads.,” he said ; “ I’ve got to hit back
now and then to prove I’m not a corpse. I’ll do your
printing for £1 a thousand. If you don’t like the terms,
there’s another printing machine at Bourke and one in
Sheol. My pard drew a breath as long as the road to
Dublin, and pleaded for his life and mine. I saw by the
look in the newsman’s eye that similar death-scenes had
been enacted in his office. Nobody could withstand my
pard’s pathos. The boss softened. “ Oh, well, I’m sorry
you’re hard-up ; I’ll do the job for £l4 10s. Where are
the envelopes?” My pard called him a “Blotched
porker” and a “Septic tank.” The boss stood at the
door and jeered us. “Mind the crows, boys!” he
shouted ; “ You’ll meet them up river singing ‘ Broke !
Broke 1 Broke !’ ” Went back to our pub. and explained
matters to landlord. He bade us be of good cheer, and
pointed out an old shed in the far distance,
where Jimmy the Eye-opener kept guard over an old
printing press. The shed was a mile from the
pub., but we ran it in six minutes, and found
Jimmy mending a fishing line. In a corner of the shed
was a mammoth hand-press with a lever that hung out
like amainyard. We found some type in an old tar-bucket,
and some ink. My pard, who was an ex comp., got to
work on the scrap-iron forme and jammed it into shape
with bars and bolts and bits of tin. Next day we pulled
off a proof. I placed an envelope on the slab, while my
pard rushed the lever round. “Crash! Thud!” Pard
wiped the sweat from his brow. “One!” he shouted.
Then he raced round the shed, the boom-like lever
pressed to his ribs, the dust from the floor lifting arid
choking him as he ran. “Whirr! Smash!” I said
“ Two,” dispassionately. The heat of the shed seemed to
settle on our ears and spine. My pard looked at tne
steadily. “Fourteen thousand nine hundrtd and ninety-
eight to print,” he said. “I’d sooner starve. ” I begged hiin
to consider the circumstances. Jimmy the Eye-opener
appeared suddenly, accompanied by a villainous-looking
Chow. The Chow looked at the printing press and
smiled. “Me welly good plinter. You tl.v me.” We
gave him the job, and watched him as he shot
round the shed while the printed envelopes fell in showers
from the press. Pard and I went to dinner. We looked in
occasionally, and found Ah Sing mangling our envelopes
with lightning energy. Sweat scoured his face as he
chased the huge lever round the shed. My pard mixed a
few hundred blank envelopes with the printed ones care-
lessly. “ Save time,” he said. But the Chow objected.
“ Him no welly honest. You takee people down”; and
he picked out the blanks and printed them honestly and
conscientiously. Next day the Chow had the envelopes
parcelled neatly, and we delivered them without a hitch.
On the coach my pard said, “ Did you notice how the
Chow stacked the printed envelopes? He did the
* verandah ’ trick. Printed about 700 out of the 15,000.
Yet whichever way you opened the packet you took out
a printed one. Sharpers do it with a pack of cards.
Thought it better not to mention it till we’d collected
the cash.” We had paid that Chow £4los. for his labor,
* * * *
The Echo (Melb.), a Seventh Day Adventist concern, is
winding up business and selling off all its old stock
and plant, blocks, etc., cheap. The reason given
is that the management believes that the end of the
world is coming next year. At that rate what good will
the money do to the co. ? And is it a fair thing to sell to
the unsuspecting sinner metal goods which will melt
within 12 months ?
* * * *
When the present unsatisfactory method of conducting
Sunday-school work was being discussed at the Maoriland
Congregational Union conference, a reporter at a side
table dropped his little bit of pencil and harangued the
clerics largely and loosely on the question. He told them
their plain duty in the matter, and pointed out just what
they ought to do. The amazed sin-shifters heard him to
the bitter end, and then adjourned in a state of partial
insensibility.

From a Kawhia (M.L.) paper: “Our composing staff
caught the mumps at the school picnic last Saturday,
and has been off duty' ever since. This issue has been set
up and printed, with but little assistance, by Piki Poiirna,
a Maori lad, a student of St. Stephen’s Colli ge, who has
been in a printing office for a fortnight only.”
* * * *
The sweeping win of the S. Australian Laborites in the
Federal elections has done the local Advertiser a world
of good. Bonython’s paper did not give them an ounce
of help; it didn’t even help Protectionist Batchelor
against Freetrader Solomon. If it had given them ever
so little aid it could have sat down and smiled at the
result, and remarked that so great is its power that even
a little of it is sufficient to turn elections. But the win
of the Laborites without its help, and in the teeth of the
strong opposition of the Register, set people wondering
whether, after all, the Advertiser is the local force
that it has had the credit of being; and Bonython
began to see that his paper’s policy of neutrality would
not pay dividends for long. The elections showed him
on which side there were most pennies; perhaps the
whipping-up of funds for the new Labor daily helped
him to see things better. Anyhow, the paper has been
streaking back again to a fairly robust Liberalism ; and
if it will keep going, nothing better will have happened
in S.A. for a long while.
* * * *
British Government publishes several papers—the
London Gazette, the Dublin Gazette and the Edinburgh
Gazette —all vehicles for the publication of news of
state—the Labor Gazette and Board of Trade Jour-
nal. To the number will soon be added The Army
Journal, issued by the War Office. It is more or less
truly stated that The Army Journal will strive for army
reform, and will publish articles to show how one can be
an English army officer with a private income of no more
than £lOOO a year.
* * * •
Journalistic amenities way back at Taihape (M.L.)are
particularly torrid. One editor lately stated that t’other
chap was a blackguardly writer and a loathsome canker
who should be working in the hard-labor gang, branded
with the broad arrow, and shunned by every decent man
in the community. The reply to this occupi d a column
and a quarter, and almost laid over the original insult.
Criminal libel actions were at once commenced on both
sides, but something very sudden happened ; the cases
were settled, and the members of the little communily
resumed their road-mending in a disappointed frame of
mind.
CAGED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A little hut upon a hill between the night and morning
Was all my home, but I lived there content and unafraid ;
Tire Dawn she came to waken me with music for her
warning—
“ The Sun is marching up the East,” her silver trumpets
played.
Below me in the Harbor lay, like huge sea-monsters
dreaming,
The great deep-bowelled steamers, and the ships from
many a shore ;
Beyond them was the City with her spires and turrets
gleaming—
To me they were a picture fair, but they were nothing'
more.
But word came to the Rulers, in the City Hall assembled,
That there was one who dwelt within their bounds, yet
had no care,
No steady daily worry—and who never paled or trembled
When Aldermen were troubled and when Councils tore
their hair.
The Rulers of the City straightway sent an expedition
To take rne at whatever cost, but cheaply as they could ;
I rolled some rocks upon them, but they captured my
position,
And before the City Rulers chained and manacled I stood.
Then spake the eldest Ruler: “ He who lives’neath us
must labor,
And push and strive, and rush and drive, and fight with
tooth and claw,
And sweat and fret, and somehow get the better of his
neighbor.
He need not ask the reason why—it is the City’s Law.
“ I know your case is sad,” said he, “ but nothing comes
from wishing,
And work you must, or surely die, there is no choice
between.
If we could have our wishes we would all of us go
fishing,
And let the world go as it would, and dance upon the
green.
“ rt is not that we wish upon your liberty to trample ;
We simply act in self-defence—which surely is our right.
For if we did not stop you, more would follow your
example—
And things would not go well with men too fat to run or
fight.”
They keep me in a sort of cage with pen and ink before
me,
And when at times I rise in rage and rave against the
stars,
They seem to think communion with my fellows may
restore me,
And let me slip a rhyme like this between my prison-bars
N.s. w.
V.J.D.
