POLITICAL
POINTS
Japan reckons she has some jugged bear
cooking at Poit Arthur. But there’s many a slip
’twixt pot and lip.
It’s all very well for John Norton to shut-
down on the German band nuisance, but has he
considered the probable'effect of his action on the
British money-lender?
On 3rd March the Australian Star dug
up a remains that a wiser paper would have left in
the tomb. It was 19 years that day since the
despatch of the Soudan contingent, and the Star
hadn’t sense enough to pass the dead calamity in
silence.
S.M. Herald gave 63 lines to Mauger’s
speech on the Add ress-in-Reply. In these lines
G. H. Reid made seven distinct interruptions, all
of which are given. Mauger’s speech appears to
have been reported just where the interjections
came in, so as to give point to Reid’s clowning.
There is talk of running four Jews—Mayor
Cohen, Chamber of Commerce Jacobs, Councillor
Isaacs, and ex-Premier Sudden Solomon —for the
four Adelaide district seats in the S.A. Assembly
next general election. At present, half the repre-
sentation (Mayor Cohen and Theo Scherk) belong
to the badly-chosen people.
Says Sydney Telegraph :
Mr. Higgs, if he should be elected, may be as good
a chairman as anybody else would be. He may be even
a better, but that is not the question.
Then what is the question, stickler for public
good above all things ? Is it the essence of the
Parliamentary contract that the chairman must
be a Government or Opposition hack ?
So far as Victoria is
concerned, the Common-
wealth tariff reduced
the protective duty
on boots. Previously,
as the result of very
careful and scien-
tific grading of
duties, the State
made practically
the whole of the
boots i t wore.
In 1898 only
34,422 pairs,
valued at
£27,737, were
imported. In
1902, how-
ever, after
the Common-
wealth tariff
had removed
part of the
Protection,
78,100 pairs,
valued at
£69,585, were
imported. The
increase was
thus over 100 per
cent. Thus the N.S
Wales Freetraders’ “ triumph ”
in knocking off a few pence from the duty resulted
in Australian workmen being deprived of the
manufacture of about 44,000 pairs of boots in the
year, the money for the labor and for the leather
and other materials going to Germany, France, or
America. Yet, when he had cut down that duty
by a ha’penny or so, the N.S. W. Dry Dog was a
very proud dog indeed.
Daring a very tedious wait for the
Governor in the Senate on opening day, when
all was solemn and silent, Senator Dawson
irreverently invited the President to “ tell us
a yarn just to pass the time away.” The visitors
present laughed loudly, and President Baker
rebuked the Queensland humorist with the
dignity of an ancient apostle casting out devils.
The Yic. municipal councils summed up
the Bent Administration pretty quickly. Bent
hadn’t got all his Ministers sworn in before the
Cranbourne council came along with a request
that the State should make one of its main roads
—from Koo-wee-rup station to the Swamp. And
this is the cheeky council that is now trying to
form a farmers’, producers’, and property-owners’
defence league to resist Socialistic legislation.
The Federal election figures give another
knock to the notion that the further north a man
goes in Queensland the more tired he gets. For
the whole State 64'2 per cent, of the men on the
rolls voted ; and in every case the far-up and far-
out electorate gave a higher percentage than that.
Maranoa (where you mostly have to ride fora day
or two before you get to a polling place) abso-
lutely topped the list, and Kennedy and Herbert,
the farthest north districts, had the next places.
The tiredest people were in Brisbane and Oxley.
The Teralba (N.S. W.) miners having re-
turned to work, the charges laid against them by
N.S.W. Government have been withdrawn. It
was partly the fear of punishment for breaking
the Arbitration Act, partly the pressure of
their fellow-workers’ hostile opinion, that forced
the men back to work. There could be really no
more complete vindication of the Act; it has
proved its efficacy in that most difficult of all
cases—when workmen are called upon to submit
to a reduction in wages which they do not con-
sider fair.
The United States has begun to fool in
real earnest with problems of “empire.” It has
now notified Russia that the ‘ integrity of
China must be respected.” Some day in the
future, when the Panama Canal has led to com-
plications, the U.S. may be served by Russia and
Germany with a notice that the integrity of
Mexico or Venezuela must be respected. Large
imperial policies generally work out like that.
According to that tin-pot organiser,
G. W. S. Dean, of Melbourne, who caught the
’Pootra when the bird was moulting and stuck
stolen feathers on him to make him look like an
eagle on the warpath, the “essentials of success-
ful organisation are a policy, a leader, followers,
constructive workers, loyalty, discipline, calmness,
stability and cash.” Given these, says Dean in
effect, any fool can organise a party—-and Dean
speaks from personal knowledge.
The Employers’Conference, which recently
met in Sydney, decided, among other things, that
“ the progress of the Commonwealth could not be
maintained ” if the carriage of mails to England
was paid for by weight instead of by subsidy as
before. The conference was so childishly anxious
to grumble at the Deakin Government that it
was quite capable of resolving that the Common-
wealth would be blighted if Postmaster-General
Fysh wiped his brow with a red hanky instead of
a white one.
When Representative Edwards’ Select
Committee reported, a year or two ago, in favor
of the adoption of the metric system for Aus-
tralian coinage and weights and measures, the
Barton Government weakly shelved the matter
on the ground that Australia should, do nothing
till England moved. Now that England has
moved so far as to pass the second reading of a
Bill to adopt the system for weights and measures,
perhaps the Australian Government will find
courage enough to move also.
A pressman, who has attended many
sittings of N.S.W. Public Works Committee,
states that he has frequently noticed not more
than three or four present. “With four in
attendance, I have seen one asleep, one assidu-
ously conducting his correspondence, and another
reading a newspaper, leaving the chairman and
the shorthand writer the only ones engaged
seriously. It has frequently occurred, too, that
members look in at 2.80 p.m., get their names
recorded so that the fee will be claimable, and
then disappear.”
“TAKING ORDERS
Mr. Taverner is to be plain General Agent. He goes
to further Victorian trade. He will not be above taking
orders if opportunity offers. —Premier Bent.
THE BULLETIN guesses not.
Both Sydney morning papers printed a
long screed the other day commencing “Senator
Pulsford writes :—lf Pulsford had written a
letter in the ordinary way and signed it “Emma
Pulsford,” or whatever his name is, people would
have wondered who Pulsford was—just as no one
would know who Smith was if he didn’t sign himself
Thomas Smith, J.P. YetN.S. W. elected Puls-
ford. At least that part of N.S.W.. did which
lectures the Labor party about “machine voting,”
and tells it that it isn’t a man because it does
what the caucus decides.
N.S.W. Public Works Committee still
goes on, oblivious to all comments, drawing fees
for inquiring into the advisability of constructing
huge public works, while O’Sullivan is sacking the
Public Works staff, because even he can’t raise
the wind to construct another culvert. O’Sullivan
stands by the Committee and points out that it has
already saved the State £12,000,000 by vetoing
useless undertakings engineered through Parlia-
ment. The Bulletin’s reason for disbelieving
this tale is that N.S.W. couldn’t have borrowed
that other £12,000,000 in any case.
London papers seem to choose as their
Australian correspondents Tories who are not only
prejudiced and stupid but actually untruthful.
This, from the London Daily Chronicle, dated
Melbourne, Dec. 8, is in proof:—
It cannot be disguised that the tactics pursued by the
Labor sympathisers during the elections have been ex-
asperating to the last degree. Meetings have been
broken up in a most cowardly manner, and the lowest
motives have been imputed to men of ability and com-
mercial standing who have braved the odium of a fierce
electoral combat in the interests of moderate progress
and efficiency. However, the Federal elections are but
the scene of the first battle in the anti-Socialism struggle.
That struggle must now go on until the Labor parties are
driven to accept the direct responsibility of their policies
and programmes by accepting office or becoming the
official Opposition in the different Legislatures. There is
a long, wearying, and bitter period of internecine strife
before Australia at the present time.
THE CONTENTS.
" The Federal Parliament was opened yesterday.
When the pie was opened
The birds began to sing.
The Anglican Synod (Brisbane) is in a
pretty pickle about landing its new bishop, who
will come, of course, under contract. Brisbane
Telegraph wants to know if the provisions of
the Immigrants’ Restriction Aot will apply in this
case and if the prelate will be asked to writo a
paragraph in modern Greek. The Bulletin
hopes he will. It is time Australian Anglicans took
to raising their own bishops. A prohibitive duty
on British bishops and clergy would encourage
the native sky-pilot industry, which is badly in
need of encouragement.
That Iceberg Irvine is to be put on the
Viotorian Supreme Court bench when he returns
from spending the £2OOO cheque given him by the
fatmen for whom he did so mueli as Premier, is
getting to be a conviction in Melbourne legal
tracks. If that is so, then the spoils of office are
being well distributed. Bent gets the Premier-
ship—whatever it may be worth to him ; Taverner
gets a three years’ engagement as general agent
for Victoria in London at £lOOO a year; and
Irvine gets a Judgeship. Also M‘Kenzie got fired
out, and Robert Reid’s firm got convicted of
defrauding the customs.
Sydney D.T. drivelling over the probable
results of a Federal Arbitration Act:
It would be necessary to have possibly a dozen Courts.
Obviously one per State would not do. In New South
Wales two or three at least would be required.
Which, seeing that the Federal Act could only
apply to disputes extending over more than one
State, is so obviously a lie that it is difficult to
understand why the D.T. should insult the in-
telligence of its readers by printing it. Patrons
of the daily press need not expect the truth, but
they have a right to expect, surely, fiction of a
higher order than this idiot asylum brand.
The Sydney press was very silent on the
announcement by G. H. Reid in his first speech
of the session, that he proposed to sink the fiscal
issue. Before the elections the papers classified
all candidates on their fiscal or supposed fiscal
leanings, and after the declaration of the polls they
prepared elaborate division lists on the “ state of
parties.” But on the assembling of the House,
Reid speedily found that the scattered minorities
against the Government were not at all worried
over a Freetrade “ revival,” and Yes-No hastened
to drop the Dry Dog. Neither the Herald nor
the Telegraph has so much as a requiem for that
jaded animal.
H. W. Lucy in S.M. Herald :
The appearance in the lists of a Labor candidate at the
by-election at Norwich is of significance far beyond its
personal bearing. It affords fresh proof of the resolve of
the labor classes to push their claim to direct represen-
tation in the House of Commons irrespective of the in-
terests of their old friend the Liberal party. Students of
the times, knowing how Labor representation works in
Australia, and, in less degree, in the United States, re-
cognise that at the forthcoming general election, when-
ever it takes place, and upon whatever issue, Labor can-
didates will have direct influence upon the balance of
power. They would be a still more potent influence in
Parliamentary life if they could only bring themselves to
work in a condition of discipline under an acknowledged
leader.
The Victorian “ Reform ” crowd’s hatred
of old age pensioners reached the climax of con-
temptible meanness when the weekly dole of eight
decrepit unfortunates of the Bairnsdale district
was reduced from Bs. to 7s. each per week. One
of tbe victims is bedridden, and another is 97
years of age, while nearly all are unable to look
after themselves. Five of them were cared for
by a hardworking woman, who must now either
turn them adrift or keep them at a loss. All this
to save a tax on tbe rich landowners of Melbourne
and to enable Premier Bent to reduce the income-
tax. In 1903 the Victorian income-tax amounted to
only one-fifth as large a levy per inhabitant as did
the income-tax in Great Britain, and it is to
reduce this that the heartless and cowardly
political bosses of Victoria are confiscating a por-
tion of the small dole of the State’s 90-year-old
pensioners.
Sydney Daily Telegraph articles on
Federal Industrial Arbitration Bill seem to be a
sad case of the ignorant misdirecting the unin-
formed. In its latest display of ignorance, the
’Graph is much concerned about the troubles of
the employer who will be dragged before both
the State and the Commonwealth Courts on the
same dispute, and will have to work under two
awards that are totally different, the State Court
saying that Bill shall have a cwt. of dirt-money
and the Federal Court saying Bill must have no
dirt-money at all, and that to force dirt-money on
him will be a hanging matter. The Telegraph is
apparently ignorant of the fact that the Constitu-
tion distinctly provides that where the Common-
wealth and the State laws come into conflict the
Commonwealth law shall prevail, which settles
the whole matter. The late John Farrell used to
say it was easy to write about a subject one was
totally ignorant of; but that seems to be a lost
art in the Telegraph office nowadays.
The Philp ex-Minisfcry is commencing
to tell the truth about its component parts.
At Roma, lately, ex-Attorney-General Rut-
ledge complained bitterly that the ineptitude
of Philp and Dalrymple caused even the
Courier to turn dog on the Government and
hastened its downfall. The irritation of the
ex- Wesleyan parson is, no doubt, increased
by the fact that a few days after he handed
over his toga a Supreme Court judgeship fell
vacant, owing to Griffith’s translation. It
was a great chance missed by a hair, and a very
thin hair, too,
“ Bell Binks ” : I’ve got the name for
the Federal capital city: Kangaremu. Com-
bines the chief bird and the chief beast, and is
sufficiently misleading to the eye to make
foreigners mispronounce it.
“Saibai” writes from Thursday’s verdant
isle:
The Bulletin (21/l/’O4) on the growth of the white
labor vote on Thursday Island misses the point that said
growth is more apparent than real. One reason is the
extension of the Federal franchise to the military
garrison here. The soldiers, to their credit, voted
solidly for a “White Australia.” Also many of the
merchants and traders, with their following, resent the
leasing by the ex-Philp Government of the only useful
jetty here to the firm of Burns, Philp and Co., which
makes these merchants and traders dependent for the
landing of their goods on that firm. Consequently they
voted in opposition to the ex-Philp Government candi-
dates, and incidentally for a White Australia. Their
sympathy for a White Australia is a temporary emotion
at best.
“Bon”: Parish politics would seem to
signify debasement every time. Have been
talking to several constituents of Bent, of
Brighton, and the general consensus of opinion
briefly put is : “ Oh, well, we’ve been told he’s a
bounder, and we’ve read all about him, but what’s
the odds? When we want anything locally we
always get it from Bent. Had we stuck to
Hfgiribotham or Moule we would have had to
take pot luck, but Tommy—well, look at our rail-
way stations, ’ etc. And this sort of thing eman-
ates as often as not from educated citizens, who,
apart from this one subject, possess broad and
comprehensive ideas concerning decency, and the
public weal.
This remarkable cablegram is another re-
sult of the policy of the Stinking Fish party in
Australia :
Some remarkable statements are made* in a letter which
the Times publishes from Sir Frank Swettenham, formerly
Governor and Commander-in-Chief of the Straits Settle-
ments, and in which a defence is made of the Chinese.
In the course of his letter, Sir Frank Swettenham de-
clares that the Australians, having got rid of the abo-
rigines, now decline to allow shipwrecked Malays to land
on their inhospitable shores.
F. Swettenham here makes three remarkable
departures from the truth. Australia hasp t got
rid of its aborigines—not wholly. The natives, it
must be allowed, were mercilessly butchered in
the early days, when there were no Australians
worth mentioning—butchered by brutal Britishers
from Swettenham’s country ; but, in later years,
since people born in England have ceased to form
the great bulk of the Australian population, the
natives have been, on the whole, much better
cared for. And Australia didn’t refuse to allow
shipwrecked Malays to land on its inhospitable
shore ; F. Swettenham has merely got hold of the
lie with which the Tory papers so industriously
besmirched the name of their country, and with
which they tried so hard to rouse the world in
general against their own land and their own
people.
A PRECARIOUS! SITUATION FOR BOTH
PARTIES.
Hugh Mahon (from Westralia) sprang a
surprise on the House of Reps, as soon as Par-
liament was opened. On the motion for the
election of Holder as Speaker, Mahon contended
that the skeleton preacher was not eligible for the
position, and that he had also forfeited his seat.
His point was that, inasmuch as Holder had drawn
his salary as Speaker between the dissolution or
the last Parliament and his re-election m De-
cember, he had broken section 45 of the Constitu-
tion Act, which provides that if any Senator or
Representative takes any fee or honorarium tor
services rendered to the Commonwealth his seat
shall become vacant. Mahon argues that as there
was no House of Reps, during the period men-
tioned the services rendered for the money re-
ceived by Holder could not have been to the
House, and must have been to the Common-
wealth. As Holder was neither a Senator nor a
Representative at the time, and as it is dimcujt
to see that he was rendering any services, Mahon s
argument seems to have a considerable rent in
it. The point is to be considered later on.

It is pretty certain that sooner or later Broken
Hill will be worked on a sliding wage-scale, rates moving
up and down with the prices of metals. Ooal-miners
have long recognised this as the fairest method ; and as
lead wobbles more even than coal, a sliding scale is even
more justified in that industry. If prices slump nowa-
days, when the wage-rate is fixed, the poorer mines
simply shut up. Certainly they have to keep the
labor conditions in force ; but that is a trifle. The North,
Junction North, Block 14, and Junction have not, on the
average, employed during the past two years one-eighth
of the men they had a fewyears previously. What the seven-
eighths have done is a mystery. A few have been absorbed
byother mines; others have wandered off, sacrificing their
homes. Broken Hill is two or three hundred miles from
everywhere else; and when a man there can’t get mining
to do he can’t get anything. This irregularity of employ-
ment has been the curse of the place. Nobody feels
settled ; and everything, almost, is thus of a temporary-
character. For its size, Broken Hill has consequently fewer
advantages of civilisation than probably any other place
in Australia, and good miners, therefore, don’t, as a
rule, stay longer than they can help, which isn’t good
for the industry.
* * * *
A remarkable 60-year-old prediction of the
Rev. W. B. Clarke, the discoverer of gold in Australia,
and the continent’s first geologist of note, has lately
been unearthed ; it is part of a report for which, with
others, the N.S.W. Government subsequently paid him
£6OOO, and refers to the ranges that include Broken Hill
and Oobar: “As respects the low ranges of the far
interior, I will venture to anticipate that in those culmi-
nating points of what are western parallels of our
cordillera, and in the ocean of alluvia around, when the
white man can traverse the desert by the aid of iron, gold
will be discovered. A search should be made in the
Barrier Ranges, visited by Oapt. Sturt in his exploration
of Central Australia. Between those ranges and the sys-
tem of the Macquarie are many localities which are highly
promising. The absence of water will always limit the
washing of gold ; but we do not know yet whether veins
may not exist which are auriferous, and which may be
wrought by fire.” The advice is the more remarkable
because at that time the country was not even occupied
for squatting purposes.
» * * *
“Cobarite”: Left Cobar for Lyell. Latter
supposed to be the best copper field in Australia. Now
satisfied it is the worst from a miner’s standpoint,
hiving is very dear; conditions all round bad. Since
amalgamation men’s wages have been reduced. Men are
black-balled for taking any interest in unionism. Result:
Local A.M.A., last year, 700 strong ; now 200. President
Mahoney, one of the selected Federal Laborites for
the Senate last November, can’t get work. Three
miners nominated for office in local A.M.A. last
month had to leave the field. Such are the
conditions of Lyell to-day, thanks to monopoly.
lie the ability of Robert Sticht *. a good general manager
in his office and no more ; outside that he is lost. I have
noticed The Bulletin has given him the credit for the
divs. paid by his co., but said praise and credit belong to
Beardsley, the metallurgist, now in America. Apropos,
Wm. Knox told meeting in October last there would be a
div. in January (last month); it has not come yet, neither
is it likely to. Writer will bet there will be no div. this
year. This is chiefly owing to the local management
all round, but particularly to the absence of Beardsley.
To-day’s quotations : sellers, 15s, ; no buyers.
* * * •
South Australia has some figures available now
which should help it to size up its remarkable stroke of
luck last year. It only had a moderate harvest in 1902-3,
and the area under crop was less than usual; and it had
lass wheat available for export than in any year since
1898. Nevertheless, such were the prices it could com-
mand through the drought in other parts that its cheque
was the biggest for 10 years, and one of the biggest it
ever had. It only had 120,530 tons to ship, yet that
quantity was worth £1,237,000, whereas in 1894 156,717
tons were worth only £595,000. Its hay crop, which
was also light, nevertheless sold for more than ever was
got hitherto. This season, ip place of having 120,530 tons
of wheat and flour, it will have 262,500 tons ; yet, judging
from present prices, it will not net more, but probably'
about £50,000 less, than last y'ear, while the farmers’
expenses have been approximately 35 per cent, higher.
Reckoning in the hay-harvest, the State will be thousands
worse off this year than it was last. And the amazing
part of the business is that, notwithstanding the State’s
extraordinary luck last year, its incompetent Govern-
ment absolutely failed to make ends meet, and only
avoided showing a heavy shortage by the trick of keeping
the Northern Territory: accounts (which always show a
deficit) separate, and by starving the railways.
* > * *
An alleged American syndicate has got an
option over the Balhannah mine, one of the many
situated in the mountain range which skirts the Adelaide
plain. They all promised great things in a mining way
30 years ago. Pretty well any mineral could be dug out
of some-of these properties—gold, silver, lead, copper,
bismuth, cobalt, antimony, and others. When a co. had
settled down to work for copper, it found that
copper was “ off ” and bismuth usually the only
thing available. In its time this Balhannah mine
was worked for copper, gold, cobalt, and bismuth,
but never gave much of anything. The ore bodies
were small and irregular, like most of those in the
Adelaide hills ; and if the mines ever had a chance it was
spoiled by the pig-rooting which passed for mining. In
most cases water has also proved a trouble. Dozens of
attempts have been made at various times to re-work
these old properties ; but none of them have been more
than a fleeting success, and none seem likely to be.
Adelaide always talks in a majestic way of “ Our Inherit-
ance in the Hills ”: but it puts its money somewhere
else.
* * * •
A correspondent writes from the White Range,
Northern Territory This is a h of a hole. That
there is good gold here goes without saying, but it is so
widely scattered, and in such poor quantities when got,
that it requires that which is not on the field—capital.
Most of the white men are poor—very poor ; and some
have been here from 14 to 20 years. The old hands are
known as ‘ Gin Tailers ’or ‘ Comboes.’ They live like the
blacks, in humpies inferior to a Kaffir’s kraal, and each
has at least two gins. They don’t dig in the ordinary
sense—the farthest they ever go down is 30ft., grubbing
like wombats. The gins are wonderfully clever with the
hammer and drill. There are no lodes or fissures ; the
gold is carried mostly in the quartz, and sometimes in
the quartzite, and runs up to 4oz. to the ton, yet in
getting (say) one ton, you will have to shift 50, and after
paying all expenses you find you have not made tucker. We
are 72 miles from Alice Springs, which is 400 miles north of
Oodnadatta, and the latter is 688 miles from Adelaide.
It is a country of magnificent distances, and although
teeming with minerals, you cannot make a living. When
anyone can make enough to get out he gets."
• • • •
A new industry may shortly be started in W. A.
A survey is now being made by Government for a tram-
line from Yellowdine, near Southern Cross, to Mt.
Oandan, Parker’s Range. The mountain is called after a
Frenchman, who pegged out the property some years
ago, and tried to float a co. in Adelaide, but, as railway
facilities did not exist at the time, the proposition was
not entertained. The property consists of an iron hill
containing rich hematite and manganese. English capital-
ists have now taken it in hand. The control of the con-
cern is in the hands of “ Dynamite ” Thomson, who first
brought Nobel’s explosives to Australia 30 years ago.

A dredging record for M.L., and, as far as the
figures are known, for the world, has lately been put
up by the Electric Co., whose No. 2 dredge has brought
up 1265f0z. gold in one week. The next best in M.L.
was done by the same co between three and four years
ago—l2Bloz. The wonderfully consistent Hartley and
Riley has twice got into four figures inside four years.
“ Itan ” writes :
The outward and inward appearance of the Royal
Agricultural Grounds at Sydney is evidence that the con-
cern is more of a huge advertising bureau than anything
else. Fifteen months ago I wrote to the secretary sug-
gesting that, in order to encourage the growth of cotton
in the State, an attempt should be made to offer a good
prize for a bulk sample. The reply has not yet come to
hand. Probably a request for a square yard of ad. space
on a fence would have been promptly answered.
A sefc-back was given to the Crankbourne
Farmers, Producers and Property-Owners’ Associa-
tion at Bacchus Marsh (Vic.) t’other day. A
meeting was held to form a branch of the wonder-
ful concern. It was explained by the chairman
that the Association was formed to protect ti e
farmers against the inroads of socialism. Tin n
there uprose a party of the name of Mark Kyle,
who pointed out that the farmers had had a very
fair share of socialism in the way of cheap money
under the Credit Foncier system, cheap railway
freights, grants for roads and other things. The
farmers were so staggered at finding they were
socialists themselves that the branch didn't
form.
“Bon”: Truth is still stranger than
fiction, despite the efforts of fiscal reformers.
Was on holiday down Wilson’s Promontory, and
at camp one night had spread myself considerably
over the marvels of Melbourne. Was considered
as veracious as most men till I mentioned
that opossums thrived in their wild state
just off the top of Collins-street. How
they laughed! And yet that tale was as
true as the marvels were mendacious. Walking
through the Fitzroy and Treasury Gardens on
moonlit early mornings I see the ’possums regu-
larly, racing across the paths and up the elms.
Further, just across the Parade, and right in the
heart of the railway yards, where some thousands
of pipes were stacked, was to be found a regular
rabbit warren. The rabbits were never molested,
and used to breed like—well, themselves. And if
any country cousin wants to blood his dog on
wild-cats, Queen-street is creeping with them.
“Jim Pan.” : The Broken Hill miner can
stand being called a liar; but woe betide the reck-
less folk who call him “scab.” The miner
guards his loyalty to unionism as a good mother
protects her babe. Unionism is love, liberty, life
and £s. d.; want of it is death—moral, social,
financial, and physical. “He who steals my purse
steals trash ; but he who robs me of my good
name,’’etc. Better a dead unionist than a live
blackleg.
How the dairy farmer loves his dirt !
The President of the Warrnambool Shire Council
lately requested the directors of the Warrnambool
Butter and Cheese Factory Co. to call a meeting
of the district butter factory companies and
farmers to organise a strong opposition to the
passage of the proposed Dairy Inspection Bill.
This splendid representative of the dirty farmer
declares that the present system of inspection by
the municipal councils is all that is required,
although the Agricultural Department has ascer-
tained that in about four shires only in the State
is anything like a thorough inspection being made
at the present time.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Of Test matches, England has now won 38 and
Australia only 27. The fact that Warner’s team was not
good enough to be representative of England, and was
treated with a sort of good-natured, half-contemptuous
toleration by the sporting scribes of both Britain and
Australia, sort of rubs in the reflection that Australian
cricket is deteriorating, or that there is something wrong
with the mode of selection, or something. England led
before, but the result of this rubber gives the Cold Coun-
try a considerable start. Australia is no longer pre-
eminent in sport. Some swimming records still remain
to us, and we have the world’s sculling champion, but at
football the Maorilanders kick chips off us, the Yankee
bruisers knock us out somewhere about the tenth round,
the ’Murkan cyclists (black and white) “jump” away
with any prizes worth having, and the cricket situation
is gloomy. Still, at borrowing in proportion to popula-
tion, we keep our end of the stick up pretty well, and, to
many people, any sort of record is better than no record
at all.
• * * *
The fourth test-match was a series of shocks
and surprises. The Englishmen were, at the close of
their first innings, declared by public opinion as heard in
the street and the tram-car, to have thrown away the
match by failing to make a big score in case wet weather
came on. Fortunately for Australia they didn’t—our
licking was bad enough in all conscience. The wicket,
as a matter of fact, was on that occasion somewhat heavy
and not nearly as good as was generally supposed. The
A WET WICKET.
“Shocking accident in the cricket field. A popular
batsman drowned at the crease.”
(The above are headings from a daily paper of
that future date when the wicked spectators have
had their way, and cricketers are denied the
privilege of coming in out of the wet.)
Australians commenced on practically the same wicket,
and tailed to do anything sensational, but, although the
rain did not really interfere with the play, the wretched
light did. It was not the rain but the misleading light
which was responsible for the long delay that memorable
Saturday afternoon, and there would have been no
further play had not the umpires yielded against their
own judgment to the demonstration of the crowd.
Crockett and Argali looked at the matter from the stand-
point of cricketers. The crowd looked at it from the
standpoint of people who had paid to see a show, and
who didn’t care nearly so much who lost or won as they
did that they should get their money’s worth. Until
Sydney Cricket Ground can find some means of returning
the crowd its cash in the event of bad weather, the
cricketers seem to be morally bound to play whether it
suits them or not.
* • •
The superiority of the English became very
evident at the close of the Australian first innings on
Tuesday evening. Our men were all out for 131 ; six
wickets had gone for 34, and the two last men made
ducks. Then the Englishmen went in on the same
wicket, and, in a worse light than ever, Hayward and
Foster knocked up 50. Next day they played from four
to six p.m., and the result w'as an addition of 105 to the
overnight score of 50. When Thursday brought fine
weather and a much improved pitch, Warner and Rhodes
lashed out and put up 55 for the last wicket. Everybody
expected the Australians to make a splendid score, as all
the conditions were favorable, but the first wicket fell
for a miserable seven. The local men were crushed with
responsibility'; gloom fell upon them like a shower of
tombstones ; the bowling got them down, and the keen
fielding bottled them up. Bosanquet was a veritable
nightmare ; he fell upon Australia like the angel of the
Lord on the Old Dragon, and Hill, Gregory, Hopkins.
M‘Leod, Trumble, and Kelly all faded from before him
for 51 runs. It was awful. Noble and Cotter made a
slight stand, and the last wicket cost 57 runs. So the
conclusion is that with both bat and ball the present
English team was superior to that representing Aus-
tralia. Whether the Australian team was truly represen-
tative of Australian cricket is another question.
* * * *
Jones is still going strong as a bowler. Playing
for Claremont against North Perth (W.A.), t’other day,
he captured four wickets for 19 runs, and crowded the
“ hat trick ” into the performance as a sort of frill.
* » * *
If, as they say, the “gate” has nothing to do
with the case, and they only play test matches in order
to find out which is really and truly the better lot of
cricketers, what’s wrong with fixing future matches
to be played at Oodnadatta, or Thargomindah, or
Tibooburra, where it rains enough to damage a wicket
only once in about seven years?
The fifth test match commenced in Melbourne
on Saturday, and although the rubber had gone to Eng-
land, was watched by an interested and enthusiastic
crowd. Weather was dull and threatening, and though
there were some rain sprinklings, play went on. Noble,
winning the toss, decided to bat at once. The bright
features of the innings were Trumper’s display and Hop-
kins’ magnificent slogging. Duff and Hill had vanished for
9 and 10 respectively, but Trumper slogged on for lhr.
50min. and knocked up 88, half of which were tourers. He
was caught by Braund off his own ball. Noble’s play was
decidedly respectable, but had no devil in it. Hopkins
provided the spicy part of the show by banging
one from Braund right into the crowd for five.
Tremendous applause. Next stroke he lifted the terrible
Bosanquet for the same number, and that doughty boy ot
bulldog breed gaped—he wasn’t accustomed to that sort
of treatment and shortly afterwards retired. The bowling
of both Hirst and Braund troubled Hopkins sorely,
and he was caught by Knight in the outfield off one by the
latter bowler. It was a great innings. After that things
were dull. M'Leod made 8 and Trumble, Kelly and hotter
6 each, and the innings ended for 247. The honors of the
bowling rested with Braund, who got eight wickets
for 81 runs. The Englishmen commenced their innings
at 5.40 p.m., and had a bad time. Hayward and Arnold
were both disposed of for ducks off Noble’s bowling, and,
when the stumps were drawn, Rhodes had 3 and Warner 1.
On Sunday night the fountains of the great deep broke
up and the pitch was chaos, so that it was nearly 4 p.m.
before play started on Monday. The only Englishmen to
reach double numbers were Tyldeslev, Foster and Bosan-
quet, and the numbers of their runs were 10, 18, and lb
respectively. Hirst and Knight went out for a pretty pair
of teal. Noble got 4 wickets for 19 runs, and Cotter b
wickets for 40. The wicket was slush, and the Aus-
tralians looked at it ruefully as they went to the
stumps. By 6 o’clock M'Leod and Cotter had gone tor
nought, M'Alister for 9, and Kelly was not out with four
opposite his name. With the bowling of Cotter play ended.
More rain fell through the night, but on Tuesday play
started at 12.15, and on the wretched wicket was dispirit-
ing. By lunch time six wickets had fallen for 92, and as Tiib
BumjEtin went to press the Australians were all out for
133 and seven English wickets were down for 61. So
the Englishmen wanted 259 to win and had only three
wickets to fall. The practice of being at an interesting
stage just as this valuable paper goes to press is a dis-
reputable one common to Test Matches.
It is a sad but solemn fact that at present
cricket is the only federalised sport. An English team
always plays Australian
teams in test-matches, the
fixtures against the various
States counting as small
potatoes ; but in every other
branch of sport the States
run on their own. At pre-
sent there doesn’t seem to be
the slightest chance of Aus-
tralia as a Commonwealth
being represented at the
Olympian games at the St.
Louis Exposition, U.S.A.—
which is a pity. There are
apparently no representative
athletes in a position to go
to St. Louis on their own
pockets, and no one seems to
care a hang whether Aus-
tralia wins any parsley
crowns or not. If any men
go they will be the repre-
sentatives of their States.
Victoria proposes to send
ped. Hunter to speak for
Bent’s State, but not for
Australia. Wanted, some
patriotic men of means to
put up the money and send
a few Australians across to
try and bring back some of
the money Yankee cyclists
and bruisers have taken out
of this country the last few
years.
“Old Cove”: “Big”
cricket has become alto-
gether too serious and
scientific a game for a turf
wicket to satisfy its re-
quirements. When it was
simply a village pastime
grass answered admirably ;
but now, when it is an
international matter, and
spectators travel hundreds
and players thousands of miles to participate, we re-
quire something more stable and reliable. Matting
does not meet the case at all, and isaorude, though cheap
and portable, expedient. The solution will probably be
found in a heavy fabric of considerable thickness, com-
posed of indiarubber and fibre, with perhaps a cheap
outer cover easily renewed. This would place all bats-
men and bowlers on comparatively equal terms, whilst
the fielding, variations of light, condition of the outfield,
&c would still leave enough of the “glorious uncer-
tainty.” The present condition of things is intolerable.
For a game which is at once a matter of international
importance and a huge money-making concern to be left
entirely at the mercy of the weather is like a millionaire
habitually leaving his scrip out on the grass on wet
nights and keeping his cheque-book on top of the rain-
barrel. _ „
• * • •
Although Marmont had only a weak field to
chase him in the Australian Oup, his runaway victory
under a light weight stamped him as a first-class horse.
Given the same intelligent boy on his back, the old son
of Wellington could probably have carried another two
stone with the same apparent ease. Marmont is a free
galloper of the most tractable kind, and he runs “ a
waiting race in front” just as though he, and not the
rider, were regulating the distance of his lead. For two
miles’ of the Australian Oup race he was never more than
a length and a half ahead of his field. For a moment
they appeared to be wearing him down. Then he put on
some more pace and practically broke up the opposition.
A quarter of a mile from home it was only a question of
how many lengths the jockey would let Marmont win by.
» * * *
Handicapper Menzies appears to have been very
nettled by Marmont’s contemptuous treatment of the
Australian Oup field. From the duffer weight of 6.12 in
the Cup the old gelding was raised, at one pop, to 9.12
for the Autumn Handicap, which was 121 b. in front of
Annotate, who won the race last year. Seems that Mar-
mont would not have got off so lightly in the Oup but for
his having cut a poor figure on the flat at Flemington
last August, when he was asked to give considerable
weight to Rhapsody, Martyr and others. Marmont then
started a hot favorite, and showed a plentiful lack of
dash. Accustomed to hurdle-racing, he was looking for
jumps and wondering why they didn’t happen. Under
the circs, it was not surprising that Menzies rated the
horse as a commoner at the legitimate game and let him
fnto the Australian Oup on “ dead bird ” terms.
* * * *
To punters who have not lost all faith in the
“ Racing Chronicle ” as a guide to “ form ” Mario looked
a somewhat good thing for the Autumn Handicap at
Flemington, and they had no trouble in getting on to it
at 15’s, 12’s, and 10’s. At one time or other Mr. M‘Arthur’s
well-bred horse had performed very respectably over a
mile and a half and upwards. He was prominent at the
turn for home in last Melbourne Cup. The “ talent,”
however, bracketted Mario with stable companion, Billali,
as a non-stayer, and would have none of him for this 13
furlong race. Judiciously ridden in front of the field
Mario did the Marmont trick, nothing troubling him at
the finish except his stable companion, Falkirk, who was
a quite despised outsider. M'Arthur’s colors—sapphire
blue, and pink sash—deserve to be popular with the pub-
lic, if only for the fact that they always give a hope to
their backers. Billali, Mario, and Marmont have been a
trio of pretty consistent pace-makers. They have gone
away with the lead in a score, or more, of races during
the past year, and, although the two little ones rarely
win, Marmont is as rarely „

Emir’s three wins at V.R.C. Autumn meeting
have gone far to justify all that was said about the son ot
Wallace and Emmie before he met with his mishap in the
Sprint. He was then declared to be one of the best colts
ever bred in Australia, but not one of the best tempered.
Being eased in his work because of the mishap, the colt
threatened to develop into a vicious loafer, and now it
is a question whether his ignominious Derby failure was
due to want of condition or his straight-out roguery, ms
failure to put up a good fight against Playaway and
Pendant over seven furlongs, at Caulfield, was not quite
inconsistent with his subsequent “ form over longer
courses, anyhow Emir has the galloping action of a
great horse, and, if he is made of good Btuff--i.e., if tie
takes kindly to hard work-the handsome fellow will put
his recent performances into the shade.
* * * * .
Before the three colts had gone a mile in the
Champion Stakes it looked “ a guinea to a gooseberry
on Emir beating Scottish King and Lord Cardigan, the
Melbourne Cup winner (who made the running at a slow
pace) is also a fine mover, but he lacks the size and hasn t
the strong lines of the colt who was travelling easily in
the rear. Scottish King held the middle position. Com-
pared with the other’two the King is an ungainly worker,
an overgrown novice. He may be expected to improve
in his action if he keeps going, but meanwhile his massive
body is more impressive than his short, heavy legs, and
he looks likely to break down under the hard labor of his
gallops ere he has matured. The massive Great Scot was
a longer and better-balanced animal than Scottish King
when he ran second to Wakeful in the Champion last
year. Mr. M‘Donald’s colt finished last of the three on
Saturday, without having challenged the leader at any
point of the race. Lord Cardigan improved greatly on
his weak, sweating display in the Loch Plate, but when
he was tackled by Emir he appeared to be outclassed.
He could only struggle on under the whip whilst Bobby
Lewis shook the Ballarat colt up and won comfortably.
* » * *
Considering that Lord Cardigan’s jockey was
pacing under protest, and that Lewis on Emir chose to
stay in the rear for two miles, making no attempt to
hurry things up, it was a fair thing for the V.R.C. to
save £5OO on the race. The Champion Stakes is subject
to a time-limit condition. Unless the track is very
sticky, the club reduces the added money from £lOOO to
£5OO in the event of thb winner failing to get home within
5.45. Emir’s time was 5.45 J, and that odd Jsec., which
the winner might easily have clipped when coming down
the straight, cost the 'three owners a bit of money all
round. Second and third prizes were reduced by 150 sovs.
and 75 sovs. respectively, and the balance of the £5OO
reduction was “up against” the winning owner. The
time-limit will doubtless not be exceeded next year. In
the old days, before it was introduced, there were some
absurdly unsatisfactory races for the classic event. The
great Carbine took 6.32| for the job of winning a Cham-
pion from a weak field.
C * * * *
The two-year-old running this season has proved
as confusing as usual. Up to Saturday last the excel-
lence of Sylvanite was considered beyond question, but,
in the Select Stakes, the slashing Ballarat colt, starting
at 5 to 4, never got near to the leaders, who ran in pretty
much the same order from start to finish, the light-
weighted Ballark winning all the way. Here was a case
of the gate giving them a most unfair start. The leaders
got away in two pairs, lengths ahead of the remaining
four horses, and the left ones were the laggards all the
time.

“B.”: Told now that Pendant’s stable chum, Sea
Kale, was so heavily backed for Newmarket Handicap,
while former was comparatively neglected except by her
owner, because Sea Kale proved better than Pendant at
the weight—running 6 furlongs in 1.16 with shoes on.
All things considered (her 9.12 load up and the holding
nature of the ground) Pendant’s performance probably
tops anything ever done in a Newmarket. Discovered
since the race that winner Mairp was more extensively
backed than was at first thought; one Sydney punter
took £lOOO to £4O on the course—£soo of it from one
book. Altogether, Mairp, in his half-score of years of
life, has started 84 times, and won 20 races. He is the
only aged nag that has succeeded in winning a New-
market.
• * • *
Finish of Newmarket Handicap was interesting
in that the two nags (Mairp and Pendant) battling it out
carried exponents of the old and the new methods.
Cooper (on Mairp) is a hard-and-fast believer of the “ sit-
up ” style, while Hood (on Pendant) is an alleged out-
and-out “Tod Sloaner”; but nearing the end it was
plainly demonstrated that the last-named isn’t such a
thorough believer in the ’Murkan way, for he sat up, too
—this after crouching between the speedy mare’s ears
during the greater part of the trip.
Said that Randwick trainer Kelso’s recent
property, Antique (winner of some good races over the
flat and the leps, but always the worst of unreliables),
figures between the shafts of a Sydney hansom now, or is
being put through his facings for that big come-down.
M.L. 16-year-old feather jockey Cotton, who
scales under 7st., looks like running the country’s crack
rider L. Hewitt very close. Like Hewitt, Cotton affects
the “ Sloan ” crouch and in a couple of close finishes at
Christchurch recently accounted for Hewitt, who was up
on a better fancied nag each time.

Major Taylor has promised to box a few rounds
with his Melbourne mentor at the Queen’s Hall, under
the Queensberry Club management. The darkie has
done a lot of sparring practice of late, and evidently
fancies himself with the gloves.
* * * *
The bulky amateur cyclist, Arthur Smyth, who
breaks records before breakfast, got off the chain again
last Saturdav, and, as usual before breakfast, broke the
Australian five miles amateur record which he created
himself, before breakfast, last year. The distance was
done in 7min. 12sec. This takes a lot- of beating, but is
still below that of H. D. Elkes, of America, who, on June
13, 1902, did it in 7min. 7 1-5 sec. Somehow, the world
doesn’t take Arthur Smyth’s records quite as seriously
as it ought—probably because they are always done
before breakfast, when the world is mostly fast asleep.
* * * *
It is a sad reflection on the quality of Australian
cycling that all the scratch men in the Sydney Thousand
are Yankees. Then there is Hardy Downing (U.S.A.)
10yds., O. L. Stevens (U.S.A.) 15yds., and the first Aus-
tralian on the list is Don Walker, way down on the 20yds.
mark. Alas ! our country.
* * * *
Sydney Thousand Meeting, which commenced
at Sydney Cricket Ground last (Wednesday) night, is now
the most richly gilded cycling event on earth. About
the healthiest lot of scramblers Australia has ever
assembled on one track are gathered together to try and
rake it in, and some of the most exciting racing seen
in Australia can be confidently looked forward to. The
event is a one mile handicap, unpaced, and the penalty
for teaming is sudden disqualification. All the same,
this writer is willing to wager what shreds of reputation
he has left that the riders will team for all they are worth,
and it will take a painful amount of watching to detect it
Appeal board of Victorian League of Wheelmen
has reduced the disqualification of Ivor Lawson from 12
to three months. This is a very unsatisfactory result.
Lawson will now miss the Sydney Thousand, but will be
eligible for a look in at the English, Continental, and
American coming seasons. As the appeal board was not
satisfied that Lawson smashed Taylor deliberately or by
design, but that the thing was really an accident through
Lawson trying to get the inside running before getting a
clear length ahead, the sentence seems excessive,
especially in the face of the fast that Taylor handles his
own wheel in a manner highly conducive to accidents.
* » * *
This year’s Fort-street School swimming carnival,
at Farmer’s baths, Sydney Domain, was the most successful
ever held. The day was perfect, and so were the arrange-
ments. The winning of the 100yds. championship of
N.S.W. by Cavill was expected, and was another illus-
tration of the fact that brains count for a lot in athletics.
The rapidity with which Dick turns is marvellous, and
neither Healy nor Wickham could compete against him
in suddenness. Wickham got in front at the start, but
Cavill made it up in the turn. In the last turn Healy and
Cavill touched simultaneously, but again the champion's
lightning reversal of position sent him ahead ; he seemed
to turn suddenly right over Healy and flash away as if
driven by machinery. He won by and Cecil Healy
beat Wickham by 4ft. Cavill’s time, 58 l-ssec., was 1-5
better than his own official record, but 1-5 slower than
his own time made a few weeks back which has not yet
been officially recognised. Healy’s effort was a very
courageous one ; he should have prospects before him.
L. Murray’s lmin. 3 4-ssec. for the 100 is the
fastest ever accomplished in a strictly school race, and is
a world’s record in its class. A few weeks ago he did the
same distance in lmin. 4 l-ssec. The improvement in
such a brief period indicates that Murray is coming on,
and may do something startling ere long.
This Saturday Farmer’s Rushcutter Bay
Baths (Sydney) will see the first carnival in connection
with the Australasian Championships Meeting. Maori-
land, Victoria, N.S.W. and Queensland will all be
represented, and the assortment of “ beautiful brown
legs ” on view will be splendid and varied.
• • • •
Final for Sydney bowling pennant, ’tween City
and previous holders (Petersham), at Victoria Park
Saturday, had exceptional green and weather conditions.
City led easily throughout and was only beaten (by 2) in
one rink. Every individual in a couple of the City’s fours
proved too much for his opponent while the other two
quartettes had a close thing each head. Winners were
never, at any stage, so much to the good as at the finish
when they showed a surplus of 43—118 to 75.
# * * »
Broken Hill police worked up a sensation on
Saturday by raiding an alleged betting club, and 63
prisoners—a mixed lot of business men, miners, and
nondescripts—were paraded through the streets while a
big crowd looked on. The spoil consisted of £l6O in cash
and a parcel of books, etc. Sunday saw the raiders busy
again—on a boarding-house where 12 arrests were made.
All admitted that they had been playing poker for money,
and paid ligs. (fine and costs) into the revenue, while the
boss of the show suffered to the tune of £2O. Broken
Hill people are doing some hard thinking in an endeavor
to account for this sudden uprising of the force, for it is
three or four years since the last raid was made.
• • • *
Fight between 50-year old Starlight (colored)
and the youthful Jack Willis, in Melbourne last week,
was a wretched display of gross partisanship on the part
of the crowd, and unpardonable ignorance by the
referee. At present when opposed to a black man a
white can do no wrong—unless he bites him, and then it
is considered a joke. Neither Starlight or Willis had any
punishing power, but they jostled and pushed. As
usual, Starlight was blamed by the crowd, although the
white man was the greater offender. The black man was
the more skilful, and the other hung on to avoid punish-
ment. In the IBth round, when Starlight had the
better of it, Willis again leaned all his weight on the
colored man, who, with the left, pushed his man upwards
and then back. Willis fell—a well-managed stage fall—
and claimed a foul, and referee Basto gave the verdict
against Starlight. It was no foul. Starlight had to
act as he did to get rid of his leech-like opponent, who
always did prefer cuddling to fighting.

About an ancient pioneer :
Mr. David Reid, who has just celebrated his diamond
wedding at Albury (N.S.W.), has had a notable Australian
experience. He came to Sydney with his parents in 1825,
being then two years old. At 16 years of age he went to
his father’s station on Monaro. Here he heard good
accounts of Port Phillip, and with 500 head of cattle
crossed the Murray at Albury and formed stations on the
Ovens River, which he held until 1844, when he married
and started on his own account, at Yackandandah. He
started the first flour mill, and commanded the trade of
a large area. Beechworth was on his run, and the out-
break of gold ruined him in a day. Subsequently he held
Barnawartha run when the Chiltern and Indigo diggings
broke out, and he was again ruined. In 1865 he moved
back to N.S. Wales and selected under old Jack Robertson’s
Band Act, formed a vineyard, and lived on the place
since. Mr. Reid, who is now 81 years of age, was the first
magistrate in the Border district.

Died at Ipswich (Q.) last week P.
O’Sullivan, aged about 90; last survivor, bar
absentee Herbert, of the original Q. State Parlia-
ment. Paddy arrived in Queensland from the
island where snakes don’t flourish in 1847, and
lived at Ipswich for 55 years. He leaves behind
him a big family, including seven sons—mostly
solicitors.

The cheekiest thing the Freetrade Reps,
from N.S.W. have done for a long while was
to complain, the very first day of the new session,
about the advent of the sectarian issue in N.S.W.
politics. The spectacle of Wilks, the man whose
electoral campaign was one long scream about a
bogus R..C. “conspiracy” and a continued yowl
about the siege of some town in Ireland over two
centuries ago, complaining of the dragging-in of
the sectarian issue makes the old vision of Satan
reproving sin look like a back number.
Last Saturday The Bulletin received
four elaborately printed documents on four
separate sheets, all having reference to the N.S.W.
State Upper House. One set forth that the
petitions received during last session were nil;
another that the “ register of addresses for orders
and papers” was nil, and that the “register of
separate and joint addresses (not being for
papers) ” was also nil; a third announced that
the “ alphabetical list ” of new members was nil:
and the fourth intimated that the number of
meetings of certain Council committees was nil.
A few shillings might be saved by ceasing to print
all this blank uninteresting nil. Who on earth
reads the information that tne alphabetical list of
new members was bowled for a duck-egg, or that
the “ register of addresses for orders and papers ”
was out first ball ?
The Commission appointed in N.S.W. to
inquire into the troubles between the shearers’
unions comes to two conclusions—first, that the
M.S.TJ. is a bogus union; second, that its
existence hasn’t prejudiced and doesn’t prejudice
the fair hearing of any case which the A. W.U. —
the only legitimate union—may bring before the
Arbitration Court. As to the latter, it is probably
true enough, provided that the Court presumed
that the M.S. U. is bogus. But how it could pre-
sume that a union was bogus which it had itself
registered, and whose agreements it had adopted
as part of its own awards, isn’t easy to grasp. It
oould only presume that by also presuming that
the law under which it is working is a particularly
cross-eyed kind of ass.
If the man on the land wishes to remain
there in peace, he should employ W. N. Willis,
Land Agent, 21 Bligh-street as his Agent. No
results —no payments.*

If Victorian Premier Bent adheres to his
promise to take a Referendum re the proposed
introduction of Scripture lessons in Victorian
State schools there is just a chance that the result
will be a bad shock to the biblical crowd._ That
party has lately been taking a vote on its own
account in isolated places, and with itself as the
candidate, the returning officer, the scrutineer
and the three cheers that are given when the vic-
tory is won, it has, by its own account, been
gaining overwhelming successes. In fact, they
ave been so overwhelming that the public has
been struggling hard not to laugh in church over
the results. The whole thing lias suggested that
famous alleged war despatch by an alleged general
to his Government: “ The enemy lost 4750 killed.
On our side there was one birth.”

The achievements of Havelock, in India, have brought undying
fame to his credit. The defence of Cawnpore and the relief of
Lucknow are both stirring events in the history of that awful
Mutiny. The name of HAVELOCK will be remembered as the
greatest name in the long list of heroes that helped to save India to
the British nation.
And in the realms of commerce, no less than of war, the
name of •
HHVELOSK
will be remembered as the title and distinguishing mark of a great
achievement. . .
Not many years ago ** HAVELOCK” Tobacco was
unknown; to-day it is spoken of and smoked throughout the
Commonwealth.
The determination of the proprietors to succeed, the efforts
towards perfect manufacture, the almost heroic attempts to secure,
amidst the greatest competition, the finest selected leaf, the
incessant study of the taste of the Australian smoker, and, finally,
the constant daily eticrt to procure for “ HAVELOCK ” Tobacco
everything that was of the best —from the leaf in Virginia to the
most skilled workmen and the best machinery—has built up for
“ HAVELOCK” Tobacco an enduring reputation that few will be
found to question. It is no exaggeration to say, that ♦ •
“HAVELOSK”
is the best pipe-smoking tobacco in the world —best made, best
material, and best in all those qualities that the intelligent smoker
looks for.
“ Havelock” is sold in plugs or tins—Aromatic or Dark.

The interesting remains of an ancient note-
book were rummaged out of a box in South Aus-
tralia recently. After Captain Sturt had finished
his travels he settled down at the Grange, just
outside of JBefore leaving for England
he told his gardener to clear out a lot of old papers
and burn them. The gardener’s wife turned up
while the clearing-up process was on, picked this
book out of the heap, and carried it off; but, as it
began and ended nowhere, it was thrown into
another box. Later on the gardener’s son, think-
ing that it might have some value, put it aside ;
and now he turns it out. It is apparently part of
the rough original notes of Sturt’s diary during
his best known expedition—that of 1844-s—and
contains entries running from April to November,
1845 r It contains an account of Depot Glen,
where the party camped during about the worst
summer that any exploring party ever struck, and
of the illness of Poole, the surveyor of the party,
who died there, and was buried at what is now
called Mt. Poole —the grave was pictured in The
Bulletin three or four years ago. Pages have
been torn out of the book in several places
(probable Sturt used them in making up his two-
volume story of the expedition), but what is left of
it will, it is understood, be handed over to the
Adelaide Public Library.

S. Australia lias had a craze during the last few
years for transporting the dried-out farmers of
the north to the south-east. It has for years
talked vaguely about this south-east as “The
Garden of the State,” though when northern
farmers, who had probably often talked like that
themselves, have gone down to inspect the garden
they have had some difficulty in finding it. The
fact is that, around a few centres of old volcanic
activity, the black soil is phenomenally rich, and
the landlords reap great harvests by letting it out
to potato-growers at a dizzy rental for a few
months. But by far the most of the country is
dog-poor; in itself it is poorer than most
of the north, and the only advantage
it has is a slightly better rainfall. This
season, in the Mount Gambier district,
which is about the pick of the lot, the wheat
crops have not averaged 10 bushels to the acre
and the sample is not to be compared to most
northern wheats. Any scheme for transporting
large numbers of northern farmers will therefore
fizzle out for a certainty. In fact, it is difficult
to conceive of any scheme for turning S. Australia
into a great or even middle-sized agricultural
country that isn’t a wild cat on the face of it.
Arthur Griffith to The Bulletin
If anyone wishes to see the perfection of the Jack-
W ant - Chamber - of - Commerce - Oon tractors’ - Association
ideal “ Private Enterprise ” in good working he should
study Sydney Brooks’ article in the Dec. Fortnightly,
“ Tammany Again.” Some of the New York papers ex-
pressed amazement that the wealthy and reputable
classes should have supported Tammany at the recent
municipal elections, on which Mr. Brooks comments :
“The reason why they did so is surely obvious enough.
There are in New York about 2500 corporations (com-
panies and syndicates controlling public services) that
are subject to regulation by the law. Their fortunes are,
therefore, nolessdependentthanthoseof the saloon-keeper
or the owner of a gambling den upon the goodwill and pro-
tection of the city government. At this very moment
the municipality of New York is prosecuting claims that
amount to nearly £5,000,000 against various water, gas
and electric-lighting companies. The Reformers have
been moving heaven and earth to bring these cases into
court and press for judgment; Tammany, for a con-
sideration, will abandon them. If you were a director or
a stockholder in one of these companies, very much in-
tent on money-making, very little concerned in politics,
for whom would you vote ?—for Tammany, of course I
One is constantly told in New York that Tammany is
good to the poor. It is certainly good to the rich, and
the rich appreciate and reciprocate its kindness.” The
great public services must be conducted by someone.
The Labor party’s ideal is that they should be worked by
the State for the benefit of the whole community. The
“ Deform ” crowd want them run by “ private enter-
prise” for the benefit of a wealthy clique, which means
Tammany
Sydney D.T. complains :—
How is it that it is almost always men of such wide
experience and such power that alone can attain to the
highest political offices in America? In spite of the
abuses that have crept into American machine politics,
high federal posts are still the rewards of genuine ser-
vice. They do not fall to untried men who can “ tickle
the ears of the groundlings.” Americans do not jump
from comparative insignificance to seats in the Senate.
A man who wishes to have a public career must prove the
stuff of which he is made in more ways than mere talking.
Which is sheer impudence coming from the paper
which was, more than any other, responsible for
booming Mr. Reed into the N.S.W. Premiership
though he had no other qualification than his
capacity for “tickling the ears of the groundlings,”
and which helped to put such nonentities as Gray,
Neild, Walker, Millen, Pulsford and—well,
really one can’t recollect names which are mere
names—into the Senate. If the D.T. really wants
know why N.S.W. has such a poor crowd of
politicians compared with America, it can find
much of the reason in its own indecent campaigns
against any man of national merit, and its own
frenzied appeals on behalf of little sectarian
insects.

A cry from the land of the sunset and the jarrah :
Bitter complaints are being made'by would-be selectors
of Crown lands in AVestralia as to the treatment they
receive from the Groper Lands Department. In response
the invitation to visit Groperland and to take up
land of superior quality, close to railways and full of
flowing creeks,” for next to nothing, scores of farmers
have gone across from the Eas ern States. Several large
estates, mostly consisting of rough, thickly-timbered
country, alternated with big patches of the everlasting
sand, have been bought back by the Department and in
some cases thrown open for selection. Applicants call at
the Lands O.fice in Perth, and find that it has no apparent
knowledge of anything. It shunts them into the
wilderness to find a ranger and he shown round,
>ut as to w'hat they will be shown it seems
to he blankly ignorant. Then the fanner and the
ranger swarm up precipitous jarrah-clad ranges, over
rocks and sand patches and desolation, for the Westralian
hush is hungrier and more forbidding than any other
msh on earth. Should, by a piece of rare luck, some
f url.v good land be discovered, ten chances to one that it
is on an area not available for selection. In any case, the
would-be selector only returns to Perth to bump up
against the blank ignorance of the Lands Office, which
seldom can identify the piece of land that is wanted, and
can rarely be convinced that there is any' such place on
earth. Then the farmer writes to the papers and
denounces Lands Minister Hopkins. After that he
probably goes home again.

N.S. Wales is just realising on the biggest har-
vest on record, yet we come across things like
this: —
Gronq Grong. —A public meeting was held here last
week to consider the question of repayment to the
Government for seed-wheat and chaff advanced during
the drought. A large number of farmers from surround-
ing districts attended. It was pointed out that the
position of the farmers still demanded the utmost help
from the Government. The farmer had bi r no means got
to the top of the hill yet, in fact he had only just begun
the climb. The Government stated during the time of
the trouble that they were anxious to help the farmer,
and restore heart to the man on the land. Now was their
time to show their honest intentions and determination
to do so. What many of the farmers wanted was exten-
sion of time to pay the Government for the seed and chaff
advanced.
It may be all true, but when the farmer, in one of
the best-watered parts of N.S.W., with the best
harvest on record, still ‘[demands the utmost help
from the Government,” it is plainly time to give
up regarding agriculture as the backbone of this
sterile oountry. A baokbone that doesn’t require
so much assistance and outside support is needed.
Dr. Allan McLaughlin, in the Popular Science
Monthly :
The real danger to the public health from immigration
lies in that class of immigrants whose physique is much
below American standards, whose employment is in the
sweat-shop, and whose residence is the east side tene-
ments in New York city. It is necessary for us to
restrict, and debar if possible, all undesirable immigra-
tion. A jealous regard for the public weal may demand
measures and standards which seem to the humanitarian
and philanthropist selfish and inhuman; but charity
begins at home, and it is the right of Americans to ex-
clude the undesirable, and to employ whatever measures,
and set whatever standards may seem necessary, to shut
out any class which menaces the social and physical
welfare of the country. There should be one standard of
physique for the immigrant, no matter whether his
destination be the Pennsylvanian mines or the New York
sweat-shops. The skilled laborer should be expected to
possess the same rugged physique as is now expected of
the unskilled laborer. The standard should be fixed by
law by comparison with other well-recognised standards
of physique, and should be sufficiently high to exclude
all who could not, beyond doubt, make a living at hard
manual labor. The wording of the law should be definite
enough to make the medical certificate of poor physique
equivalent to exportation.

“Pacha ” about a visit to England :
The Australian has only a faint idea of the poverty of
the submerged half (not tenth) in England. I know of a
large insurance company in London that employs able-
bodied men as clerks on thirty shillings a week. If
a man asks for a rise he gets the sack. I never could
understand how people of this class make both ends
meet. Many of them are married too. But the clerks
are bloated aristocrats compared with the unskilled,
horny-handed workers. The pay of all kinds of laborers,
except in foundries and the like, is bad, and work is at
best irregular. To show the British notion of a fair
wage: A few years ago the Rt. Hon. A. J. Mundella,
President of the Board of Trade, told the House of
Commons how he had befriended an inventor. “I gave
him a job at our works, and paid him thirty shillings a
week until he perfected his machine.” Mundella was
a kindly man according to his lights, but such a speech
would be received by an Australian audience with cries
of “sweater.” And yet the poverty-stricken half of the
population talk about the Empire, and walk about in a
state of dread lest England should become “ a third-rate
power." Met a pious gentleman recently who delivered,
for my benefit, an excellent homily on religion and
patriotism. When we fell to talking about S’Africa, he
said, incidentally, that if the Boers again rebelled against
their lawful sovereign, he would put every man of them
to the sword. He was an Englishman.

Theatrical business is evidently overdone in the Land
of the Timber Ham. During last November and December
it is reckoned that 185 touring companies broke up for
lack of support, and that 8500 dismasted actors started to
walk home, with a view of going into the peanut business
or some other solvent calling.

South New Moon, Bendigo, was Victoria’s
biggest gold producer last year but its is not quite
so high just now. The disturbing element utotett
in the reef met with at 1140 ft. which the sto.ie to
pinch. That level was the bonanza of the mine, a
helped to sweeten up the rest of the quartz sent to the
battery The break affected the quality of the stone ,
hence y late reduced yields. Still these do not
mean that dividends are going to cease, nor
is it altogether certain that South New Moon will
be actually deposed from, its premier position as
plenty of stone is exposed in the mine to ensure good
returns for many a year. The outlook at Present,
however, is that yields will not be so hl K h hitherto.
There is, of course, the hope that the new make of stone
cut in the Clarence may avert a reduction in yields,
but this stone has yet to be proved by the South.
* * *
Peter Finlayson, chairman of the Bong
Tunnel Extended, Walhalla (Vic.) has done a foolish
thing. He has attempted to prove that there is four
years’ stone in sight in the mine. It is a matter of easy
computation. No. 7 level and above is an unknown
quantity and it is admitted that the stone being broken
there is not as good as the quartz at depth and that the
margin between gold contents and expenses is very
slight. However, put the quantity in sight at No. 7at
5000 tons, that at the No. 20 level (which is nearly worked
out) at 2000 tons, and then take the blocks in the 21 and
22 levels. Average the reef here at 4 ft. fch ™ l 'K ho dt,take
the average lengths of the two levels as oOOft. and the
average of stone unstoped as 70ft. in each, and it
will be seen that with the battery crushing 1200 tons per
month there cannot be anything like four y e ® rs sfc °"® in
sight. It is a pity that Finlayson decided to argue,
because the reef in the Long Tunnel, No. 21, 22 and 23
crosscuts, north-west shaft, went down practically veiti
cal, that the same thing would happen in the Extended.
The two points are hundreds of feet apart, and it has to
be remembered that on the boundary of the two Proper-
ties, so far as the reef was developed m the Long Tunnel
mine, the underlie was about normal-viz., 15ft. in 100 ft.
What makes the reference more unfortunate is that the
reef in the bottom levels of the Tunnel left the dyke.
This, no doubt, accounted for the poorer grade of the
stone. If the Extended has to look for a similar experi-
ence, then with costs as high as they are the co. runs the
risk of having to look somewhere else for dividends.
** * •
What a miserable affair is the burst of water at
the Spring Hill Leads mine, Oreswick, but how well-
deserved ! The board was changed by some of the
shareholders, who found fault with Browne, the mine
manager, for wanting to push on to the diamond du
bore that indicated settled country to the north. JLhen
when he went east, against his better judgment, he
seems to have been led to go farther than he ought
to have driven. Result, the flooding of the ltufie.
All that can be done is to pump and pump and pump, it
may be that the flooding will enable the water to be got
away sooner than otherwise would have been the case, for
pumps and tanks are both in use. Anyway, the Berry
United, next door, must profit, and so all the antagonism
of some of the shareholders to that claim goes for naught.
* * * *
Keats, of Melbourne' Stock Exchange, may
shortly visit London to test the weight of Bull’s purse.
This is not his first venture in that direction, for he had
a hand in inducing the British financier to embark in the
Ohillagoe scheme. It was not Keats eloquence
that convinced, for there is nothing of the Horatio
Bottomley about him. He is quiet in his ways and
halting in his address, yet he has the knack of ™a kln g
friends. Stewart, of Broken Hill, was the Aladdin of
Ohillagoe in the old days, and it is understood that
Hoover (of Bewick and Moreing) is to rub the magic
lamp this time. The idea is to extend the Ohillagoe
railway to the Etheridge, and John Bull is to be asked to
find fiinds. Where the money will be made by the pro-
moters will be in floating off Etheridge options that have
been picked up cheap. Understood that Bull is being
offered Etheridge properties apart from Keats lot.

Reported that -T. Bull is being tried with a
litter of Etheridge (N.Q.) wild-cats, several of which have
already been pretty well hawked round this side, while
even the Maoriland market has been tried without success.
There are some good enough claims on the Etheridge, but
they are not in the sack that has been taken to Europe.
Some big crushings have come lately from the old
Gilbert district ; the Joseph Morris (owned by Win.
Morris) sent out 3700 z. from 9 tons. Kitchener, Roberts,
and Majuba Hill, in that vicinity, have very rich
leaders. Georgetown and Cumberland districts have
also had some good crushings^-the. Leichhardt,
Hawkins’ Hill, No. 1 S.Durham, Harts Content,
No 1 Wexford (4oz. crushing lately), Better Luck
(lately 30z.), and Caledonia (20z., with even better
to follow) are looking like good shows. Most of them
are held by syndicates of miners. The International is
another that promises all right. Though the announced
results from these are genuine, Etheridge figures can t
always be relied upon. One game is to hold back the
cyanide gold, and use it for salting purposes.; that was
how one 4oz. crushing was formerly got from lldwt-
stone. „ „ '

The world’s gold production last year, as nearly
as it can yet be stated, was worth £68,135,000, of which
Australasia contributed about £18,370,000 or 27 per cent
Taking Australasia in this way as one single producer, it
more than any other country in the world—some-
thin" like £2 600,000 more than the United States and
£5 620,000 more than the Transvaal No other country
nrinvoaches these figures ; Russia, which is fourth on the
has considerably less than a third of Australasia’s
out-turn. The totals and proportions may be set down as
follow :
Australasia .. •• £18,370,000 27 per cent.

Transvaal 12,750,000 19 „ „
Russia .. •• 5,000,000 7 „ „

The increase in output during the past few years is
amarine. Even in " the fifties,” when on top of the
Californian came the Australian discoveries, the annual
average was only £28,250,000, while between 1881 and
1890 the average fell just short of £19,000,000— but little
more than the present production of Australasia alone.
It is interesting to recall in this connection that in the
fifties there was such a scare regarding, the increasing
production of gold that in many leases it was provided
that payment might be required to be made in silver.
The present big gold production has, however, done
silver a bad turn in this way : it has kdled stone-dead
the agitation for bimetallism, which was based chieflj on
the shortage of the gold supply.
