Cracked mirrors in the dressing-rooms of a Melbourne
theatre bear witness to the broils and throwing of hard
objects that made up the doings and spare time ol a
swagger dramatic company not long departed to Jturope.
Thomasllorton, alleged to have shot his wife at Adelaide
on Feb. 27, is a pretty expert juggler, and he tried to run a
season at the Tivoli in that city some months ago. The
people did not come along, and the modest week which
he aimed at filling was a cruel frost. He got money from
his mother and went to England to try his hand as a
juggler there, but once more struck an unappreciative
world.
Oirnoc, the man in the Rickards show who laughs at
locks and escapes from handcuffs, has been striking some
trouble in the West. This side the police couldn tbe in-
veigled into trying their skill upon him ; over in Perth
they were not so stand-offish, and handed out a handcuff
which Oirnoc barred, on the ground that it wasn t of
“ regulation pattern.” The local Com. of Police answered
effectively enough by stating that the bracelet was
a well-advertised production of an American firm and was
in considerable use among Westralian officers. All things
considered, Oirnoc seems to have had a good deal of luck
not to have struck Westralia first.
Somebody has been commenting on the wondrous
botany of Japan, as it appears in English comic opera.
The first act of “ The Geisha,” for instance, is supposed
to happen in the middle of the cherry-blossom season,
and the second act (timed 24 hours later) brings chrysan-
themums into full bloom. But why didn’t the critic
confine his scorn to the ordinary scenic vagaries of
drama, in which daffodils and other early vegetables
usually grow at harvest time? Nothing can be more
impossible than a basketful of artificial flowers fresh
gathered from the garden by an innocent young dramatic
heroine. She sticks huge dahlias into the vases on the
parlor table, and then she makes up a buttonhole of
primroses for Jack’s .coat, and selects two blue roses and
a poppy for dear old dad.
Apropos Tanner, the freak who has been fasting 21
days in Maoriland. Ten years ago this alleged fasting
foolishness was a brief craze, and two or three of the
stupid “ shows ran almost simultaneously. Some of them
may have been genuine enough ; one or two weren’t—one
certainly; for it was found out that the ‘‘faster had
hired a wet-nurse beforehand, Who visited him when no-
body else was about, and he got enough in that way to
keep himself going.
When Bland Holt stages “The Great Millionaire” in
Sydney he will do well to rationalise the scene where
Kin"- Edward and Queen Alexandra come along to the
Guildhall. These silent characters were ludicrously mis-
represented in all respects when the drama got its trial
production at Melbourne Royal. For the first week or
two Edward was rather too short and much too lean for
the billet, and later on he was replaced by a tall, burly
fellow with a dark bushy beard. The Queen was the
same cheerful young woman all the time. Moreover,
they were dolled up in crowns and “ coronation robes ”
of the penny pictorial kind, so that the arrival of the
royal pair and their attendant courtiers reminded one of
the comic House of Peers coming to mash Phyllis in
“ lolanthe.” This is in open defiance of the fact that Holt s
imported poster-pictures of the scene showed King Ned
in military togs with a cocked hat on his august head.
Australian notions of regal mummery are not so unso-
phisticated that a showman can afford to present an up-
to-date monarch as a person who wears his crown and
robes when visiting the Lord Mayor.
Beerbohm Tree didn’t make a financial success of
“ Resurrection ” last year at His Majesty’s, Haymarket,
Fogtown. Ghastly Siberian-convict posters were scat-
tered lavishly from Hampstead to Blackheath, but the
promised horrors didn’t make your head swim round or
clutch you by the throat when the show was going on.
You just missed them every time. Lena Ashwell cussed
and swore and looked blear-eyed and dishevelled enough
as Katusha in the prison scene with the other drunken
hags, but when she lurched up to tidy Dmitry you only
thought “How very unpleasant for the gentleman!
The gentleman himself was gloomy, unperturbed—an
unconvincing blurred stage-figure. Londoners prefei
musical comedy to tragedy' that fails to trag, and Resur-
rection” had to put on its grave-clothes. Beerbohm
Tree, after his unfruitful “Resurrection,” essayed “The
Gord’ian Knot,” which didn’t catch on. Ran one ot
perhaps two nights. Yells, hoots and hisses greeted the
performers, first night, through all its dreary dulness. At
the end of the performance Tree tried to make a speech,
and in an interval between the howls he managed to get
out “ The author is not in the house.” Then he fled. If
Tree had been found with a pair of wings on trying to be
an Elijah the crowd could hardly have been more dis-
respectful. By way of added bitterness, the remark
“the author is not in the house ” became a catchword.
A manageress who had been doing “ Hamlet,” or some-
thing of the sort, came before the curtain shortly after
to assure the audience that Shakespeare wasn’t on the
premises.
The convict hulk Success, formerly a penal ash-barrel off
Williamstown (Vic.), is being exhibited at various impor-
tant coastal cities of Great Britain, and will shortly go to
advertise our birthstain in the United States. Unless
the old dug-out sinks, pretty nearly all the world will
hear of our birthstain. Still, the U.S. can’t say much.
It was in the same line of business at one time.
Santley, after testing Linda O’Hara’s mighty chest
notes: “She has the appearance of 13 and the organ of
30. After a few years of study she will outshine Clara
Butt in oratorio.” Which is enthusiastic, coming from a
man like Santley. If the prophecy be fulfilled, the critics
of the Svdney dailies who let fall a heavy weight of faint
praise on Miss O’Hara when she last sang in Sydney, will
realise that it is sometimes a mistake to keep opinion on
the chain until they hear what Europe thinks.
Backwash from the Chicago horror. Her Majesty’s
Theatre, Melbourne, prints on the programme a plan of
the exits “ in case of fire.”
In comparison with the pedal-propel led machine,
the tyres of a motor-cycle have to stand very severe and
sudden strains and a greatly-added weight. Necessarily
for this reason a satisfactory tyre for motor work should
be made particularly strong and stout, and be a thorough
shield against punctures. In the Dunlop motor tyre the
very highest skill and precision enters into its construc-
tion ; it is, in fact, the embodiment of all that a motor-
bicycle tyre should be- -speedy, durable, and strong.
In this issue, on page 17, appears a portrait
of that famous, or notorious, buckjumper, Dargan’s
Gray, now the property of the Martini Brothers.
Messrs. Martini have suffered much annoyance
owing to ill-informed papers having spread areporir
that the original Dargan’s Grayis deadandthat the
present animal is a substitute rung in by an
enterprising showman. The genuine outlaw was
so well known that the portrait will doubtless
settle all question as to his identity.
The Federal Immigration Act gets m
where it was intended it should. In the' "WR
land (Victoria) coal miners lock-out many of
shift bosses left with the men. The companies
immediately rushed off to Grat Britain for shi
bosses to take over the work. As a result m
arrived. The miners’ representatives appealed
to the Federal authorities on the g rou^ d tb^
these men were coming in under contract, but
both the new arrivals and their employers demo
the existence of any contract nothing could be
done, and the new arrivals went on to the mines.
They were totally ignorant of the state of anairs
there, but when informed by the strikers five of
them refused to go to work. The compames a
helpless ; they can’t prosecute for breach ot con-
tract after denying the existence of any contracc.
It is worth remembering just here that when tne
Immigration Act was before Parliament, the tat
men rose like a forest of stumps to declare that no
employer would ever attempt to obtain .
from outside when a lock-out was in progress.
The British House of Lords has got some
little distance in a sensible career—it has agreed to
the second reading of the Bill providing for a com-
pulsory system of metric weights and measures, to
come into force two years after the passing of the
Act. Apropos, a codicil to the will of the late
Herbert Spencer, who was a strong opponent ot
the metric system, and wanted to retain the
ancient confusion, provides that in the event ot a
Bill for the compulsory adoption of metric weights
and measures being introduced into Parliament
within 10 years after his death, his work,
“ Against the Metric System,” should be reprinted
from the stereotyped plates and distributed gratis
at the cost of his estate among Members of Parlia-
ment. Also that it should be sold to the public at
a nominal price.
Dowie Elijah’s early history in brief
Born Edinburgh ’47. Barents brought him in ’6O to
Adelaide, where he drove a quill in a storekeepers office
for seven years or so. In ’67 he returned to> Edinbui g
and studied for the ministry. Returned to Adelaide 72,
and was ordained pastor at Alma (S.A.). Went to Sydney
in Sept. ’73, and had a church at Manly. Accepted a
“ call” to the Newtown Congregational Church, where he
prayed from 75 to 78. Then he renounced Oongre-
gationalism and started the Free Christian Church.
In' ’B2 went to the Sackville-street (Collmgwood)
tabernacle—a wooden building behind Wrens tote
Ousted from that by the previous occupant of the
pulpit, Dowie started again on his own in Fitzroy, where
he had lively arguments—even scrimmages—with several
publicans, outside whose premises he was wont to
assemble on Sunday and urge them to give up the
accursed traffic'and come out and be saved. One came
out once with a hose .attached to the water-main, ana
Dowie’s feathers were wet. At Fitzroy he first started the
“faith-healing” industry. In-November, 87, he resigned
from his Fitzroy church and ran a nine days mission ot
“ preaching and healing ”in Sydney. Thence he went to
Maoriland where his mission success was great illumi-
nated addresses and purses being thrust on him. in
Christchurch he met a printer named Abbott, aprayertui
man of solemn habit of thought. Him he persuaded to
take- up the Fitzroy Bethel. The ex-printer still preaches
in Oollingwood, and between whiles celebrates marriages
at an agency and does a little phrenological diagnosing.
Christchu-rch. seems to be specially favored of
parsons of the Dowie genus—thence came also
Worthington. From M.L. Dowie went on to
America and fame.
Nearly 40 years ago, when the site of the
old George-street cemetery was given to the
Sydney City Council for the building of a town
hall, the contract for removing the bones, &c., fell
to a Sydney undertaker, Mr. Stewart, of Bathurst-
street. From recent evidences the work was very
imperfectly performed. When the foundations
for'' the Centennial Hall were being' excavated
many skeletons were unearthed, the same hap-
pened when the foundations for the deanery were
being'dug out; again, others were‘found when The
porch was being built, and last week bones, coffins
and leg-irons were dug up while the men were en-
gaged in digging a trench for the electric-light
cable.

Uncle Sam’s Daughters in Chicago.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
American women are nothing if not ingenious.
When they are suddenly forced out into the arena
of life they seem to find no lack of paths open
before them. Besides the profession of foot-pad
which they are exploiting at present (three men
having been successfully “held-up” by a single,
armed woman a few weeks ago) they have entered
various other grooves.
Though domestic help in the ordinary sense of
the word is difficult, if not impossible, to obtain,
you can easily secure a variety of assistants. A
“ professional duster” will visit your home at
stated hours, the clock winder will follow her, and
later in the day the plant waterer will make her
appearance on her accustomed round.
As for less mundane matters, you can hire a
charming American to visit the nursery and tell
fairy stories to the children for the modest sum of
two shillings an hour —one wonders how the
delioate myths fare when brought into the dollar
scale.
For fivepence you can have your boots shined
by a feminine bootblack, and for a coin just as
modest in proportion you can have your hair cut,
or even a shave, if you are willing to risk it. A
trust is being formed in this as in all other busi-
nesses, and a strenuous effort is being made to
congregate all the “female stars of the tonsorial
profession” beneath one roof, as the frequent
advertisements say. A short time ago, a jealous
barber-husband, having an hour to spare, walked
in to see how his fascinating wife was progressing
with her numerous customers. The result was
that the ready revolver was drawn and shots flew.
It was a busy hour in the day, the chairs were
full of recumbent forms, yet every occupant
sprang up, towels, lather and all, and dashed
frantically out into State-street.
One charming lady of a literary turn has
realised that life is too short to admit of time
being spared from calls, shopping, and the proper
conduct of the toilette, to enable Chicagoans to
read, study, and inwardly digest the fiotions
of the great masters. Wishing to supply
this crying need, she has started wnat is literally
a “Society of Condensers,” though she describes
herself as a “re-creator of the Great Masters,”
and guarantees to give you the essence of such
works as. “The Tale of Two Cities” or Hugo’s
“Les Miserables” in the space of two hours.
The last-mentioned book cost her years of labor
before she .could present it to her satisfaction in
the given time. “ But—Victor is so verbose ! ”
Who but one of Uncle Sam’s daughters would
think of claiming no less a sum than £lOOO because
some other woman had alienated the affections of
her pug dog ? Could any but an American
woman claim and obtain a divorce on the ground
that her husband came home with a single blonde
hair on his sleeve, or because he wore a wig and it
affected her nerves ? Yet these were the only
crimes necessary on the husband’s part to enable
the wives to obtain instant freedom, one in the
record time of six minutes.
I only know of one case in the States when a
idea for divorce failed, and in this instance it was
the husband who desired it. His young wife
would persist in feeding him on “ unholy and dis-
tasteful concoctions.” The judge, however, held
that she had a perfect right to feed him on what
she chose, especially when she tearfully owned
they were “love potions.” He had not kissed her
as warmly of late as he had done during the first
few weeks of their matrimonial venture.
Another case, still hangs in the balance just
now. A certain dame in Ohio is suing for a
divorce from her husband on the ground that he
is jealous of the family cat!
MAORILANDA.
Cardinal Herrera y Espinosa, Archbishop
of Valencia, who died the other day, left £2OOO
“to the first Spanish general landing in the
United States territory with an army sufficiently
strong to avenge the defeat of Cuba and the
Philippines.’’ The money will remain in the
Bank of Spain till the general makes his appear-
ance to claim it, and if it remains long enough at
compound interest a few centuries hence the
game may be worth the candle.


“Burglar Bill”: In gaol the day the
Queen died. All the grey-coats were elated,
thinking remission of sentences would be granted
by the new King. When no remission arrived,
the long-sentence prisoners started to pray for
Edward Vll.’s death, thinking they might have
better luck with his successor. Only met three
loyal prisoners—-they were all Wussleans.

OUR'HISTORY.
IN the year eighteen-seventy-one we occu-
pied a small store in Park Road, Liver-
pool, England, where we sold a watch
now and again, but not nearly so often as
we would have liked nor as often as we con-
sidered such reliable watches should be sold.
We were confident that our watches were as l
good as could be got—the trouble was how i
to get the public to know. We pondered ,
over this subject day and night and finally
concluded that we would try what the news-
-1 papers could do for us. Such a thing as
1 selling watches through advertisements had
i never been done in England before, still we
were convinced that once the general public
knew that our watches were what we said
they were—Reliable—we must succeed.
You know how it is with a man who has a
dependable watch—he is proud of it and
1 loves to speak of its accuracy, and naturally
i the firm who supplied the watch comes in for
, a full share of the credit. Well—we put
i our everything into the venture. How we
succeeded our eight splendid Australasian
* stores give testimony. Orders flowed in
> from all over the World, particularly from
> Australia and New Zealand. In fact, so
I numerous were the orders that we decided
to open in Sydney. Our success was
. phenomenal, and in Melbourne, Auckland,
[ and Dunedin we had to open shops, followed
* later by Perth, Wellington, Christchurch,
f and Brisbane, each being a success. What
[better proof of the satisfactory performance
of our watches do you want 1

Cairns.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Australians generally have a vague idea that
Cairns is rather Asiatic in appearance, produces
cocoanuts and bananas, is the centre of a large
sugar and mining district, counts amongst its
population representatives of many colored races
—and the rest is hazy and unknown.
it is a gloriously beautiful spot, and in the
winter is a Paradise. Its seas are the calmest in
the world. One can stand within fifteen yards of
the water and there i 3 not a murmur—only an in-
finite mirror stretching away to the horizon, and
all the beauties of land and sky reflected on its
surface. There are hills everywhere. Far behind
the town they tower away to cloudland—to the
right they rise sheer out of the sea to a height of
3000 ft. There is range after range of sun-flecked
hills, well-wooded and green and beautiful, and
far away is the beginning of the Bellenden Ker
Range, with its high peaks cutting the clouds.
The highest point in this.range is about 7000 ft.
high, and is the second loftiest peak in Australia.
The trip up after one leaves Mackay forms a
series of beauty-pictures in itself, and the lovely
scenery one meets with coming through Whit-
sunday Passage and Hinchinbrook Channel is
hardly to be surpassed. The sea is always like a
mirror here, and one passes through hundreds of
islands. In Whitsunday Passage they are covered
with Norfolk pines, and well-wooded all oyer.
Some of the islands are mere dots of gardens just
showing above the waves ; others are larger and
more hilly. Hinchinbrook Channel is almost
lovelier, and the Palm Islands are very fair —
carpeted in green to the edge of the sea. There
are tiny islets of granite showing here and there,
with green mosses trailing over. A strange feature
of the islands of this channel and Whitsunday is
their leonine shape. In the latter passage one
large island looks exactly like a lion couchant,
and is generally known as Lion Island, and there
is this same peculiar feature noticeable in all of
them.
On reaching Cairns the boat anchors in an inlet
of Trinity Bay. The bay itself is very beautiful,
and is capable of accommodating “ all the navies
of the world.”
The main street of Cairns has not a busy ap-
pearance, arid its buildings of wood and galvanised
iron are hardly imposing, but there are trees
everywhere, green, and wide-spreading, and cool,
with leaves of a glossy green, and great twisted
trunks. There is a greenness wherever one turns,
and the air is sweetly scented with the frangi-
panni. Some distance from the main street is
Chinatown, where there are rows and rows of
Chinese and Japanese shops and houses, and
where one hardly meets a white face.
Twenty-two miles by rail, and eight as the crow
flies, are the Barron Falls, which are connected
with the town by the most costly railway in Aus-
tralia. All the way up the scenery is magnificent,
more rugged and grand than that of the Blue
Mountains of N.S.W., with greater suggestions
of vastness in the lines of the hills and the sudden
blackness of the gorges. The falls are beautiful
at all times, but they are magnificent in flood.
About eight miles from Kuranda, the train passes
the Stony Creek Falls so closely that after rain,
when there is a heavy fall of water, the carriages
are soaked with spray. These falls, though not
very high, are classed with the most beautiful in
Australia. The first cascade is 400 ft. high, the
second 900 ft., and on all sides the most delicate
ferns grow, their fronds sparkling with silver
spray.
At Mareeba, which is beyond the Falls, the
temperature is 10° below that of Cairns, it being
some 2000 ft. above sea level. The whole district
around Barron Falls is starred with many spots of
loveliness, which some day will be haunted by
tourists, as the Blue Mountains are to-day.
Queenslanders do not advertise their State suffi-
ciently ; its beauties are comparatively unknown.
Cairns is a star set with stars. It is all beauti-
ful. From a balcony one has a variety of scenery
—a glimpse of the inlet of the bay, where the
great cedar logs lie afloat; the dark, tree-covered
mountains; the ocean ; and a green sward of
velvet reaching to the edge of the salt water. At
full tide one walks along the strand over short,
crisp grasses, with the sea lapping softly not a,
foot away. Back from the strand are dear little
homes with the wildest of little gardens, and m
every garden frangipanni, and strange little bells
of scarlet, and palms —palms everywhere, and
vines trailing up the verandah -posts.
In the hot weather business men cease work
early, and go to the baths (there is no open-sea
bathing, on account of sharks), or walk along the
strand, under the shady trees. The town looks
like some great garden, with its trees everywhere.
Under each one is a comfortable seat. Towards
evening the black boys go down to the beach and
hold converse with the aboriginal girls. In the
evening, too, the Kanakas stray, one behind the
other, along the shore, silent and morose, and
occasionally one meets a dozen or so Hindus, tall
and thin, with sad faces and melancholy eyes, and
gaily-colored turbans of filth around their heads.
At sunset, when the sea is like some great
masterpiece, with its painted hills and rose-colored
clouds, when the nearer hills are all gray or
purple, or faintly sun-flecked, and the blue, far
mountains have faded into a violet sky, the
sailing boats drop down the bay and steer for the
inlet, the shadows of the sails flitting across the
sea like white ocean-ghosts. _
And in this sweet land the white man takes ott
his hat to the Chinaman !

The Ghosts’ Play-Time.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When all the world is sleeping
(The world that lives by day)
And stars are vigils keeping,
The Ghosts come out to play.
There is a city quiet,
With houses green and low;
Black Care rides swiftly by it—
For in he may not go.
The white doors of its houses
Full well their secrets keep;
No postman’s knock arouses
The inmates from their sleep.
Each silent tenement there
Securicy denotes—
No landlord comes for rent there,
No canvasser for votes.
No car or cab approaches
Its gates with dust and din,
But black glass-sided coaches
Move slowly out and in.
So quiet is that city,
So free from vulgar care,
No politicians witty
Make rousing speeches there.
But when the world is sleeping—
The world that lives by day—
Its citizens come creeping,
Or leaping, out to play.
Is that a ground-flame glancing
From grave to grave ? Ah, no !
It is two lovers dancing,
Who died long years ago.
At rest from all their labors,
At end of all their woes,
Behold where two tomb-neighbors
Are playing dominoes !
The game is no wise vapid—
See how on his white door,
With phosphor pencil rapid,
Each spectre marks his score !
And Aged Ghosts converse there
With ardor ever fresh,
And curious tales rehearse there
Of tilings done in the flesh.
They say that Sydney City
May lie improved, but she
Is not—and more’s the pity—
The Town she used to be.
What wreaths of mist are swirling
Across the moonlit sky.
It is the Shroud Dance whirling—
The gay young Ghosts flit by.
Fear not! They will not harm you—
I know them ; they know me—
They simply wish to charm you
To join their company.
When all the world is sleeping
(The world that lives by day),
And stars are vigils keeping,
The Ghosts come out to play.
N.S.W.
V.J.D.
Bonchetti's Boarders,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
kstraTjTa in the moonlight. Stunted gum,
sand, rock, mulga, and empty “dog’’-tins. The
iglits and noises of the Slung Star Mine show in
the distance ; ditto those of March and Erek-
mann’s.
Bonchetti sat on a bag of ore—one out of a
uge pyramid of them, piled up, waiting to be
milled—-with his violin-handle tucked under his
cnin ; Morrison smoked.
Queer place for “ Masaniello,” “II Barbiere,”
and such things. Queer place for anything, for
that matter.
Bonchetti put his ’lin down presently, and
smoked, too ; a broad, short stack of muscle, half-
Australian, half-Italian.
hen his pipe was emptied, he yawned and
picked up the instrument of music. “ How
about bed, Jack?” he queried, flicking the
strings. Morrison uncoiled the yards of himself,
and ambled off to bunk like a child.
Bonchetti followed, and stowed the violin away
under his stretcher. Then he lay down likewise,
without blowing the candle out, chiefly because
there wasn’t any.
Morrison’s snore and the distant r-r-r-r-r of the
blung Star battery made a kind of harmony
together, and Bonchetti absently beat time to it
on the empty dynamite case alongside his bunk.
Presently Morrison gave over, and the battery
was left to it alone.

About one, his eyes closed at last, and he slept,
with the bare, muscle-roped arm and hand still
moving uneasily .on the packing case. Between
that time and 2.30 he woke thrice, and kicked
and cursed things. At three he sat up with a
jerk.
“Somebody’s calling me,” he muttered. “Or
did I dream it ? It sounded like my wife’s voice-
but she s away east.”
Of a sudden there was the slightest noise out-
side-just a rustle, and Bonehetti’s hand slid
under his bag pillow. It came out with a heavy
Uolt m it. J
Outside it was lighter than before, but the huge
pile of bagged ore cast a dark shadow around the
camp. Bonchetti stepped softly through the sand
m his bare feet, and glanced around a promontory
formed by the ramifications of the ore-pile A
man was leaning lazily on the brace; one hand on
the handle, and gazing away over towards the
flickering lights of the Slung Star.
“Good little girl,” the little man whispered to
himself; “ good little girl. She did call me.”
Then the rope dangling down the shaft moved
from side to side. Bonchetti slipped round the
corner and jammed the revolver-barrel to the lazy
man’s ear.
“What game’s this?” he asked, in an angry
whisper. The man at the brace yelled, and
nearly fell down the open shaft.
Bonchetti’s face hardened. “ You ruffian ! ore-
stealing, hey? How many of you below?” The
other’s teeth chattered, “ T-two !”
“That all? Up with that bucket then, and
into it, you thief ! ”
The other grasped the windlass-handle with the
Colt-end still at his ear, and hoisted the bucket.
Bonchetti cursed. “ Into her, or I’ll blow your
brains from here to Kookynie. Into her, quick 1
Try a day or two’s starvation, you thieves ! ”
The other got in, with knees clattering together
like young stampers. Bonchetti lowered him, by
letting go when the rope was about half out, en-
tailing a further drop of 10 or 15ft. There was a
sudden buzz of voices from below, and the brace-
man wound the bucket up empty. Then he laid
three or four rough-chopped planks across the
shaft, piled a dozen heavy bags of ore on top of
them, and went back to his bunk and slept the
sleep of the indignantly just.
He woke at seven. Morrison was grinning at
him with a coil of fuse in his hand. “ Takin’ full
measure, Bon. ? ”
The Italian thought hard, then spoke: “Yes,
chuck that fuse down. We’ll take a breeze to-day
and go over and see March and Erckmann about
crushing our stuff. Time we got it through ;
there’s nineteen hundred bags of it.” Morrison
dropped the fuse obediently.
About 10 o’clock Bonchetti put his coat on and
they ambled off towards the battery. Morrison
squinted at the pile over the shaft as they went,
but said nothing. Bonchetti noticed the glance
and grinned. “ That’s through your snoring last
night. You kicked up such a darn row I thought
you’d scare the shaft away, so I piled a few bag-
fullson her to keep her down.” Morrison smiled
a 12-in. smile, and said nothing again.
About 11 they hove-to at the office-door of
March and Erokinann’s. The accountant-cum-
clerk turned round in his chair.
Bonchetti nodded, “March about?” The
accountant looked puzzled. “No, he’s not; both
he and Erckmann are away. Anything I can
do?”
Bonchetti smiled. “Oh no, I just wanted to
arrange with them to crush our stuff. Will look
in to-morrow. So-long.”
“So-long,” the accountant echoed; he was
thinking furiously, and might have said “Goto
blazes ” for all he knew.
Morrison was still standing gazing at March
and Co.’s muck-heap when Bonchetti came out of
the office. “ Let’s go over to the Slung Star and
have a yarn with Charlie, anyhow,” he said.
“We’ll make some sort of a holiday out of it.
* * * *
Whisky and other magnetic influences kept
them at “Charlie’s” all day and all night, and
the boss of the Star parted with them regretfully
about 9 a.m. next day.
He stood on his dusty office doorstep—six-feet-
six of him—and waved a fraternal good-bye.
“ Hooroo, boys, look over next week. I’ll have
another stock of whisky in by then. Sorry it ran
out.”
Bonchetti and his mate nodded, and made
tracks for their own show. Passing March’s, Bon-
chetti said, “Let’s look in and see if either of
them’s back.”
They looked in. The accountant was sitting on
his table, his feet in the chair. “ Either of them
back yet? Bonchetti queried. The accountant
shook his head “No; damned if I know where
they are; funny business to slope away without
saying a word. It’s our pay-day to-morrow,
too.”
“Is it?” Bonchetti looked grave. “PVaps
they ve gone into town on the bust—the show can
stand it, I suppose.”
Damned if it can,” the accountant snapped;
then he continued, with unusual confidence, “ It
can t stand a single cheap shypoo drunk. I’m
sick of the show, and the pair of sharps that run
it. Want an accountant over at your place?”
Bonchetti laughed. “Not yet—some day,
p rhaps ; our stuff’s as thick as mud.”
* l f s it?” the accountant remarked, wearily;
we re getting about an ounce to the cubic mile.
Going? So-long.”

Bonchetti yawned after dinner, and stood up.
Damn work ! I suppose we’d better get to it,
though. Morrison smiled, and doubled round
the ore promontory to the shaft; Bonchecti fol-
lowed.
When the last bag and the planks had been
taken off, he looked at Morrison. “Lower her
empty, Jack,” he said, “and hang on to the
handle when she comes to the surface again.
We re taking boarders in for a change.” He
smiled here grimly, and Morrison saw, with sur-
pnse he was the kind of quiet man that doesn’t

big revolver in his hand.

:;che. My stomach was distended after

Along the Barrier,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To-night, along the Barrier,
With silken voice, the Tide
Hath called the Moon to marry her,
White sweetness, long denied,
To clinging Seas that carry her,
Deep-imaged, as they glide.
The yearnings of a wooer.
That no long years appease,
Are whispered faintly to her
Upon the nor’east breeze —
For vainly call unto her
These ever-pulsing Seas.
A breath of camphor laurel
Is wafted thro' the Night;
A song of breakers choral,
In tremulous delight,
Is heard across the coral
And by the beaches white.
One lone sea-light is gleaming;
And on the headland high
One shoreward light a-beaming
Beneath the moonlit sky,
Where, by their camp-fire dreaming,
The dugong fishers lie.
Now, quenched the last lamp glaring
Beneath the station roofs ;
And out of distance wearing
Falls sound of faithful hoofs—
My star-eyed love is daring
Her kinsfolk’s cold reproofs.
’Neath clumped pandanus hiding,
Two figures in the grass,
Witli stolen Joy abiding,
Heed not how hoars may pass,
Though all the world were chiding
A lover and a lass.
There may be ladies fairer,
Beloved, in the South ;
But none whose charms are larer—
Like rain-time after drouth —
But none so free a sharer
The honey of her mouth.
Yea, hearts in lands much older
Beat not so fast I swear;
For Southern maidens colder
Let prudent lovers fare;
The glowing North is bolder
On such sweet quest to dar >.
’Neath warmer skies Australian
Our blood hath warmer grown ;
Here shall no homage alien
Be paid to maids of stone,
But each inspired Pygmalion
Shall clasp and hold his own.
What woes the Fates are holding
In store I do not know,
What griefs the gods are moulding
For mortals here below —
This present bliss enfolding—
My arms around you—so.
Now were yon cutter truly
A painted ship, and mine ;
To-night in island Thule
We’d drink the red, red wine—
Like Love’s own blood pressed newly
From its enchanted vine.
And thou, my Northern maiden—
At music’s soft command—
Some starlit path in Aidenn,
Far winding from the strand,
Would tread, with white hand laid in
A sailor’s sun-browned hand.
There neither Grief nor Sorrow,
Nor either Death or Pain,
Nor grey, unsought To-morrow
Creep ever in again,—
There no dull soul may borrow
False glamor from false gain.
To-night those dull souls languish
In riches and in strife,
To-night the cities languish
With Hate and Horror rife,
And God looks down in anguish
Upon their Death-in-Life.
To-night along the Barrier,
A thousand stars above—
In tropic skies grown starrier —
With ordered cadence move ;
To-night, along the Barrier,
Reign only Youth and Love.
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
The Sin of Two.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I suppose there is no happier man in all N.S. W.
than I am now. Yet I am still an invalid for
life, and there is a look in my poor wife’s eyes
that tells me she will never quite forget the
troubles she has gone through for me.
We married five years ago, in the happy-go-
lucky manner of young Australia, whose only
provision for the future is an occasional contri-
bution to Tattersall’s sweep. My illness came
eighteen months later, when our little Archie was
three months old. It wasn’t an accident, worse
luck, so I couldn’t get anything for it—just a
plain, straight-forward, life-long, spinal com-
plaint. So I had to give up my billet on a small
weekly paper. For a few months people were
very good in influencing tome the limited amount
of pen-work that can be done by a journalist who
never goes anywhere, never sees anything, and
experiences no emotion except as against a back-
ground of incessant pain. After three months —
well, they didn’t.
Charitable persons, somehow or other, never
care about helping the same people long. They
seem to think it counts more to be rather good to
Brown, Smith and Jones than to be very kind to
Thompson—a longer list of names to hand up to
the Throne, I suppose. So the stream of charity
was diverted to a man who had got mangled in a
novel, up-to-date fashion, an a consequence of
which Ernestine and I discovered that two meals
a day were more wholesome than three, and that
there was something rather piquant in removing
to a neighborhood where the aristocracy consisted
of the few who get Government help, and whose
characters were enviously taken away on door-
steps by the many.
The background of pain stood out visibly In
these days. Summer dragged itself away, with
fierce, recurrent bursts of heat, and at last cool,
wet breezes stole into our one crowded room—a
room that no amount of toil could keep free from
feverish closeness before. So then I wrote again,
principally pathetic descriptions of persons who
got miraculously cured of some complaint by
drinking somebody’s Marvellous Mixture. I,
under different names, was always the person, and
it was always my complaint.
When that failed, Ernestine declared that it
was now her turn to work. I almost laughed.
What could she do—so little, so delicate; the old-
fashioned, lovable woman meant for the hearth-
stone only ! Certainly she had been a State-school
teacher before marriage, and would have taken it
up again but for the prejudice against married
women in that department—but, what more help-
less than a school-teacher out of school ?
Ernestine’s attempts at breadwinning lasted a
month. She tried teaching dancing at threepence
a lesson, till Santley’s Lane found a preceptress
who would include lending a hand at the wash-
tub for the same price. She tried fortune-telling,
and the sixpences of frowsy servants kept us
going till an adjacent grocery lady started "clair-
voyant seances in opposition, training her little
girl (in a pinafore hieroglyphic’d in treacle) to see
spooks for threepence extra. A little money came
in when this ventute had failed and Ernestine
had started a registry-office for servants—the
office was a Chinaman’s disused store in the next
street. But there was a cabal against her in
Santley’s Lane, and she was publicly accused of
taking the bread out of the mouths of various
widows whose garments looked as if they wanted
burial.
From my window I could see and hear the con-
clave of indignant ladies set upon Ernestine as
she came home from the registry after a day of
failure. It seemed all of them had husbands who
never allowed them to soil their hands when they
—the husbands—were alive. This class of hus-
band seems generally dead. It appeared, also, on
the authority of the clairvoyant widow who wore a
violent feather over her nose, that the Government
was going to put down registry offices that weren’t
kept by widows with orphans to feed. The
dancing-washing lady, who had black, hard-
looking ankles beneath a red cotton underskirt,
thanked God that work was going to be given to
the widows and not such as had lazy, loafing
husbands to do for them if they’d a mind to.
“Is the world only for widows?” I heard
Ernestine say these words indignantly, bitterly,
before her slight form disappeared into her own
doorway.
It came suddenly, that temptation. I should
have expected it to dawn gradually upon me; but
it came like a flash. Only it was a flash that
stayed. It was as if the idea had been growing in
the dark places of my mind, unknown even to
myself, and now burst, fully grown, on my mental
sight. Ernestine’s innocent words had uncon-
sciously revealed my path to me. Poor Ernestine
—she must never know it.
More and more clearly throughout the evening
I saw that my duty led to death. I had leisure
enough for thought. Ernestine, curiously enough,
was silent, too.
Baby lying against her shoulder, his face
cuddled over on his hands, woke every now and
then to convulsive coughing. “ Give him some-
thing,” I whispered. My own pain was great.
“ It’s no use. He needs different air, better
food.”
A remembrance of the healthful, country town-
ship where I had met her as school-teacher
flashed over me. They might take her back—a
widow. Ernestine, clasping her baby close, gazed
at the small, new-lit fire. There seemed a strange
aloofness, even coldness, in her manner. I saw it,
but I knew that it was not in her heart at all. It
was my own dark determination that made every
look and word seem constrained —that kept her
away from me, as soon she would be for ever.
During three days I thought it over. I had the
laudanum under my pillow, and, though she
might suspect, she could never know for certain
that the overdose was intentional. I was not
kind to her these three days. I anticipated the
time when this life of illness and poverty com-
bined would have sapped my man’s courage and
made callous and dull my purest affections. She
must realise when I was gone that my very soul
had received an all-but mortal wound, and now
needed to be invalided Home.
So the night came when she gave me my glass
of medicine. How her hand trembled as I took
it. Unknown to her, as she stooped to smooth
my pillow, I kissed the dark locks that shaded
the pallid, tender face. Then I drank, and as she
turned away I put my hand under the pillow
where my own bottle of laudanum was hidden.
As I touohed it, I saw Ernestine standing by the
fire, hugging her baby to her, while the fire flames
beat fiercely in her eyes. How dared they, I
thought dully—those tender eyes!
* * * •
To the confines of the Unknown, I travelled
rapidly ; then back, back ! I think they guessed,
both doctor and wife, that the overdose was
intentional. But Ernestine did not reproach me.
Well, the worst is over now. Help came;
friends came; the registry has begun to pay, and
we have been able to send baby up the country for
change of air.
Ernestine was not half so cut-up at parting with
him as. I expected. “ He’s as well there as here,”
she said shortly, when I condoled with her, and
crept closer to me. Yet once I had fancied her
more the mother than the wife.
There is only one thing that troubles me. I
mentioned it to Ernestine, the other day. “You
don’t notice my memory failing, dear ? Because,
it is strange, I can never remember taking that
dose of laudanum. I recollect reaching for it—
but no more. Except for the doctor’s word, I
would not have believed I took it.”
But Ernestine soothed me. “It is the only
thing you have ever forgotten. You were not
yourself that night—nor I! ” she added, in a lower
and more tremulous voice.
C.

Burnand’s autobiography is impudent and amusing-.
In one of his miserable attempts as a barrister, in the
London Criminal Court, his lady client snatched off her
shoe, and flung it at him, in the spirit with which stage
aspirants are treated to vegetables. Almost his first
dramatic piece was the extravaganza of “ Dido,” for Chas-
oung, comedian of the London St. James’s Theatre.
Burnand says that manager E. T. Smith, father of L. L.
Smith, had been a policeman, and that Wizard Anderson
began as a street conjurer. They had a row once. Said
Smith: ‘ What are you? A street mountebank.” And
Anderson: “What the devil are you? A policeman.”
Whereupon Smith put in : “By thunder, I wish I had
caught you with your fakements, on your bit of carpet 1”

“ T.D. ” : The exorbitant prima donna and her accom-
plice, the high-priced tenor, seem to have reached the
end of their tether. They have gradually brought up
their fees to such a pitch that it is absolutely impossible
for managers to fix up a well-balanced opera caste—public
taste nowadays having been educated beyond a demand
for one great singer supported by a lot of absolute
rotters. Tlse Sunday American recently published the
following list of “ some of the high>priced stars not
working this year”—Emma Eames, Nellie Melba, Lillian
Nordica, Madame Sehumann-lleink, M. Van Dyck, M.
Alvarez, Jean de Reszke, Suzanne Adams, and Madame
Scheff. The same paper gave a list of “ less expensh e
but satisfactory new stars.” This list, by the way, is
headed by Madame Aino Ackte, who was a fellow-pupil
with Amy Castles under Bouhy, the Parisian teacher,
and about whom Amy used to rave as the possessor of
the “ finest voice in the world.”
“Vulcan”: In selecting his new comedy co., which is
to play “Marriage of Kitty,” “Cousin Kate,” etc.,
Manager Williamson filled every position from “ outside,”
except that of leading lady. After seeing Marie Tempest
in London, and most of her substitutes in the provinces,
he decided that a better lady for the part couldn’t be
found than the local Rose Musgrove.
Lydia Thompson, the ancient elder sister of Mrs.
Bracy, is going to make tier re-appearance on the London
stage. It is exactly 30 years since Lydia made a re-
appearance in London after a long absence in America,
and people at that time remarked how different she was
from the young original Lydia they had known aforetime.
“J. A.C.”: In Bulletin (25/2/’O4) is published a portrait
of Clarence Weber, “ the young Victorian strong man,
who forms a bridge across two chairs with an anvil rest-
ing on his chest, whilst a blacksmith breaks a piece of
cold iron on the anvil.” Apart from ,the supporting of
the anvil, there is nothing remarkable in this. The force
of the blows is distributed throughout the anvil, and
never reaches the person bearing it. Should anyone have
a doubt on the subject, let him hold (say) a 561 b. weight
in his hands while another man strikes it violently with
a hammer. But for the senses of sight and hearing the
former would be unaware that anything was happening.
It is an old “fake.” When I was a boy in Glasgow there
was performing at the music halls a little man named
Samson Boleno, not much bigger than a good-sized
Shanghai rooster, who used to form a bridge, resting his
hands and feet on the floor, while a block of building
■tone, weighing two or three cwt., was placed on his
chest, and someone from the audience broke it in two
with a sledge-hammer.
Eskell, the sleight-of-finger person at Melbourne Opera
House, who does a nice little turn in a pleasing manner,
has one peculiarity—a word-formula which runs, “ Back
again once more.” “ Here we have the egg—back again
once more,” or “ We find it in the hat—back again once
more." The fourteenth repetition makes the public
tired.
“ Holdar M." : T’other day was nearly knocked over by
an infuriated mob crowded out of a city church where a
theatrical wedding was on. It was a wealthy, fashion-
able mob, too. As a live “ draw ” I beg to suggest the
stage as the scene of a real “pro.” wedding if it
could happen as a curtain-raiser. The banns" of Mr.
Leading Actor and Miss Principal Charmer could be
called in the usual ad. column of the daily press. The
cast of best man, bridesmaids and other auxiliaries by
“ our talented co.” A parson of theatrical leanings would
appear for one night only. “ Box plan and reserved seats
at enhanced prices for this special performance,” &c.
Before a large and animated audience the limelight would
be turned on to “ the numerous and costly presents ” (to
be returned to the warehouse next day). As the orchestra
asserted “ The Voice that Breathed ” the tremulous bride
would step from the wings supported on the arm of the
man who gave her away in one sense and not in another.
Real orange blossoms and other trimmings complete ;
confetti and pea-nuts to be showered o’er the happy
scene by the huge and delighted audience as the rag
temporarily descends. The honeymoon starts in the first
act, and continues, with intermissions, during the even-
ing. Next day’s press would describe at length the loud
and gorgeous ceremony, and business would boom. I
wonder some astute manager hasn’t dreamt of this before.
Douglas Sladen tells us in his new book
on Japan of. how once a year paupers who have
not enough to eat will walk a couple of hundred
miles to see the irises of Harokini in bloom. It is
not a religious, simply an aesthetic, pilgrimage. It
is their equivalent to a test match. But that’s
part of the life of “old Japan ’’—the Japan which
has been smothered of late by European civilisa-
tion and industrialism and militarism. The
modem Jap considers himself a gentleman if he
oan own a battered belltopper hat; there is a
huge gulf between that ambition and the pil-
grimage to the irises.
